1         Romeo and Juliet
2         Shakespeare homepage | Romeo and Juliet | Entire play

3         ACT I

4         PROLOGUE

5         Two households, both alike in dignity,
6         In fair Verona, where we lay our scene,
7         From ancient grudge break to new mutiny,
8         Where civil blood makes civil hands unclean.
9         From forth the fatal loins of these two foes
10        A pair of star-cross'd lovers take their life;
11        Whose misadventured piteous overthrows
12        Do with their death bury their parents' strife.
13        The fearful passage of their death-mark'd love,
14        And the continuance of their parents' rage,Chorus. Two households, both alike in dignity,
15        In fair Verona, where we lay our scene,
16        From ancient grudge break to new mutiny,
17        Where civil blood makes civil hands unclean.
18        From forth the fatal loins of these two foes5
19        A pair of star-cross'd lovers take their life;
20        Whose misadventured piteous overthrows
21        Do with their death bury their parents' strife.
22        The fearful passage of their death-mark'd love,
23        And the continuance of their parents' rage,10
24        Which, but their children's end, nought could remove,
25        Is now the two hours' traffic of our stage;
26        The which if you with patient ears attend,
27        What here shall miss, our toil shall strive to mend.
28         	     	
29        Act I, Scene 1

30        Verona. A public place.

31             	 
32        [Enter SAMPSON and GREGORY, of the house of Capulet, armed with swords and bucklers]

33        Sampson. Gregory, o' my word, we'll not carry coals.
34        Gregory. No, for then we should be colliers.
35        Sampson. I mean, an we be in choler, we'll draw.
36        Gregory. Ay, while you live, draw your neck out o' the collar.20
37        Sampson. I strike quickly, being moved.
38        Gregory. But thou art not quickly moved to strike.
39        Sampson. A dog of the house of Montague moves me.
40        Gregory. To move is to stir; and to be valiant is to stand:
41        therefore, if thou art moved, thou runn'st away.25
42        Sampson. A dog of that house shall move me to stand: I will
43        take the wall of any man or maid of Montague's.
44        Gregory. That shows thee a weak slave; for the weakest goes
45        to the wall.
46        Sampson. True; and therefore women, being the weaker vessels,30
47        are ever thrust to the wall: therefore I will push
48        Montague's men from the wall, and thrust his maids
49        to the wall.
50        Gregory. The quarrel is between our masters and us their men.
51        Sampson. 'Tis all one, I will show myself a tyrant: when I35
52        have fought with the men, I will be cruel with the
53        maids, and cut off their heads.
54        Gregory. The heads of the maids?
55        Sampson. Ay, the heads of the maids, or their maidenheads;
56        take it in what sense thou wilt.40
57        Gregory. They must take it in sense that feel it.
58        Sampson. Me they shall feel while I am able to stand: and
59        'tis known I am a pretty piece of flesh.
60        Gregory. 'Tis well thou art not fish; if thou hadst, thou
61        hadst been poor John. Draw thy tool! here comes45
62        two of the house of the Montagues.
63        Sampson. My naked weapon is out: quarrel, I will back thee.
64        Gregory. How! turn thy back and run?
65        Sampson. Fear me not.
66        Gregory. No, marry; I fear thee!50
67        Sampson. Let us take the law of our sides; let them begin.
68        Gregory. I will frown as I pass by, and let them take it as
69        they list.
70        Sampson. Nay, as they dare. I will bite my thumb at them;
71        which is a disgrace to them, if they bear it.55
72        [Enter ABRAHAM and BALTHASAR]

73        Abraham. Do you bite your thumb at us, sir?
74        Sampson. I do bite my thumb, sir.
75        Abraham. Do you bite your thumb at us, sir?
76        Sampson. [Aside to GREGORY] Is the law of our side, if I say60
77        ay?
78        Gregory. No.
79        Sampson. No, sir, I do not bite my thumb at you, sir, but I
80        bite my thumb, sir.
81        Gregory. Do you quarrel, sir?65
82        Abraham. Quarrel sir! no, sir.
83        Sampson. If you do, sir, I am for you: I serve as good a man as you.
84        Abraham. No better.
85        Sampson. Well, sir.
86        Gregory. Say 'better:' here comes one of my master's kinsmen.70
87        Sampson. Yes, better, sir.
88        Abraham. You lie.
89        Sampson. Draw, if you be men. Gregory, remember thy swashing blow.
90        [They fight]

91        [Enter BENVOLIO]

92        Benvolio. Part, fools!
93        Put up your swords; you know not what you do.
94        [Beats down their swords]

95        [Enter TYBALT]

96        Tybalt. What, art thou drawn among these heartless hinds?80
97        Turn thee, Benvolio, look upon thy death.
98        Benvolio. I do but keep the peace: put up thy sword,
99        Or manage it to part these men with me.
100       Tybalt. What, drawn, and talk of peace! I hate the word,
101       As I hate hell, all Montagues, and thee:85
102       Have at thee, coward!
103       [They fight]
104       [Enter, several of both houses, who join the fray;
105       then enter Citizens, with clubs]
106       First Citizen. Clubs, bills, and partisans! strike! beat them down!90
107       Down with the Capulets! down with the Montagues!
108       [Enter CAPULET in his gown, and LADY CAPULET]

109       Capulet. What noise is this? Give me my long sword, ho!
110       Lady Capulet. A crutch, a crutch! why call you for a sword?
111       Capulet. My sword, I say! Old Montague is come,95
112       And flourishes his blade in spite of me.
113       [Enter MONTAGUE and LADY MONTAGUE]

114       Montague. Thou villain Capulet,—Hold me not, let me go.
115       Lady Montague. Thou shalt not stir a foot to seek a foe.
116       [Enter PRINCE, with Attendants]

117       Prince Escalus. Rebellious subjects, enemies to peace,
118       Profaners of this neighbour-stained steel,—
119       Will they not hear? What, ho! you men, you beasts,
120       That quench the fire of your pernicious rage
121       With purple fountains issuing from your veins,105
122       On pain of torture, from those bloody hands
123       Throw your mistemper'd weapons to the ground,
124       And hear the sentence of your moved prince.
125       Three civil brawls, bred of an airy word,
126       By thee, old Capulet, and Montague,110
127       Have thrice disturb'd the quiet of our streets,
128       And made Verona's ancient citizens
129       Cast by their grave beseeming ornaments,
130       To wield old partisans, in hands as old,
131       Canker'd with peace, to part your canker'd hate:115
132       If ever you disturb our streets again,
133       Your lives shall pay the forfeit of the peace.
134       For this time, all the rest depart away:
135       You Capulet; shall go along with me:
136       And, Montague, come you this afternoon,120
137       To know our further pleasure in this case,
138       To old Free-town, our common judgment-place.
139       Once more, on pain of death, all men depart.
140       [Exeunt all but MONTAGUE, LADY MONTAGUE, and BENVOLIO]

141       Montague. Who set this ancient quarrel new abroach?125
142       Speak, nephew, were you by when it began?
143       Benvolio. Here were the servants of your adversary,
144       And yours, close fighting ere I did approach:
145       I drew to part them: in the instant came
146       The fiery Tybalt, with his sword prepared,130
147       Which, as he breathed defiance to my ears,
148       He swung about his head and cut the winds,
149       Who nothing hurt withal hiss'd him in scorn:
150       While we were interchanging thrusts and blows,
151       Came more and more and fought on part and part,135
152       Till the prince came, who parted either part.
153       Lady Montague. O, where is Romeo? saw you him to-day?
154       Right glad I am he was not at this fray.
155       Benvolio. Madam, an hour before the worshipp'd sun
156       Peer'd forth the golden window of the east,140
157       A troubled mind drave me to walk abroad;
158       Where, underneath the grove of sycamore
159       That westward rooteth from the city's side,
160       So early walking did I see your son:
161       Towards him I made, but he was ware of me145
162       And stole into the covert of the wood:
163       I, measuring his affections by my own,
164       That most are busied when they're most alone,
165       Pursued my humour not pursuing his,
166       And gladly shunn'd who gladly fled from me.150
167       Montague. Many a morning hath he there been seen,
168       With tears augmenting the fresh morning dew.
169       Adding to clouds more clouds with his deep sighs;
170       But all so soon as the all-cheering sun
171       Should in the furthest east begin to draw155
172       The shady curtains from Aurora's bed,
173       Away from the light steals home my heavy son,
174       And private in his chamber pens himself,
175       Shuts up his windows, locks far daylight out
176       And makes himself an artificial night:160
177       Black and portentous must this humour prove,
178       Unless good counsel may the cause remove.
179       Benvolio. My noble uncle, do you know the cause?
180       Montague. I neither know it nor can learn of him.
181       Benvolio. Have you importuned him by any means?165
182       Montague. Both by myself and many other friends:
183       But he, his own affections' counsellor,
184       Is to himself—I will not say how true—
185       But to himself so secret and so close,
186       So far from sounding and discovery,170
187       As is the bud bit with an envious worm,
188       Ere he can spread his sweet leaves to the air,
189       Or dedicate his beauty to the sun.
190       Could we but learn from whence his sorrows grow.
191       We would as willingly give cure as know.175
192       [Enter ROMEO]

193       Benvolio. See, where he comes: so please you, step aside;
194       I'll know his grievance, or be much denied.
195       Montague. I would thou wert so happy by thy stay,
196       To hear true shrift. Come, madam, let's away.180
197       [Exeunt MONTAGUE and LADY MONTAGUE]

198       Benvolio. Good-morrow, cousin.
199       Romeo. Is the day so young?
200       Benvolio. But new struck nine.
201       Romeo. Ay me! sad hours seem long.185
202       Was that my father that went hence so fast?
203       Benvolio. It was. What sadness lengthens Romeo's hours?
204       Romeo. Not having that, which, having, makes them short.
205       Benvolio. In love?
206       Romeo. Out—190
207       Benvolio. Of love?
208       Romeo. Out of her favour, where I am in love.
209       Benvolio. Alas, that love, so gentle in his view,
210       Should be so tyrannous and rough in proof!
211       Romeo. Alas, that love, whose view is muffled still,195
212       Should, without eyes, see pathways to his will!
213       Where shall we dine? O me! What fray was here?
214       Yet tell me not, for I have heard it all.
215       Here's much to do with hate, but more with love.
216       Why, then, O brawling love! O loving hate!200
217       O any thing, of nothing first create!
218       O heavy lightness! serious vanity!
219       Mis-shapen chaos of well-seeming forms!
220       Feather of lead, bright smoke, cold fire,
221       sick health!205
222       Still-waking sleep, that is not what it is!
223       This love feel I, that feel no love in this.
224       Dost thou not laugh?
225       Benvolio. No, coz, I rather weep.
226       Romeo. Good heart, at what?210
227       Benvolio. At thy good heart's oppression.
228       Romeo. Why, such is love's transgression.
229       Griefs of mine own lie heavy in my breast,
230       Which thou wilt propagate, to have it prest
231       With more of thine: this love that thou hast shown215
232       Doth add more grief to too much of mine own.
233       Love is a smoke raised with the fume of sighs;
234       Being purged, a fire sparkling in lovers' eyes;
235       Being vex'd a sea nourish'd with lovers' tears:
236       What is it else? a madness most discreet,220
237       A choking gall and a preserving sweet.
238       Farewell, my coz.
239       Benvolio. Soft! I will go along;
240       An if you leave me so, you do me wrong.
241       Romeo. Tut, I have lost myself; I am not here;225
242       This is not Romeo, he's some other where.
243       Benvolio. Tell me in sadness, who is that you love.
244       Romeo. What, shall I groan and tell thee?
245       Benvolio. Groan! why, no.
246       But sadly tell me who.230
247       Romeo. Bid a sick man in sadness make his will:
248       Ah, word ill urged to one that is so ill!
249       In sadness, cousin, I do love a woman.
250       Benvolio. I aim'd so near, when I supposed you loved.
251       Romeo. A right good mark-man! And she's fair I love.235
252       Benvolio. A right fair mark, fair coz, is soonest hit.
253       Romeo. Well, in that hit you miss: she'll not be hit
254       With Cupid's arrow; she hath Dian's wit;
255       And, in strong proof of chastity well arm'd,
256       From love's weak childish bow she lives unharm'd.240
257       She will not stay the siege of loving terms,
258       Nor bide the encounter of assailing eyes,
259       Nor ope her lap to saint-seducing gold:
260       O, she is rich in beauty, only poor,
261       That when she dies with beauty dies her store.245
262       Benvolio. Then she hath sworn that she will still live chaste?
263       Romeo. She hath, and in that sparing makes huge waste,
264       For beauty starved with her severity
265       Cuts beauty off from all posterity.
266       She is too fair, too wise, wisely too fair,250
267       To merit bliss by making me despair:
268       She hath forsworn to love, and in that vow
269       Do I live dead that live to tell it now.
270       Benvolio. Be ruled by me, forget to think of her.
271       Romeo. O, teach me how I should forget to think.255
272       Benvolio. By giving liberty unto thine eyes;
273       Examine other beauties.
274       Romeo. 'Tis the way
275       To call hers exquisite, in question more:
276       These happy masks that kiss fair ladies' brows260
277       Being black put us in mind they hide the fair;
278       He that is strucken blind cannot forget
279       The precious treasure of his eyesight lost:
280       Show me a mistress that is passing fair,
281       What doth her beauty serve, but as a note265
282       Where I may read who pass'd that passing fair?
283       Farewell: thou canst not teach me to forget.
284       Benvolio. I'll pay that doctrine, or else die in debt.
285       [Exeunt]

286        	     	
287       Act I, Scene 2

288       A street.

289            	 
290       [Enter CAPULET, PARIS, and Servant]

291       Capulet. But Montague is bound as well as I,
292       In penalty alike; and 'tis not hard, I think,
293       For men so old as we to keep the peace.
294       Paris. Of honourable reckoning are you both;
295       And pity 'tis you lived at odds so long.275
296       But now, my lord, what say you to my suit?
297       Capulet. But saying o'er what I have said before:
298       My child is yet a stranger in the world;
299       She hath not seen the change of fourteen years,
300       Let two more summers wither in their pride,280
301       Ere we may think her ripe to be a bride.
302       Paris. Younger than she are happy mothers made.
303       Capulet. And too soon marr'd are those so early made.
304       The earth hath swallow'd all my hopes but she,
305       She is the hopeful lady of my earth:285
306       But woo her, gentle Paris, get her heart,
307       My will to her consent is but a part;
308       An she agree, within her scope of choice
309       Lies my consent and fair according voice.
310       This night I hold an old accustom'd feast,290
311       Whereto I have invited many a guest,
312       Such as I love; and you, among the store,
313       One more, most welcome, makes my number more.
314       At my poor house look to behold this night
315       Earth-treading stars that make dark heaven light:295
316       Such comfort as do lusty young men feel
317       When well-apparell'd April on the heel
318       Of limping winter treads, even such delight
319       Among fresh female buds shall you this night
320       Inherit at my house; hear all, all see,300
321       And like her most whose merit most shall be:
322       Which on more view, of many mine being one
323       May stand in number, though in reckoning none,
324       Come, go with me.
325       [To Servant, giving a paper]305
326       Go, sirrah, trudge about
327       Through fair Verona; find those persons out
328       Whose names are written there, and to them say,
329       My house and welcome on their pleasure stay.
330       [Exeunt CAPULET and PARIS]

331       Servant. Find them out whose names are written here! It is
332       written, that the shoemaker should meddle with his
333       yard, and the tailor with his last, the fisher with
334       his pencil, and the painter with his nets; but I am
335       sent to find those persons whose names are here315
336       writ, and can never find what names the writing
337       person hath here writ. I must to the learned.—In good time.
338       [Enter BENVOLIO and ROMEO]

339       Benvolio. Tut, man, one fire burns out another's burning,
340       One pain is lessen'd by another's anguish;320
341       Turn giddy, and be holp by backward turning;
342       One desperate grief cures with another's languish:
343       Take thou some new infection to thy eye,
344       And the rank poison of the old will die.
345       Romeo. Your plaintain-leaf is excellent for that.325
346       Benvolio. For what, I pray thee?
347       Romeo. For your broken shin.
348       Benvolio. Why, Romeo, art thou mad?
349       Romeo. Not mad, but bound more than a mad-man is;
350       Shut up in prison, kept without my food,330
351       Whipp'd and tormented and—God-den, good fellow.
352       Servant. God gi' god-den. I pray, sir, can you read?
353       Romeo. Ay, mine own fortune in my misery.
354       Servant. Perhaps you have learned it without book: but, I
355       pray, can you read any thing you see?335
356       Romeo. Ay, if I know the letters and the language.
357       Servant. Ye say honestly: rest you merry!
358       Romeo. Stay, fellow; I can read.
359       [Reads]
360       'Signior Martino and his wife and daughters;340
361       County Anselme and his beauteous sisters; the lady
362       widow of Vitravio; Signior Placentio and his lovely
363       nieces; Mercutio and his brother Valentine; mine
364       uncle Capulet, his wife and daughters; my fair niece
365       Rosaline; Livia; Signior Valentio and his cousin345
366       Tybalt, Lucio and the lively Helena.' A fair
367       assembly: whither should they come?
368       Servant. Up.
369       Romeo. Whither?
370       Servant. To supper; to our house.350
371       Romeo. Whose house?
372       Servant. My master's.
373       Romeo. Indeed, I should have ask'd you that before.
374       Servant. Now I'll tell you without asking: my master is the
375       great rich Capulet; and if you be not of the house355
376       of Montagues, I pray, come and crush a cup of wine.
377       Rest you merry!
378       [Exit]

379       Benvolio. At this same ancient feast of Capulet's
380       Sups the fair Rosaline whom thou so lovest,360
381       With all the admired beauties of Verona:
382       Go thither; and, with unattainted eye,
383       Compare her face with some that I shall show,
384       And I will make thee think thy swan a crow.
385       Romeo. When the devout religion of mine eye365
386       Maintains such falsehood, then turn tears to fires;
387       And these, who often drown'd could never die,
388       Transparent heretics, be burnt for liars!
389       One fairer than my love! the all-seeing sun
390       Ne'er saw her match since first the world begun.370
391       Benvolio. Tut, you saw her fair, none else being by,
392       Herself poised with herself in either eye:
393       But in that crystal scales let there be weigh'd
394       Your lady's love against some other maid
395       That I will show you shining at this feast,375
396       And she shall scant show well that now shows best.
397       Romeo. I'll go along, no such sight to be shown,
398       But to rejoice in splendor of mine own.
399       [Exeunt]

400        	     	
401       Act I, Scene 3

402       A room in Capulet’s house.

403            	 
404       [Enter LADY CAPULET and Nurse]

405       Lady Capulet. Nurse, where's my daughter? call her forth to me.
406       Nurse. Now, by my maidenhead, at twelve year old,
407       I bade her come. What, lamb! what, ladybird!
408       God forbid! Where's this girl? What, Juliet!
409       [Enter JULIET]

410       Juliet. How now! who calls?
411       Nurse. Your mother.
412       Juliet. Madam, I am here.
413       What is your will?
414       Lady Capulet. This is the matter:—Nurse, give leave awhile,390
415       We must talk in secret:—nurse, come back again;
416       I have remember'd me, thou's hear our counsel.
417       Thou know'st my daughter's of a pretty age.
418       Nurse. Faith, I can tell her age unto an hour.
419       Lady Capulet. She's not fourteen.395
420       Nurse. I'll lay fourteen of my teeth,—
421       And yet, to my teeth be it spoken, I have but four—
422       She is not fourteen. How long is it now
423       To Lammas-tide?
424       Lady Capulet. A fortnight and odd days.400
425       Nurse. Even or odd, of all days in the year,
426       Come Lammas-eve at night shall she be fourteen.
427       Susan and she—God rest all Christian souls!—
428       Were of an age: well, Susan is with God;
429       She was too good for me: but, as I said,405
430       On Lammas-eve at night shall she be fourteen;
431       That shall she, marry; I remember it well.
432       'Tis since the earthquake now eleven years;
433       And she was wean'd,—I never shall forget it,—
434       Of all the days of the year, upon that day:410
435       For I had then laid wormwood to my dug,
436       Sitting in the sun under the dove-house wall;
437       My lord and you were then at Mantua:—
438       Nay, I do bear a brain:—but, as I said,
439       When it did taste the wormwood on the nipple415
440       Of my dug and felt it bitter, pretty fool,
441       To see it tetchy and fall out with the dug!
442       Shake quoth the dove-house: 'twas no need, I trow,
443       To bid me trudge:
444       And since that time it is eleven years;420
445       For then she could stand alone; nay, by the rood,
446       She could have run and waddled all about;
447       For even the day before, she broke her brow:
448       And then my husband—God be with his soul!
449       A' was a merry man—took up the child:425
450       'Yea,' quoth he, 'dost thou fall upon thy face?
451       Thou wilt fall backward when thou hast more wit;
452       Wilt thou not, Jule?' and, by my holidame,
453       The pretty wretch left crying and said 'Ay.'
454       To see, now, how a jest shall come about!430
455       I warrant, an I should live a thousand years,
456       I never should forget it: 'Wilt thou not, Jule?' quoth he;
457       And, pretty fool, it stinted and said 'Ay.'
458       Lady Capulet. Enough of this; I pray thee, hold thy peace.
459       Nurse. Yes, madam: yet I cannot choose but laugh,435
460       To think it should leave crying and say 'Ay.'
461       And yet, I warrant, it had upon its brow
462       A bump as big as a young cockerel's stone;
463       A parlous knock; and it cried bitterly:
464       'Yea,' quoth my husband,'fall'st upon thy face?440
465       Thou wilt fall backward when thou comest to age;
466       Wilt thou not, Jule?' it stinted and said 'Ay.'
467       Juliet. And stint thou too, I pray thee, nurse, say I.
468       Nurse. Peace, I have done. God mark thee to his grace!
469       Thou wast the prettiest babe that e'er I nursed:445
470       An I might live to see thee married once,
471       I have my wish.
472       Lady Capulet. Marry, that 'marry' is the very theme
473       I came to talk of. Tell me, daughter Juliet,
474       How stands your disposition to be married?450
475       Juliet. It is an honour that I dream not of.
476       Nurse. An honour! were not I thine only nurse,
477       I would say thou hadst suck'd wisdom from thy teat.
478       Lady Capulet. Well, think of marriage now; younger than you,
479       Here in Verona, ladies of esteem,455
480       Are made already mothers: by my count,
481       I was your mother much upon these years
482       That you are now a maid. Thus then in brief:
483       The valiant Paris seeks you for his love.
484       Nurse. A man, young lady! lady, such a man460
485       As all the world—why, he's a man of wax.
486       Lady Capulet. Verona's summer hath not such a flower.
487       Nurse. Nay, he's a flower; in faith, a very flower.
488       Lady Capulet. What say you? can you love the gentleman?
489       This night you shall behold him at our feast;465
490       Read o'er the volume of young Paris' face,
491       And find delight writ there with beauty's pen;
492       Examine every married lineament,
493       And see how one another lends content
494       And what obscured in this fair volume lies470
495       Find written in the margent of his eyes.
496       This precious book of love, this unbound lover,
497       To beautify him, only lacks a cover:
498       The fish lives in the sea, and 'tis much pride
499       For fair without the fair within to hide:475
500       That book in many's eyes doth share the glory,
501       That in gold clasps locks in the golden story;
502       So shall you share all that he doth possess,
503       By having him, making yourself no less.
504       Nurse. No less! nay, bigger; women grow by men.480
505       Lady Capulet. Speak briefly, can you like of Paris' love?
506       Juliet. I'll look to like, if looking liking move:
507       But no more deep will I endart mine eye
508       Than your consent gives strength to make it fly.
509       [Enter a Servant]

510       Servant. Madam, the guests are come, supper served up, you
511       called, my young lady asked for, the nurse cursed in
512       the pantry, and every thing in extremity. I must
513       hence to wait; I beseech you, follow straight.
514       Lady Capulet. We follow thee.490
515       [Exit Servant]
516       Juliet, the county stays.
517       Nurse. Go, girl, seek happy nights to happy days.
518       [Exeunt]

519        	     	
520       Act I, Scene 4

521       A street.

522            	 
523       [Enter ROMEO, MERCUTIO, BENVOLIO, with five or six [p]Maskers, Torch-bearers, and others]

524       Romeo. What, shall this speech be spoke for our excuse?
525       Or shall we on without a apology?
526       Benvolio. The date is out of such prolixity:
527       We'll have no Cupid hoodwink'd with a scarf,500
528       Bearing a Tartar's painted bow of lath,
529       Scaring the ladies like a crow-keeper;
530       Nor no without-book prologue, faintly spoke
531       After the prompter, for our entrance:
532       But let them measure us by what they will;505
533       We'll measure them a measure, and be gone.
534       Romeo. Give me a torch: I am not for this ambling;
535       Being but heavy, I will bear the light.
536       Mercutio. Nay, gentle Romeo, we must have you dance.
537       Romeo. Not I, believe me: you have dancing shoes510
538       With nimble soles: I have a soul of lead
539       So stakes me to the ground I cannot move.
540       Mercutio. You are a lover; borrow Cupid's wings,
541       And soar with them above a common bound.
542       Romeo. I am too sore enpierced with his shaft515
543       To soar with his light feathers, and so bound,
544       I cannot bound a pitch above dull woe:
545       Under love's heavy burden do I sink.
546       Mercutio. And, to sink in it, should you burden love;
547       Too great oppression for a tender thing.520
548       Romeo. Is love a tender thing? it is too rough,
549       Too rude, too boisterous, and it pricks like thorn.
550       Mercutio. If love be rough with you, be rough with love;
551       Prick love for pricking, and you beat love down.
552       Give me a case to put my visage in:525
553       A visor for a visor! what care I
554       What curious eye doth quote deformities?
555       Here are the beetle brows shall blush for me.
556       Benvolio. Come, knock and enter; and no sooner in,
557       But every man betake him to his legs.530
558       Romeo. A torch for me: let wantons light of heart
559       Tickle the senseless rushes with their heels,
560       For I am proverb'd with a grandsire phrase;
561       I'll be a candle-holder, and look on.
562       The game was ne'er so fair, and I am done.535
563       Mercutio. Tut, dun's the mouse, the constable's own word:
564       If thou art dun, we'll draw thee from the mire
565       Of this sir-reverence love, wherein thou stick'st
566       Up to the ears. Come, we burn daylight, ho!
567       Romeo. Nay, that's not so.540
568       Mercutio. I mean, sir, in delay
569       We waste our lights in vain, like lamps by day.
570       Take our good meaning, for our judgment sits
571       Five times in that ere once in our five wits.
572       Romeo. And we mean well in going to this mask;545
573       But 'tis no wit to go.
574       Mercutio. Why, may one ask?
575       Romeo. I dream'd a dream to-night.
576       Mercutio. And so did I.
577       Romeo. Well, what was yours?550
578       Mercutio. That dreamers often lie.
579       Romeo. In bed asleep, while they do dream things true.
580       Mercutio. O, then, I see Queen Mab hath been with you.
581       She is the fairies' midwife, and she comes
582       In shape no bigger than an agate-stone555
583       On the fore-finger of an alderman,
584       Drawn with a team of little atomies
585       Athwart men's noses as they lie asleep;
586       Her wagon-spokes made of long spiders' legs,
587       The cover of the wings of grasshoppers,560
588       The traces of the smallest spider's web,
589       The collars of the moonshine's watery beams,
590       Her whip of cricket's bone, the lash of film,
591       Her wagoner a small grey-coated gnat,
592       Not so big as a round little worm565
593       Prick'd from the lazy finger of a maid;
594       Her chariot is an empty hazel-nut
595       Made by the joiner squirrel or old grub,
596       Time out o' mind the fairies' coachmakers.
597       And in this state she gallops night by night570
598       Through lovers' brains, and then they dream of love;
599       O'er courtiers' knees, that dream on court'sies straight,
600       O'er lawyers' fingers, who straight dream on fees,
601       O'er ladies ' lips, who straight on kisses dream,
602       Which oft the angry Mab with blisters plagues,575
603       Because their breaths with sweetmeats tainted are:
604       Sometime she gallops o'er a courtier's nose,
605       And then dreams he of smelling out a suit;
606       And sometime comes she with a tithe-pig's tail
607       Tickling a parson's nose as a' lies asleep,580
608       Then dreams, he of another benefice:
609       Sometime she driveth o'er a soldier's neck,
610       And then dreams he of cutting foreign throats,
611       Of breaches, ambuscadoes, Spanish blades,
612       Of healths five-fathom deep; and then anon585
613       Drums in his ear, at which he starts and wakes,
614       And being thus frighted swears a prayer or two
615       And sleeps again. This is that very Mab
616       That plats the manes of horses in the night,
617       And bakes the elflocks in foul sluttish hairs,590
618       Which once untangled, much misfortune bodes:
619       This is the hag, when maids lie on their backs,
620       That presses them and learns them first to bear,
621       Making them women of good carriage:
622       This is she—595
623       Romeo. Peace, peace, Mercutio, peace!
624       Thou talk'st of nothing.
625       Mercutio. True, I talk of dreams,
626       Which are the children of an idle brain,
627       Begot of nothing but vain fantasy,600
628       Which is as thin of substance as the air
629       And more inconstant than the wind, who wooes
630       Even now the frozen bosom of the north,
631       And, being anger'd, puffs away from thence,
632       Turning his face to the dew-dropping south.605
633       Benvolio. This wind, you talk of, blows us from ourselves;
634       Supper is done, and we shall come too late.
635       Romeo. I fear, too early: for my mind misgives
636       Some consequence yet hanging in the stars
637       Shall bitterly begin his fearful date610
638       With this night's revels and expire the term
639       Of a despised life closed in my breast
640       By some vile forfeit of untimely death.
641       But He, that hath the steerage of my course,
642       Direct my sail! On, lusty gentlemen.615
643       Benvolio. Strike, drum.
644       [Exeunt]

645        	     	
646       Act I, Scene 5

647       A hall in Capulet’s house.

648            	 
649       [Musicians waiting. Enter Servingmen with napkins]

650       First Servant. Where's Potpan, that he helps not to take away? He
651       shift a trencher? he scrape a trencher!620
652       Second Servant. When good manners shall lie all in one or two men's
653       hands and they unwashed too, 'tis a foul thing.
654       First Servant. Away with the joint-stools, remove the
655       court-cupboard, look to the plate. Good thou, save
656       me a piece of marchpane; and, as thou lovest me, let625
657       the porter let in Susan Grindstone and Nell.
658       Antony, and Potpan!
659       Second Servant. Ay, boy, ready.
660       First Servant. You are looked for and called for, asked for and
661       sought for, in the great chamber.630
662       Second Servant. We cannot be here and there too. Cheerly, boys; be
663       brisk awhile, and the longer liver take all.
664       [Enter CAPULET, with JULIET and others of his house, meeting the Guests and Maskers]

665       Capulet. Welcome, gentlemen! ladies that have their toes
666       Unplagued with corns will have a bout with you.635
667       Ah ha, my mistresses! which of you all
668       Will now deny to dance? she that makes dainty,
669       She, I'll swear, hath corns; am I come near ye now?
670       Welcome, gentlemen! I have seen the day
671       That I have worn a visor and could tell640
672       A whispering tale in a fair lady's ear,
673       Such as would please: 'tis gone, 'tis gone, 'tis gone:
674       You are welcome, gentlemen! come, musicians, play.
675       A hall, a hall! give room! and foot it, girls.
676       [Music plays, and they dance]645
677       More light, you knaves; and turn the tables up,
678       And quench the fire, the room is grown too hot.
679       Ah, sirrah, this unlook'd-for sport comes well.
680       Nay, sit, nay, sit, good cousin Capulet;
681       For you and I are past our dancing days:650
682       How long is't now since last yourself and I
683       Were in a mask?
684       Second Capulet. By'r lady, thirty years.
685       Capulet. What, man! 'tis not so much, 'tis not so much:
686       'Tis since the nuptials of Lucentio,655
687       Come pentecost as quickly as it will,
688       Some five and twenty years; and then we mask'd.
689       Second Capulet. 'Tis more, 'tis more, his son is elder, sir;
690       His son is thirty.
691       Capulet. Will you tell me that?660
692       His son was but a ward two years ago.
693       Romeo. [To a Servingman] What lady is that, which doth
694       enrich the hand
695       Of yonder knight?
696       Servant. I know not, sir.665
697       Romeo. O, she doth teach the torches to burn bright!
698       It seems she hangs upon the cheek of night
699       Like a rich jewel in an Ethiope's ear;
700       Beauty too rich for use, for earth too dear!
701       So shows a snowy dove trooping with crows,670
702       As yonder lady o'er her fellows shows.
703       The measure done, I'll watch her place of stand,
704       And, touching hers, make blessed my rude hand.
705       Did my heart love till now? forswear it, sight!
706       For I ne'er saw true beauty till this night.675
707       Tybalt. This, by his voice, should be a Montague.
708       Fetch me my rapier, boy. What dares the slave
709       Come hither, cover'd with an antic face,
710       To fleer and scorn at our solemnity?
711       Now, by the stock and honour of my kin,680
712       To strike him dead, I hold it not a sin.
713       Capulet. Why, how now, kinsman! wherefore storm you so?
714       Tybalt. Uncle, this is a Montague, our foe,
715       A villain that is hither come in spite,
716       To scorn at our solemnity this night.685
717       Capulet. Young Romeo is it?
718       Tybalt. 'Tis he, that villain Romeo.
719       Capulet. Content thee, gentle coz, let him alone;
720       He bears him like a portly gentleman;
721       And, to say truth, Verona brags of him690
722       To be a virtuous and well-govern'd youth:
723       I would not for the wealth of all the town
724       Here in my house do him disparagement:
725       Therefore be patient, take no note of him:
726       It is my will, the which if thou respect,695
727       Show a fair presence and put off these frowns,
728       And ill-beseeming semblance for a feast.
729       Tybalt. It fits, when such a villain is a guest:
730       I'll not endure him.
731       Capulet. He shall be endured:700
732       What, goodman boy! I say, he shall: go to;
733       Am I the master here, or you? go to.
734       You'll not endure him! God shall mend my soul!
735       You'll make a mutiny among my guests!
736       You will set cock-a-hoop! you'll be the man!705
737       Tybalt. Why, uncle, 'tis a shame.
738       Capulet. Go to, go to;
739       You are a saucy boy: is't so, indeed?
740       This trick may chance to scathe you, I know what:
741       You must contrary me! marry, 'tis time.710
742       Well said, my hearts! You are a princox; go:
743       Be quiet, or—More light, more light! For shame!
744       I'll make you quiet. What, cheerly, my hearts!
745       Tybalt. Patience perforce with wilful choler meeting
746       Makes my flesh tremble in their different greeting.715
747       I will withdraw: but this intrusion shall
748       Now seeming sweet convert to bitter gall.
749       [Exit]

750       Romeo. [To JULIET] If I profane with my unworthiest hand
751       This holy shrine, the gentle fine is this:720
752       My lips, two blushing pilgrims, ready stand
753       To smooth that rough touch with a tender kiss.
754       Juliet. Good pilgrim, you do wrong your hand too much,
755       Which mannerly devotion shows in this;
756       For saints have hands that pilgrims' hands do touch,725
757       And palm to palm is holy palmers' kiss.
758       Romeo. Have not saints lips, and holy palmers too?
759       Juliet. Ay, pilgrim, lips that they must use in prayer.
760       Romeo. O, then, dear saint, let lips do what hands do;
761       They pray, grant thou, lest faith turn to despair.730
762       Juliet. Saints do not move, though grant for prayers' sake.
763       Romeo. Then move not, while my prayer's effect I take.
764       Thus from my lips, by yours, my sin is purged.
765       Juliet. Then have my lips the sin that they have took.
766       Romeo. Sin from thy lips? O trespass sweetly urged!735
767       Give me my sin again.
768       Juliet. You kiss by the book.
769       Nurse. Madam, your mother craves a word with you.
770       Romeo. What is her mother?
771       Nurse. Marry, bachelor,740
772       Her mother is the lady of the house,
773       And a good lady, and a wise and virtuous
774       I nursed her daughter, that you talk'd withal;
775       I tell you, he that can lay hold of her
776       Shall have the chinks.745
777       Romeo. Is she a Capulet?
778       O dear account! my life is my foe's debt.
779       Benvolio. Away, begone; the sport is at the best.
780       Romeo. Ay, so I fear; the more is my unrest.
781       Capulet. Nay, gentlemen, prepare not to be gone;750
782       We have a trifling foolish banquet towards.
783       Is it e'en so? why, then, I thank you all
784       I thank you, honest gentlemen; good night.
785       More torches here! Come on then, let's to bed.
786       Ah, sirrah, by my fay, it waxes late:755
787       I'll to my rest.
788       [Exeunt all but JULIET and Nurse]

789       Juliet. Come hither, nurse. What is yond gentleman?
790       Nurse. The son and heir of old Tiberio.
791       Juliet. What's he that now is going out of door?760
792       Nurse. Marry, that, I think, be young Petrucio.
793       Juliet. What's he that follows there, that would not dance?
794       Nurse. I know not.
795       Juliet. Go ask his name: if he be married.
796       My grave is like to be my wedding bed.765
797       Nurse. His name is Romeo, and a Montague;
798       The only son of your great enemy.
799       Juliet. My only love sprung from my only hate!
800       Too early seen unknown, and known too late!
801       Prodigious birth of love it is to me,770
802       That I must love a loathed enemy.
803       Nurse. What's this? what's this?
804       Juliet. A rhyme I learn'd even now
805       Of one I danced withal.
806       [One calls within 'Juliet.']

807       Nurse. Anon, anon!
808       Come, let's away; the strangers all are gone.
809       [Exeunt]

810        	     	
811       Prologue

812            	 
813       [Enter Chorus]

814       Chorus. Now old desire doth in his death-bed lie,780
815       And young affection gapes to be his heir;
816       That fair for which love groan'd for and would die,
817       With tender Juliet match'd, is now not fair.
818       Now Romeo is beloved and loves again,
819       Alike betwitched by the charm of looks,785
820       But to his foe supposed he must complain,
821       And she steal love's sweet bait from fearful hooks:
822       Being held a foe, he may not have access
823       To breathe such vows as lovers use to swear;
824       And she as much in love, her means much less790
825       To meet her new-beloved any where:
826       But passion lends them power, time means, to meet
827       Tempering extremities with extreme sweet.
828       [Exit]

829        	     	
830       Act II, Scene 1

831       A lane by the wall of Capulet’s orchard.

832            	 
833       [Enter ROMEO]

834       Romeo. Can I go forward when my heart is here?
835       Turn back, dull earth, and find thy centre out.
836       [He climbs the wall, and leaps down within it]

837       [Enter BENVOLIO and MERCUTIO]

838       Benvolio. Romeo! my cousin Romeo!800
839       Mercutio. He is wise;
840       And, on my lie, hath stol'n him home to bed.
841       Benvolio. He ran this way, and leap'd this orchard wall:
842       Call, good Mercutio.
843       Mercutio. Nay, I'll conjure too.805
844       Romeo! humours! madman! passion! lover!
845       Appear thou in the likeness of a sigh:
846       Speak but one rhyme, and I am satisfied;
847       Cry but 'Ay me!' pronounce but 'love' and 'dove;'
848       Speak to my gossip Venus one fair word,810
849       One nick-name for her purblind son and heir,
850       Young Adam Cupid, he that shot so trim,
851       When King Cophetua loved the beggar-maid!
852       He heareth not, he stirreth not, he moveth not;
853       The ape is dead, and I must conjure him.815
854       I conjure thee by Rosaline's bright eyes,
855       By her high forehead and her scarlet lip,
856       By her fine foot, straight leg and quivering thigh
857       And the demesnes that there adjacent lie,
858       That in thy likeness thou appear to us!820
859       Benvolio. And if he hear thee, thou wilt anger him.
860       Mercutio. This cannot anger him: 'twould anger him
861       To raise a spirit in his mistress' circle
862       Of some strange nature, letting it there stand
863       Till she had laid it and conjured it down;825
864       That were some spite: my invocation
865       Is fair and honest, and in his mistress' name
866       I conjure only but to raise up him.
867       Benvolio. Come, he hath hid himself among these trees,
868       To be consorted with the humorous night:830
869       Blind is his love and best befits the dark.
870       Mercutio. If love be blind, love cannot hit the mark.
871       Now will he sit under a medlar tree,
872       And wish his mistress were that kind of fruit
873       As maids call medlars, when they laugh alone.835
874       Romeo, that she were, O, that she were
875       An open et caetera, thou a poperin pear!
876       Romeo, good night: I'll to my truckle-bed;
877       This field-bed is too cold for me to sleep:
878       Come, shall we go?840
879       Benvolio. Go, then; for 'tis in vain
880       To seek him here that means not to be found.
881       [Exeunt]

882        	     	
883       Act II, Scene 2

884       Capulet’s orchard.

885            	 
886       [Enter ROMEO]

887       Romeo. He jests at scars that never felt a wound.845
888       [JULIET appears above at a window]
889       But, soft! what light through yonder window breaks?
890       It is the east, and Juliet is the sun.
891       Arise, fair sun, and kill the envious moon,
892       Who is already sick and pale with grief,850
893       That thou her maid art far more fair than she:
894       Be not her maid, since she is envious;
895       Her vestal livery is but sick and green
896       And none but fools do wear it; cast it off.
897       It is my lady, O, it is my love!855
898       O, that she knew she were!
899       She speaks yet she says nothing: what of that?
900       Her eye discourses; I will answer it.
901       I am too bold, 'tis not to me she speaks:
902       Two of the fairest stars in all the heaven,860
903       Having some business, do entreat her eyes
904       To twinkle in their spheres till they return.
905       What if her eyes were there, they in her head?
906       The brightness of her cheek would shame those stars,
907       As daylight doth a lamp; her eyes in heaven865
908       Would through the airy region stream so bright
909       That birds would sing and think it were not night.
910       See, how she leans her cheek upon her hand!
911       O, that I were a glove upon that hand,
912       That I might touch that cheek!870
913       Juliet. Ay me!
914       Romeo. She speaks:
915       O, speak again, bright angel! for thou art
916       As glorious to this night, being o'er my head
917       As is a winged messenger of heaven875
918       Unto the white-upturned wondering eyes
919       Of mortals that fall back to gaze on him
920       When he bestrides the lazy-pacing clouds
921       And sails upon the bosom of the air.
922       Juliet. O Romeo, Romeo! wherefore art thou Romeo?880
923       Deny thy father and refuse thy name;
924       Or, if thou wilt not, be but sworn my love,
925       And I'll no longer be a Capulet.
926       Romeo. [Aside] Shall I hear more, or shall I speak at this?
927       Juliet. 'Tis but thy name that is my enemy;885
928       Thou art thyself, though not a Montague.
929       What's Montague? it is nor hand, nor foot,
930       Nor arm, nor face, nor any other part
931       Belonging to a man. O, be some other name!
932       What's in a name? that which we call a rose890
933       By any other name would smell as sweet;
934       So Romeo would, were he not Romeo call'd,
935       Retain that dear perfection which he owes
936       Without that title. Romeo, doff thy name,
937       And for that name which is no part of thee895
938       Take all myself.
939       Romeo. I take thee at thy word:
940       Call me but love, and I'll be new baptized;
941       Henceforth I never will be Romeo.
942       Juliet. What man art thou that thus bescreen'd in night900
943       So stumblest on my counsel?
944       Romeo. By a name
945       I know not how to tell thee who I am:
946       My name, dear saint, is hateful to myself,
947       Because it is an enemy to thee;905
948       Had I it written, I would tear the word.
949       Juliet. My ears have not yet drunk a hundred words
950       Of that tongue's utterance, yet I know the sound:
951       Art thou not Romeo and a Montague?
952       Romeo. Neither, fair saint, if either thee dislike.910
953       Juliet. How camest thou hither, tell me, and wherefore?
954       The orchard walls are high and hard to climb,
955       And the place death, considering who thou art,
956       If any of my kinsmen find thee here.
957       Romeo. With love's light wings did I o'er-perch these walls;915
958       For stony limits cannot hold love out,
959       And what love can do that dares love attempt;
960       Therefore thy kinsmen are no let to me.
961       Juliet. If they do see thee, they will murder thee.
962       Romeo. Alack, there lies more peril in thine eye920
963       Than twenty of their swords: look thou but sweet,
964       And I am proof against their enmity.
965       Juliet. I would not for the world they saw thee here.
966       Romeo. I have night's cloak to hide me from their sight;
967       And but thou love me, let them find me here:925
968       My life were better ended by their hate,
969       Than death prorogued, wanting of thy love.
970       Juliet. By whose direction found'st thou out this place?
971       Romeo. By love, who first did prompt me to inquire;
972       He lent me counsel and I lent him eyes.930
973       I am no pilot; yet, wert thou as far
974       As that vast shore wash'd with the farthest sea,
975       I would adventure for such merchandise.
976       Juliet. Thou know'st the mask of night is on my face,
977       Else would a maiden blush bepaint my cheek935
978       For that which thou hast heard me speak to-night
979       Fain would I dwell on form, fain, fain deny
980       What I have spoke: but farewell compliment!
981       Dost thou love me? I know thou wilt say 'Ay,'
982       And I will take thy word: yet if thou swear'st,940
983       Thou mayst prove false; at lovers' perjuries
984       Then say, Jove laughs. O gentle Romeo,
985       If thou dost love, pronounce it faithfully:
986       Or if thou think'st I am too quickly won,
987       I'll frown and be perverse an say thee nay,945
988       So thou wilt woo; but else, not for the world.
989       In truth, fair Montague, I am too fond,
990       And therefore thou mayst think my 'havior light:
991       But trust me, gentleman, I'll prove more true
992       Than those that have more cunning to be strange.950
993       I should have been more strange, I must confess,
994       But that thou overheard'st, ere I was ware,
995       My true love's passion: therefore pardon me,
996       And not impute this yielding to light love,
997       Which the dark night hath so discovered.955
998       Romeo. Lady, by yonder blessed moon I swear
999       That tips with silver all these fruit-tree tops—
1000      Juliet. O, swear not by the moon, the inconstant moon,
1001      That monthly changes in her circled orb,
1002      Lest that thy love prove likewise variable.960
1003      Romeo. What shall I swear by?
1004      Juliet. Do not swear at all;
1005      Or, if thou wilt, swear by thy gracious self,
1006      Which is the god of my idolatry,
1007      And I'll believe thee.965
1008      Romeo. If my heart's dear love—
1009      Juliet. Well, do not swear: although I joy in thee,
1010      I have no joy of this contract to-night:
1011      It is too rash, too unadvised, too sudden;
1012      Too like the lightning, which doth cease to be970
1013      Ere one can say 'It lightens.' Sweet, good night!
1014      This bud of love, by summer's ripening breath,
1015      May prove a beauteous flower when next we meet.
1016      Good night, good night! as sweet repose and rest
1017      Come to thy heart as that within my breast!975
1018      Romeo. O, wilt thou leave me so unsatisfied?
1019      Juliet. What satisfaction canst thou have to-night?
1020      Romeo. The exchange of thy love's faithful vow for mine.
1021      Juliet. I gave thee mine before thou didst request it:
1022      And yet I would it were to give again.980
1023      Romeo. Wouldst thou withdraw it? for what purpose, love?
1024      Juliet. But to be frank, and give it thee again.
1025      And yet I wish but for the thing I have:
1026      My bounty is as boundless as the sea,
1027      My love as deep; the more I give to thee,985
1028      The more I have, for both are infinite.
1029      [Nurse calls within]
1030      I hear some noise within; dear love, adieu!
1031      Anon, good nurse! Sweet Montague, be true.
1032      Stay but a little, I will come again.990
1033      [Exit, above]

1034      Romeo. O blessed, blessed night! I am afeard.
1035      Being in night, all this is but a dream,
1036      Too flattering-sweet to be substantial.
1037      [Re-enter JULIET, above]

1038      Juliet. Three words, dear Romeo, and good night indeed.
1039      If that thy bent of love be honourable,
1040      Thy purpose marriage, send me word to-morrow,
1041      By one that I'll procure to come to thee,
1042      Where and what time thou wilt perform the rite;1000
1043      And all my fortunes at thy foot I'll lay
1044      And follow thee my lord throughout the world.
1045      Nurse. [Within] Madam!
1046      Juliet. I come, anon.—But if thou mean'st not well,
1047      I do beseech thee—1005
1048      Nurse. [Within] Madam!
1049      Juliet. By and by, I come:—
1050      To cease thy suit, and leave me to my grief:
1051      To-morrow will I send.
1052      Romeo. So thrive my soul—1010
1053      Juliet. A thousand times good night!
1054      [Exit, above]

1055      Romeo. A thousand times the worse, to want thy light.
1056      Love goes toward love, as schoolboys from
1057      their books,1015
1058      But love from love, toward school with heavy looks.
1059      [Retiring]

1060      [Re-enter JULIET, above]

1061      Juliet. Hist! Romeo, hist! O, for a falconer's voice,
1062      To lure this tassel-gentle back again!1020
1063      Bondage is hoarse, and may not speak aloud;
1064      Else would I tear the cave where Echo lies,
1065      And make her airy tongue more hoarse than mine,
1066      With repetition of my Romeo's name.
1067      Romeo. It is my soul that calls upon my name:1025
1068      How silver-sweet sound lovers' tongues by night,
1069      Like softest music to attending ears!
1070      Juliet. Romeo!
1071      Romeo. My dear?
1072      Juliet. At what o'clock to-morrow1030
1073      Shall I send to thee?
1074      Romeo. At the hour of nine.
1075      Juliet. I will not fail: 'tis twenty years till then.
1076      I have forgot why I did call thee back.
1077      Romeo. Let me stand here till thou remember it.1035
1078      Juliet. I shall forget, to have thee still stand there,
1079      Remembering how I love thy company.
1080      Romeo. And I'll still stay, to have thee still forget,
1081      Forgetting any other home but this.
1082      Juliet. 'Tis almost morning; I would have thee gone:1040
1083      And yet no further than a wanton's bird;
1084      Who lets it hop a little from her hand,
1085      Like a poor prisoner in his twisted gyves,
1086      And with a silk thread plucks it back again,
1087      So loving-jealous of his liberty.1045
1088      Romeo. I would I were thy bird.
1089      Juliet. Sweet, so would I:
1090      Yet I should kill thee with much cherishing.
1091      Good night, good night! parting is such
1092      sweet sorrow,1050
1093      That I shall say good night till it be morrow.
1094      [Exit above]

1095      Romeo. Sleep dwell upon thine eyes, peace in thy breast!
1096      Would I were sleep and peace, so sweet to rest!
1097      Hence will I to my ghostly father's cell,1055
1098      His help to crave, and my dear hap to tell.
1099      [Exit]

1100       	     	
1101      Act II, Scene 3

1102      Friar Laurence’s cell.

1103           	 
1104      [Enter FRIAR LAURENCE, with a basket]

1105      Friar Laurence. The grey-eyed morn smiles on the frowning night,
1106      Chequering the eastern clouds with streaks of light,1060
1107      And flecked darkness like a drunkard reels
1108      From forth day's path and Titan's fiery wheels:
1109      Now, ere the sun advance his burning eye,
1110      The day to cheer and night's dank dew to dry,
1111      I must up-fill this osier cage of ours1065
1112      With baleful weeds and precious-juiced flowers.
1113      The earth that's nature's mother is her tomb;
1114      What is her burying grave that is her womb,
1115      And from her womb children of divers kind
1116      We sucking on her natural bosom find,1070
1117      Many for many virtues excellent,
1118      None but for some and yet all different.
1119      O, mickle is the powerful grace that lies
1120      In herbs, plants, stones, and their true qualities:
1121      For nought so vile that on the earth doth live1075
1122      But to the earth some special good doth give,
1123      Nor aught so good but strain'd from that fair use
1124      Revolts from true birth, stumbling on abuse:
1125      Virtue itself turns vice, being misapplied;
1126      And vice sometimes by action dignified.1080
1127      Within the infant rind of this small flower
1128      Poison hath residence and medicine power:
1129      For this, being smelt, with that part cheers each part;
1130      Being tasted, slays all senses with the heart.
1131      Two such opposed kings encamp them still1085
1132      In man as well as herbs, grace and rude will;
1133      And where the worser is predominant,
1134      Full soon the canker death eats up that plant.
1135      [Enter ROMEO]

1136      Romeo. Good morrow, father.1090
1137      Friar Laurence. Benedicite!
1138      What early tongue so sweet saluteth me?
1139      Young son, it argues a distemper'd head
1140      So soon to bid good morrow to thy bed:
1141      Care keeps his watch in every old man's eye,1095
1142      And where care lodges, sleep will never lie;
1143      But where unbruised youth with unstuff'd brain
1144      Doth couch his limbs, there golden sleep doth reign:
1145      Therefore thy earliness doth me assure
1146      Thou art up-roused by some distemperature;1100
1147      Or if not so, then here I hit it right,
1148      Our Romeo hath not been in bed to-night.
1149      Romeo. That last is true; the sweeter rest was mine.
1150      Friar Laurence. God pardon sin! wast thou with Rosaline?
1151      Romeo. With Rosaline, my ghostly father? no;1105
1152      I have forgot that name, and that name's woe.
1153      Friar Laurence. That's my good son: but where hast thou been, then?
1154      Romeo. I'll tell thee, ere thou ask it me again.
1155      I have been feasting with mine enemy,
1156      Where on a sudden one hath wounded me,1110
1157      That's by me wounded: both our remedies
1158      Within thy help and holy physic lies:
1159      I bear no hatred, blessed man, for, lo,
1160      My intercession likewise steads my foe.
1161      Friar Laurence. Be plain, good son, and homely in thy drift;1115
1162      Riddling confession finds but riddling shrift.
1163      Romeo. Then plainly know my heart's dear love is set
1164      On the fair daughter of rich Capulet:
1165      As mine on hers, so hers is set on mine;
1166      And all combined, save what thou must combine1120
1167      By holy marriage: when and where and how
1168      We met, we woo'd and made exchange of vow,
1169      I'll tell thee as we pass; but this I pray,
1170      That thou consent to marry us to-day.
1171      Friar Laurence. Holy Saint Francis, what a change is here!1125
1172      Is Rosaline, whom thou didst love so dear,
1173      So soon forsaken? young men's love then lies
1174      Not truly in their hearts, but in their eyes.
1175      Jesu Maria, what a deal of brine
1176      Hath wash'd thy sallow cheeks for Rosaline!1130
1177      How much salt water thrown away in waste,
1178      To season love, that of it doth not taste!
1179      The sun not yet thy sighs from heaven clears,
1180      Thy old groans ring yet in my ancient ears;
1181      Lo, here upon thy cheek the stain doth sit1135
1182      Of an old tear that is not wash'd off yet:
1183      If e'er thou wast thyself and these woes thine,
1184      Thou and these woes were all for Rosaline:
1185      And art thou changed? pronounce this sentence then,
1186      Women may fall, when there's no strength in men.1140
1187      Romeo. Thou chid'st me oft for loving Rosaline.
1188      Friar Laurence. For doting, not for loving, pupil mine.
1189      Romeo. And bad'st me bury love.
1190      Friar Laurence. Not in a grave,
1191      To lay one in, another out to have.1145
1192      Romeo. I pray thee, chide not; she whom I love now
1193      Doth grace for grace and love for love allow;
1194      The other did not so.
1195      Friar Laurence. O, she knew well
1196      Thy love did read by rote and could not spell.1150
1197      But come, young waverer, come, go with me,
1198      In one respect I'll thy assistant be;
1199      For this alliance may so happy prove,
1200      To turn your households' rancour to pure love.
1201      Romeo. O, let us hence; I stand on sudden haste.1155
1202      Friar Laurence. Wisely and slow; they stumble that run fast.
1203      [Exeunt]

1205      Act II, Scene 4

1206      A street.

1207           	 
1208      [Enter BENVOLIO and MERCUTIO]

1209      Mercutio. Where the devil should this Romeo be?
1210      Came he not home to-night?1160
1211      Benvolio. Not to his father's; I spoke with his man.
1212      Mercutio. Ah, that same pale hard-hearted wench, that Rosaline.
1213      Torments him so, that he will sure run mad.
1214      Benvolio. Tybalt, the kinsman of old Capulet,
1215      Hath sent a letter to his father's house.1165
1216      Mercutio. A challenge, on my life.
1217      Benvolio. Romeo will answer it.
1218      Mercutio. Any man that can write may answer a letter.
1219      Benvolio. Nay, he will answer the letter's master, how he
1220      dares, being dared.1170
1221      Mercutio. Alas poor Romeo! he is already dead; stabbed with a
1222      white wench's black eye; shot through the ear with a
1223      love-song; the very pin of his heart cleft with the
1224      blind bow-boy's butt-shaft: and is he a man to
1225      encounter Tybalt?1175
1226      Benvolio. Why, what is Tybalt?
1227      Mercutio. More than prince of cats, I can tell you. O, he is
1228      the courageous captain of compliments. He fights as
1229      you sing prick-song, keeps time, distance, and
1230      proportion; rests me his minim rest, one, two, and1180
1231      the third in your bosom: the very butcher of a silk
1232      button, a duellist, a duellist; a gentleman of the
1233      very first house, of the first and second cause:
1234      ah, the immortal passado! the punto reverso! the
1235      hai!1185
1236      Benvolio. The what?
1237      Mercutio. The pox of such antic, lisping, affecting
1238      fantasticoes; these new tuners of accents! 'By Jesu,
1239      a very good blade! a very tall man! a very good
1240      whore!' Why, is not this a lamentable thing,1190
1241      grandsire, that we should be thus afflicted with
1242      these strange flies, these fashion-mongers, these
1243      perdona-mi's, who stand so much on the new form,
1244      that they cannot at ease on the old bench? O, their
1245      bones, their bones!1195
1246      [Enter ROMEO]

1247      Benvolio. Here comes Romeo, here comes Romeo.
1248      Mercutio. Without his roe, like a dried herring: flesh, flesh,
1249      how art thou fishified! Now is he for the numbers
1250      that Petrarch flowed in: Laura to his lady was but a1200
1251      kitchen-wench; marry, she had a better love to
1252      be-rhyme her; Dido a dowdy; Cleopatra a gipsy;
1253      Helen and Hero hildings and harlots; Thisbe a grey
1254      eye or so, but not to the purpose. Signior
1255      Romeo, bon jour! there's a French salutation1205
1256      to your French slop. You gave us the counterfeit
1257      fairly last night.
1258      Romeo. Good morrow to you both. What counterfeit did I give you?
1259      Mercutio. The ship, sir, the slip; can you not conceive?
1260      Romeo. Pardon, good Mercutio, my business was great; and in1210
1261      such a case as mine a man may strain courtesy.
1262      Mercutio. That's as much as to say, such a case as yours
1263      constrains a man to bow in the hams.
1264      Romeo. Meaning, to court'sy.
1265      Mercutio. Thou hast most kindly hit it.1215
1266      Romeo. A most courteous exposition.
1267      Mercutio. Nay, I am the very pink of courtesy.
1268      Romeo. Pink for flower.
1269      Mercutio. Right.
1270      Romeo. Why, then is my pump well flowered.1220
1271      Mercutio. Well said: follow me this jest now till thou hast
1272      worn out thy pump, that when the single sole of it
1273      is worn, the jest may remain after the wearing sole singular.
1274      Romeo. O single-soled jest, solely singular for the
1275      singleness.1225
1276      Mercutio. Come between us, good Benvolio; my wits faint.
1277      Romeo. Switch and spurs, switch and spurs; or I'll cry a match.
1278      Mercutio. Nay, if thy wits run the wild-goose chase, I have
1279      done, for thou hast more of the wild-goose in one of
1280      thy wits than, I am sure, I have in my whole five:1230
1281      was I with you there for the goose?
1282      Romeo. Thou wast never with me for any thing when thou wast
1283      not there for the goose.
1284      Mercutio. I will bite thee by the ear for that jest.
1285      Romeo. Nay, good goose, bite not.1235
1286      Mercutio. Thy wit is a very bitter sweeting; it is a most
1287      sharp sauce.
1288      Romeo. And is it not well served in to a sweet goose?
1289      Mercutio. O here's a wit of cheveril, that stretches from an
1290      inch narrow to an ell broad!1240
1291      Romeo. I stretch it out for that word 'broad;' which added
1292      to the goose, proves thee far and wide a broad goose.
1293      Mercutio. Why, is not this better now than groaning for love?
1294      now art thou sociable, now art thou Romeo; now art
1295      thou what thou art, by art as well as by nature:1245
1296      for this drivelling love is like a great natural,
1297      that runs lolling up and down to hide his bauble in a hole.
1298      Benvolio. Stop there, stop there.
1299      Mercutio. Thou desirest me to stop in my tale against the hair.
1300      Benvolio. Thou wouldst else have made thy tale large.1250
1301      Mercutio. O, thou art deceived; I would have made it short:
1302      for I was come to the whole depth of my tale; and
1303      meant, indeed, to occupy the argument no longer.
1304      Romeo. Here's goodly gear!
1305      [Enter Nurse and PETER]

1306      Mercutio. A sail, a sail!
1307      Benvolio. Two, two; a shirt and a smock.
1308      Nurse. Peter!
1309      Peter. Anon!
1310      Nurse. My fan, Peter.1260
1311      Mercutio. Good Peter, to hide her face; for her fan's the
1312      fairer face.
1313      Nurse. God ye good morrow, gentlemen.
1314      Mercutio. God ye good den, fair gentlewoman.
1315      Nurse. Is it good den?1265
1316      Mercutio. 'Tis no less, I tell you, for the bawdy hand of the
1317      dial is now upon the prick of noon.
1318      Nurse. Out upon you! what a man are you!
1319      Romeo. One, gentlewoman, that God hath made for himself to
1320      mar.1270
1321      Nurse. By my troth, it is well said; 'for himself to mar,'
1322      quoth a'? Gentlemen, can any of you tell me where I
1323      may find the young Romeo?
1324      Romeo. I can tell you; but young Romeo will be older when
1325      you have found him than he was when you sought him:1275
1326      I am the youngest of that name, for fault of a worse.
1327      Nurse. You say well.
1328      Mercutio. Yea, is the worst well? very well took, i' faith;
1329      wisely, wisely.
1330      Nurse. if you be he, sir, I desire some confidence with1280
1331      you.
1332      Benvolio. She will indite him to some supper.
1333      Mercutio. A bawd, a bawd, a bawd! so ho!
1334      Romeo. What hast thou found?
1335      Mercutio. No hare, sir; unless a hare, sir, in a lenten pie,1285
1336      that is something stale and hoar ere it be spent.
1337      [Sings]
1338      An old hare hoar,
1339      And an old hare hoar,
1340      Is very good meat in lent1290
1341      But a hare that is hoar
1342      Is too much for a score,
1343      When it hoars ere it be spent.
1344      Romeo, will you come to your father's? we'll
1345      to dinner, thither.1295
1346      Romeo. I will follow you.
1347      Mercutio. Farewell, ancient lady; farewell,
1348      [Singing]
1349      'lady, lady, lady.'
1350      [Exeunt MERCUTIO and BENVOLIO]

1351      Nurse. Marry, farewell! I pray you, sir, what saucy
1352      merchant was this, that was so full of his ropery?
1353      Romeo. A gentleman, nurse, that loves to hear himself talk,
1354      and will speak more in a minute than he will stand
1355      to in a month.1305
1356      Nurse. An a' speak any thing against me, I'll take him
1357      down, an a' were lustier than he is, and twenty such
1358      Jacks; and if I cannot, I'll find those that shall.
1359      Scurvy knave! I am none of his flirt-gills; I am
1360      none of his skains-mates. And thou must stand by1310
1361      too, and suffer every knave to use me at his pleasure?
1362      Peter. I saw no man use you a pleasure; if I had, my weapon
1363      should quickly have been out, I warrant you: I dare
1364      draw as soon as another man, if I see occasion in a
1365      good quarrel, and the law on my side.1315
1366      Nurse. Now, afore God, I am so vexed, that every part about
1367      me quivers. Scurvy knave! Pray you, sir, a word:
1368      and as I told you, my young lady bade me inquire you
1369      out; what she bade me say, I will keep to myself:
1370      but first let me tell ye, if ye should lead her into1320
1371      a fool's paradise, as they say, it were a very gross
1372      kind of behavior, as they say: for the gentlewoman
1373      is young; and, therefore, if you should deal double
1374      with her, truly it were an ill thing to be offered
1375      to any gentlewoman, and very weak dealing.1325
1376      Romeo. Nurse, commend me to thy lady and mistress. I
1377      protest unto thee—
1378      Nurse. Good heart, and, i' faith, I will tell her as much:
1379      Lord, Lord, she will be a joyful woman.
1380      Romeo. What wilt thou tell her, nurse? thou dost not mark me.1330
1381      Nurse. I will tell her, sir, that you do protest; which, as
1382      I take it, is a gentlemanlike offer.
1383      Romeo. Bid her devise
1384      Some means to come to shrift this afternoon;
1385      And there she shall at Friar Laurence' cell1335
1386      Be shrived and married. Here is for thy pains.
1387      Nurse. No truly sir; not a penny.
1388      Romeo. Go to; I say you shall.
1389      Nurse. This afternoon, sir? well, she shall be there.
1390      Romeo. And stay, good nurse, behind the abbey wall:1340
1391      Within this hour my man shall be with thee
1392      And bring thee cords made like a tackled stair;
1393      Which to the high top-gallant of my joy

1395      Farewell; be trusty, and I'll quit thy pains:1345
1396      Farewell; commend me to thy mistress.
1397      Nurse. Now God in heaven bless thee! Hark you, sir.
1398      Romeo. What say'st thou, my dear nurse?
1399      Nurse. Is your man secret? Did you ne'er hear say,
1400      Two may keep counsel, putting one away?1350
1401      Romeo. I warrant thee, my man's as true as steel.
1402      Nurse. Well, sir; my mistress is the sweetest lady—Lord,
1403      Lord! when 'twas a little prating thing:—O, there
1404      is a nobleman in town, one Paris, that would fain
1405      lay knife aboard; but she, good soul, had as lief1355
1406      see a toad, a very toad, as see him. I anger her
1407      sometimes and tell her that Paris is the properer
1408      man; but, I'll warrant you, when I say so, she looks
1409      as pale as any clout in the versal world. Doth not
1410      rosemary and Romeo begin both with a letter?1360
1411      Romeo. Ay, nurse; what of that? both with an R.
1412      Nurse. Ah. mocker! that's the dog's name; R is for
1413      the—No; I know it begins with some other
1414      letter:—and she hath the prettiest sententious of
1415      it, of you and rosemary, that it would do you good1365
1416      to hear it.
1417      Romeo. Commend me to thy lady.
1418      Nurse. Ay, a thousand times.
1419      [Exit Romeo]
1420      Peter!1370
1421      Peter. Anon!
1422      Nurse. Peter, take my fan, and go before and apace.
