1424       	     	
1425      Act II, Scene 5

1426      Capulet’s orchard.

1427           	 
1428      [Enter JULIET]

1429      Juliet. The clock struck nine when I did send the nurse;1375
1430      In half an hour she promised to return.
1431      Perchance she cannot meet him: that's not so.
1432      O, she is lame! love's heralds should be thoughts,
1433      Which ten times faster glide than the sun's beams,
1434      Driving back shadows over louring hills:1380
1435      Therefore do nimble-pinion'd doves draw love,
1436      And therefore hath the wind-swift Cupid wings.
1437      Now is the sun upon the highmost hill
1438      Of this day's journey, and from nine till twelve
1439      Is three long hours, yet she is not come.1385
1440      Had she affections and warm youthful blood,
1441      She would be as swift in motion as a ball;
1442      My words would bandy her to my sweet love,
1443      And his to me:
1444      But old folks, many feign as they were dead;1390
1445      Unwieldy, slow, heavy and pale as lead.
1446      O God, she comes!
1447      [Enter Nurse and PETER]
1448      O honey nurse, what news?
1449      Hast thou met with him? Send thy man away.1395
1450      Nurse. Peter, stay at the gate.
1451      [Exit PETER]

1452      Juliet. Now, good sweet nurse,—O Lord, why look'st thou sad?
1453      Though news be sad, yet tell them merrily;
1454      If good, thou shamest the music of sweet news1400
1455      By playing it to me with so sour a face.
1456      Nurse. I am a-weary, give me leave awhile:
1457      Fie, how my bones ache! what a jaunt have I had!
1458      Juliet. I would thou hadst my bones, and I thy news:
1459      Nay, come, I pray thee, speak; good, good nurse, speak.1405
1460      Nurse. Jesu, what haste? can you not stay awhile?
1461      Do you not see that I am out of breath?
1462      Juliet. How art thou out of breath, when thou hast breath
1463      To say to me that thou art out of breath?
1464      The excuse that thou dost make in this delay1410
1465      Is longer than the tale thou dost excuse.
1466      Is thy news good, or bad? answer to that;
1467      Say either, and I'll stay the circumstance:
1468      Let me be satisfied, is't good or bad?
1469      Nurse. Well, you have made a simple choice; you know not1415
1470      how to choose a man: Romeo! no, not he; though his
1471      face be better than any man's, yet his leg excels
1472      all men's; and for a hand, and a foot, and a body,
1473      though they be not to be talked on, yet they are
1474      past compare: he is not the flower of courtesy,1420
1475      but, I'll warrant him, as gentle as a lamb. Go thy
1476      ways, wench; serve God. What, have you dined at home?
1477      Juliet. No, no: but all this did I know before.
1478      What says he of our marriage? what of that?
1479      Nurse. Lord, how my head aches! what a head have I!1425
1480      It beats as it would fall in twenty pieces.
1481      My back o' t' other side,—O, my back, my back!
1482      Beshrew your heart for sending me about,
1483      To catch my death with jaunting up and down!
1484      Juliet. I' faith, I am sorry that thou art not well.1430
1485      Sweet, sweet, sweet nurse, tell me, what says my love?
1486      Nurse. Your love says, like an honest gentleman, and a
1487      courteous, and a kind, and a handsome, and, I
1488      warrant, a virtuous,—Where is your mother?
1489      Juliet. Where is my mother! why, she is within;1435
1490      Where should she be? How oddly thou repliest!
1491      'Your love says, like an honest gentleman,
1492      Where is your mother?'
1493      Nurse. O God's lady dear!
1494      Are you so hot? marry, come up, I trow;1440
1495      Is this the poultice for my aching bones?
1496      Henceforward do your messages yourself.
1497      Juliet. Here's such a coil! come, what says Romeo?
1498      Nurse. Have you got leave to go to shrift to-day?
1499      Juliet. I have.1445
1500      Nurse. Then hie you hence to Friar Laurence' cell;
1501      There stays a husband to make you a wife:
1502      Now comes the wanton blood up in your cheeks,
1503      They'll be in scarlet straight at any news.
1504      Hie you to church; I must another way,1450
1505      To fetch a ladder, by the which your love
1506      Must climb a bird's nest soon when it is dark:
1507      I am the drudge and toil in your delight,
1508      But you shall bear the burden soon at night.
1509      Go; I'll to dinner: hie you to the cell.1455
1510      Juliet. Hie to high fortune! Honest nurse, farewell.
1511      [Exeunt]

1512       	     	
1513      Act II, Scene 6

1514      Friar Laurence’s cell.

1515           	 
1516      [Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and ROMEO]

1517      Friar Laurence. So smile the heavens upon this holy act,
1518      That after hours with sorrow chide us not!1460
1519      Romeo. Amen, amen! but come what sorrow can,
1520      It cannot countervail the exchange of joy
1521      That one short minute gives me in her sight:
1522      Do thou but close our hands with holy words,
1523      Then love-devouring death do what he dare;1465
1524      It is enough I may but call her mine.
1525      Friar Laurence. These violent delights have violent ends
1526      And in their triumph die, like fire and powder,
1527      Which as they kiss consume: the sweetest honey
1528      Is loathsome in his own deliciousness1470
1529      And in the taste confounds the appetite:
1530      Therefore love moderately; long love doth so;
1531      Too swift arrives as tardy as too slow.
1532      [Enter JULIET]
1533      Here comes the lady: O, so light a foot1475
1534      Will ne'er wear out the everlasting flint:
1535      A lover may bestride the gossamer
1536      That idles in the wanton summer air,
1537      And yet not fall; so light is vanity.
1538      Juliet. Good even to my ghostly confessor.1480
1539      Friar Laurence. Romeo shall thank thee, daughter, for us both.
1540      Juliet. As much to him, else is his thanks too much.
1541      Romeo. Ah, Juliet, if the measure of thy joy
1542      Be heap'd like mine and that thy skill be more
1543      To blazon it, then sweeten with thy breath1485
1544      This neighbour air, and let rich music's tongue
1545      Unfold the imagined happiness that both
1546      Receive in either by this dear encounter.
1547      Juliet. Conceit, more rich in matter than in words,
1548      Brags of his substance, not of ornament:1490
1549      They are but beggars that can count their worth;
1550      But my true love is grown to such excess
1551      I cannot sum up sum of half my wealth.
1552      Friar Laurence. Come, come with me, and we will make short work;
1553      For, by your leaves, you shall not stay alone1495
1554      Till holy church incorporate two in one.
1555      [Exeunt]

1557      Act III, Scene 1

1558      A public place.

1559           	 
1560      [Enter MERCUTIO, BENVOLIO, Page, and Servants]

1561      Benvolio. I pray thee, good Mercutio, let's retire:
1562      The day is hot, the Capulets abroad,1500
1563      And, if we meet, we shall not scape a brawl;
1564      For now, these hot days, is the mad blood stirring.
1565      Mercutio. Thou art like one of those fellows that when he
1566      enters the confines of a tavern claps me his sword
1567      upon the table and says 'God send me no need of1505
1568      thee!' and by the operation of the second cup draws
1569      it on the drawer, when indeed there is no need.
1570      Benvolio. Am I like such a fellow?
1571      Mercutio. Come, come, thou art as hot a Jack in thy mood as
1572      any in Italy, and as soon moved to be moody, and as1510
1573      soon moody to be moved.
1574      Benvolio. And what to?
1575      Mercutio. Nay, an there were two such, we should have none
1576      shortly, for one would kill the other. Thou! why,
1577      thou wilt quarrel with a man that hath a hair more,1515
1578      or a hair less, in his beard, than thou hast: thou
1579      wilt quarrel with a man for cracking nuts, having no
1580      other reason but because thou hast hazel eyes: what
1581      eye but such an eye would spy out such a quarrel?
1582      Thy head is as fun of quarrels as an egg is full of1520
1583      meat, and yet thy head hath been beaten as addle as
1584      an egg for quarrelling: thou hast quarrelled with a
1585      man for coughing in the street, because he hath
1586      wakened thy dog that hath lain asleep in the sun:
1587      didst thou not fall out with a tailor for wearing1525
1588      his new doublet before Easter? with another, for
1589      tying his new shoes with old riband? and yet thou
1590      wilt tutor me from quarrelling!
1591      Benvolio. An I were so apt to quarrel as thou art, any man
1592      should buy the fee-simple of my life for an hour and a quarter.1530
1593      Mercutio. The fee-simple! O simple!
1594      Benvolio. By my head, here come the Capulets.
1595      Mercutio. By my heel, I care not.
1596      [Enter TYBALT and others]

1597      Tybalt. Follow me close, for I will speak to them.1535
1598      Gentlemen, good den: a word with one of you.
1599      Mercutio. And but one word with one of us? couple it with
1600      something; make it a word and a blow.
1601      Tybalt. You shall find me apt enough to that, sir, an you
1602      will give me occasion.1540
1603      Mercutio. Could you not take some occasion without giving?
1604      Tybalt. Mercutio, thou consort'st with Romeo,—
1605      Mercutio. Consort! what, dost thou make us minstrels? an
1606      thou make minstrels of us, look to hear nothing but
1607      discords: here's my fiddlestick; here's that shall1545
1608      make you dance. 'Zounds, consort!
1609      Benvolio. We talk here in the public haunt of men:
1610      Either withdraw unto some private place,
1611      And reason coldly of your grievances,
1612      Or else depart; here all eyes gaze on us.1550
1613      Mercutio. Men's eyes were made to look, and let them gaze;
1614      I will not budge for no man's pleasure, I.
1615      [Enter ROMEO]

1616      Tybalt. Well, peace be with you, sir: here comes my man.
1617      Mercutio. But I'll be hanged, sir, if he wear your livery:1555
1618      Marry, go before to field, he'll be your follower;
1619      Your worship in that sense may call him 'man.'
1620      Tybalt. Romeo, the hate I bear thee can afford
1621      No better term than this,—thou art a villain.
1622      Romeo. Tybalt, the reason that I have to love thee1560
1623      Doth much excuse the appertaining rage
1624      To such a greeting: villain am I none;
1625      Therefore farewell; I see thou know'st me not.
1626      Tybalt. Boy, this shall not excuse the injuries
1627      That thou hast done me; therefore turn and draw.1565
1628      Romeo. I do protest, I never injured thee,
1629      But love thee better than thou canst devise,
1630      Till thou shalt know the reason of my love:
1631      And so, good Capulet,—which name I tender
1632      As dearly as my own,—be satisfied.1570
1633      Mercutio. O calm, dishonourable, vile submission!
1634      Alla stoccata carries it away.
1635      [Draws]
1636      Tybalt, you rat-catcher, will you walk?
1637      Tybalt. What wouldst thou have with me?1575
1638      Mercutio. Good king of cats, nothing but one of your nine
1639      lives; that I mean to make bold withal, and as you
1640      shall use me hereafter, drybeat the rest of the
1641      eight. Will you pluck your sword out of his pitcher
1642      by the ears? make haste, lest mine be about your1580
1643      ears ere it be out.
1644      Tybalt. I am for you.
1645      [Drawing]

1646      Romeo. Gentle Mercutio, put thy rapier up.
1647      Mercutio. Come, sir, your passado.1585
1648      [They fight]

1649      Romeo. Draw, Benvolio; beat down their weapons.
1650      Gentlemen, for shame, forbear this outrage!
1651      Tybalt, Mercutio, the prince expressly hath
1652      Forbidden bandying in Verona streets:1590
1653      Hold, Tybalt! good Mercutio!
1654      [TYBALT under ROMEO's arm stabs MERCUTIO, and flies with his followers]

1655      Mercutio. I am hurt.
1656      A plague o' both your houses! I am sped.
1657      Is he gone, and hath nothing?1595
1658      Benvolio. What, art thou hurt?
1659      Mercutio. Ay, ay, a scratch, a scratch; marry, 'tis enough.
1660      Where is my page? Go, villain, fetch a surgeon.
1661      [Exit Page]

1662      Romeo. Courage, man; the hurt cannot be much.1600
1663      Mercutio. No, 'tis not so deep as a well, nor so wide as a
1664      church-door; but 'tis enough,'twill serve: ask for
1665      me to-morrow, and you shall find me a grave man. I
1666      am peppered, I warrant, for this world. A plague o'
1667      both your houses! 'Zounds, a dog, a rat, a mouse, a1605
1668      cat, to scratch a man to death! a braggart, a
1669      rogue, a villain, that fights by the book of
1670      arithmetic! Why the devil came you between us? I
1671      was hurt under your arm.
1672      Romeo. I thought all for the best.1610
1673      Mercutio. Help me into some house, Benvolio,
1674      Or I shall faint. A plague o' both your houses!
1675      They have made worms' meat of me: I have it,
1676      And soundly too: your houses!
1677      [Exeunt MERCUTIO and BENVOLIO]

1678      Romeo. This gentleman, the prince's near ally,
1679      My very friend, hath got his mortal hurt
1680      In my behalf; my reputation stain'd
1681      With Tybalt's slander,—Tybalt, that an hour
1682      Hath been my kinsman! O sweet Juliet,1620
1683      Thy beauty hath made me effeminate
1684      And in my temper soften'd valour's steel!
1685      [Re-enter BENVOLIO]

1686      Benvolio. O Romeo, Romeo, brave Mercutio's dead!
1687      That gallant spirit hath aspired the clouds,1625
1688      Which too untimely here did scorn the earth.
1689      Romeo. This day's black fate on more days doth depend;
1690      This but begins the woe, others must end.
1691      Benvolio. Here comes the furious Tybalt back again.
1692      Romeo. Alive, in triumph! and Mercutio slain!1630
1693      Away to heaven, respective lenity,
1694      And fire-eyed fury be my conduct now!
1695      [Re-enter TYBALT]
1696      Now, Tybalt, take the villain back again,
1697      That late thou gavest me; for Mercutio's soul1635
1698      Is but a little way above our heads,
1699      Staying for thine to keep him company:
1700      Either thou, or I, or both, must go with him.
1701      Tybalt. Thou, wretched boy, that didst consort him here,
1702      Shalt with him hence.1640
1703      Romeo. This shall determine that.
1704      [They fight; TYBALT falls]

1705      Benvolio. Romeo, away, be gone!
1706      The citizens are up, and Tybalt slain.
1707      Stand not amazed: the prince will doom thee death,1645
1708      If thou art taken: hence, be gone, away!
1709      Romeo. O, I am fortune's fool!
1710      Benvolio. Why dost thou stay?
1711      [Exit ROMEO]

1712      [Enter Citizens, &c]

1713      First Citizen. Which way ran he that kill'd Mercutio?
1714      Tybalt, that murderer, which way ran he?
1715      Benvolio. There lies that Tybalt.
1716      First Citizen. Up, sir, go with me;
1717      I charge thee in the princes name, obey.1655
1718      [Enter Prince, attended; MONTAGUE, CAPULET, their]
1719      Wives, and others]
1720      Prince Escalus. Where are the vile beginners of this fray?
1721      Benvolio. O noble prince, I can discover all
1722      The unlucky manage of this fatal brawl:1660
1723      There lies the man, slain by young Romeo,
1724      That slew thy kinsman, brave Mercutio.
1725      Lady Capulet. Tybalt, my cousin! O my brother's child!
1726      O prince! O cousin! husband! O, the blood is spilt
1727      O my dear kinsman! Prince, as thou art true,1665
1728      For blood of ours, shed blood of Montague.
1729      O cousin, cousin!
1730      Prince Escalus. Benvolio, who began this bloody fray?
1731      Benvolio. Tybalt, here slain, whom Romeo's hand did slay;
1732      Romeo that spoke him fair, bade him bethink1670
1733      How nice the quarrel was, and urged withal
1734      Your high displeasure: all this uttered
1735      With gentle breath, calm look, knees humbly bow'd,
1736      Could not take truce with the unruly spleen
1737      Of Tybalt deaf to peace, but that he tilts1675
1738      With piercing steel at bold Mercutio's breast,
1739      Who all as hot, turns deadly point to point,
1740      And, with a martial scorn, with one hand beats
1741      Cold death aside, and with the other sends
1742      It back to Tybalt, whose dexterity,1680
1743      Retorts it: Romeo he cries aloud,
1744      'Hold, friends! friends, part!' and, swifter than
1745      his tongue,
1746      His agile arm beats down their fatal points,
1747      And 'twixt them rushes; underneath whose arm1685
1748      An envious thrust from Tybalt hit the life
1749      Of stout Mercutio, and then Tybalt fled;
1750      But by and by comes back to Romeo,
1751      Who had but newly entertain'd revenge,
1752      And to 't they go like lightning, for, ere I1690
1753      Could draw to part them, was stout Tybalt slain.
1754      And, as he fell, did Romeo turn and fly.
1755      This is the truth, or let Benvolio die.
1756      Lady Capulet. He is a kinsman to the Montague;
1757      Affection makes him false; he speaks not true:1695
1758      Some twenty of them fought in this black strife,
1759      And all those twenty could but kill one life.
1760      I beg for justice, which thou, prince, must give;
1761      Romeo slew Tybalt, Romeo must not live.
1762      Prince Escalus. Romeo slew him, he slew Mercutio;1700
1763      Who now the price of his dear blood doth owe?
1764      Montague. Not Romeo, prince, he was Mercutio's friend;
1765      His fault concludes but what the law should end,
1766      The life of Tybalt.
1767      Prince Escalus. And for that offence1705
1768      Immediately we do exile him hence:
1769      I have an interest in your hate's proceeding,
1770      My blood for your rude brawls doth lie a-bleeding;
1771      But I'll amerce you with so strong a fine
1772      That you shall all repent the loss of mine:1710
1773      I will be deaf to pleading and excuses;
1774      Nor tears nor prayers shall purchase out abuses:
1775      Therefore use none: let Romeo hence in haste,
1776      Else, when he's found, that hour is his last.
1777      Bear hence this body and attend our will:1715
1778      Mercy but murders, pardoning those that kill.
1779      [Exeunt]

1780       	     	
1781      Act III, Scene 2

1782      Capulet’s orchard.

1783           	 
1784      [Enter JULIET]

1785      Juliet. Gallop apace, you fiery-footed steeds,
1786      Towards Phoebus' lodging: such a wagoner1720
1787      As Phaethon would whip you to the west,
1788      And bring in cloudy night immediately.
1789      Spread thy close curtain, love-performing night,
1790      That runaway's eyes may wink and Romeo
1791      Leap to these arms, untalk'd of and unseen.1725
1792      Lovers can see to do their amorous rites
1793      By their own beauties; or, if love be blind,
1794      It best agrees with night. Come, civil night,
1795      Thou sober-suited matron, all in black,
1796      And learn me how to lose a winning match,1730
1797      Play'd for a pair of stainless maidenhoods:
1798      Hood my unmann'd blood, bating in my cheeks,
1799      With thy black mantle; till strange love, grown bold,
1800      Think true love acted simple modesty.
1801      Come, night; come, Romeo; come, thou day in night;1735
1802      For thou wilt lie upon the wings of night
1803      Whiter than new snow on a raven's back.
1804      Come, gentle night, come, loving, black-brow'd night,
1805      Give me my Romeo; and, when he shall die,
1806      Take him and cut him out in little stars,1740
1807      And he will make the face of heaven so fine
1808      That all the world will be in love with night
1809      And pay no worship to the garish sun.
1810      O, I have bought the mansion of a love,
1811      But not possess'd it, and, though I am sold,1745
1812      Not yet enjoy'd: so tedious is this day
1813      As is the night before some festival
1814      To an impatient child that hath new robes
1815      And may not wear them. O, here comes my nurse,
1816      And she brings news; and every tongue that speaks1750
1817      But Romeo's name speaks heavenly eloquence.
1818      [Enter Nurse, with cords]
1819      Now, nurse, what news? What hast thou there? the cords
1820      That Romeo bid thee fetch?
1821      Nurse. Ay, ay, the cords.1755
1822      [Throws them down]

1823      Juliet. Ay me! what news? why dost thou wring thy hands?
1824      Nurse. Ah, well-a-day! he's dead, he's dead, he's dead!
1825      We are undone, lady, we are undone!
1826      Alack the day! he's gone, he's kill'd, he's dead!1760
1827      Juliet. Can heaven be so envious?
1828      Nurse. Romeo can,
1829      Though heaven cannot: O Romeo, Romeo!
1830      Who ever would have thought it? Romeo!
1831      Juliet. What devil art thou, that dost torment me thus?1765
1832      This torture should be roar'd in dismal hell.
1833      Hath Romeo slain himself? say thou but 'I,'
1834      And that bare vowel 'I' shall poison more
1835      Than the death-darting eye of cockatrice:
1836      I am not I, if there be such an I;1770
1837      Or those eyes shut, that make thee answer 'I.'
1838      If he be slain, say 'I'; or if not, no:
1839      Brief sounds determine of my weal or woe.
1840      Nurse. I saw the wound, I saw it with mine eyes,—
1841      God save the mark!—here on his manly breast:1775
1842      A piteous corse, a bloody piteous corse;
1843      Pale, pale as ashes, all bedaub'd in blood,
1844      All in gore-blood; I swounded at the sight.
1845      Juliet. O, break, my heart! poor bankrupt, break at once!
1846      To prison, eyes, ne'er look on liberty!1780
1847      Vile earth, to earth resign; end motion here;
1848      And thou and Romeo press one heavy bier!
1849      Nurse. O Tybalt, Tybalt, the best friend I had!
1850      O courteous Tybalt! honest gentleman!
1851      That ever I should live to see thee dead!1785
1852      Juliet. What storm is this that blows so contrary?
1853      Is Romeo slaughter'd, and is Tybalt dead?
1854      My dear-loved cousin, and my dearer lord?
1855      Then, dreadful trumpet, sound the general doom!
1856      For who is living, if those two are gone?1790
1857      Nurse. Tybalt is gone, and Romeo banished;
1858      Romeo that kill'd him, he is banished.
1859      Juliet. O God! did Romeo's hand shed Tybalt's blood?
1860      Nurse. It did, it did; alas the day, it did!
1861      Juliet. O serpent heart, hid with a flowering face!1795
1862      Did ever dragon keep so fair a cave?
1863      Beautiful tyrant! fiend angelical!
1864      Dove-feather'd raven! wolvish-ravening lamb!
1865      Despised substance of divinest show!
1866      Just opposite to what thou justly seem'st,1800
1867      A damned saint, an honourable villain!
1868      O nature, what hadst thou to do in hell,
1869      When thou didst bower the spirit of a fiend
1870      In moral paradise of such sweet flesh?
1871      Was ever book containing such vile matter1805
1872      So fairly bound? O that deceit should dwell
1873      In such a gorgeous palace!
1874      Nurse. There's no trust,
1875      No faith, no honesty in men; all perjured,
1876      All forsworn, all naught, all dissemblers.1810
1877      Ah, where's my man? give me some aqua vitae:
1878      These griefs, these woes, these sorrows make me old.
1879      Shame come to Romeo!
1880      Juliet. Blister'd be thy tongue
1881      For such a wish! he was not born to shame:1815
1882      Upon his brow shame is ashamed to sit;
1883      For 'tis a throne where honour may be crown'd
1884      Sole monarch of the universal earth.
1885      O, what a beast was I to chide at him!
1886      Nurse. Will you speak well of him that kill'd your cousin?1820
1887      Juliet. Shall I speak ill of him that is my husband?
1888      Ah, poor my lord, what tongue shall smooth thy name,
1889      When I, thy three-hours wife, have mangled it?
1890      But, wherefore, villain, didst thou kill my cousin?
1891      That villain cousin would have kill'd my husband:1825
1892      Back, foolish tears, back to your native spring;
1893      Your tributary drops belong to woe,
1894      Which you, mistaking, offer up to joy.
1895      My husband lives, that Tybalt would have slain;
1896      And Tybalt's dead, that would have slain my husband:1830
1897      All this is comfort; wherefore weep I then?
1898      Some word there was, worser than Tybalt's death,
1899      That murder'd me: I would forget it fain;
1900      But, O, it presses to my memory,
1901      Like damned guilty deeds to sinners' minds:1835
1902      'Tybalt is dead, and Romeo—banished;'
1903      That 'banished,' that one word 'banished,'
1904      Hath slain ten thousand Tybalts. Tybalt's death
1905      Was woe enough, if it had ended there:
1906      Or, if sour woe delights in fellowship1840
1907      And needly will be rank'd with other griefs,
1908      Why follow'd not, when she said 'Tybalt's dead,'
1909      Thy father, or thy mother, nay, or both,
1910      Which modern lamentations might have moved?
1911      But with a rear-ward following Tybalt's death,1845
1912      'Romeo is banished,' to speak that word,
1913      Is father, mother, Tybalt, Romeo, Juliet,
1914      All slain, all dead. 'Romeo is banished!'
1915      There is no end, no limit, measure, bound,
1916      In that word's death; no words can that woe sound.1850
1917      Where is my father, and my mother, nurse?
1918      Nurse. Weeping and wailing over Tybalt's corse:
1919      Will you go to them? I will bring you thither.
1920      Juliet. Wash they his wounds with tears: mine shall be spent,
1921      When theirs are dry, for Romeo's banishment.1855
1922      Take up those cords: poor ropes, you are beguiled,
1923      Both you and I; for Romeo is exiled:
1924      He made you for a highway to my bed;
1925      But I, a maid, die maiden-widowed.
1926      Come, cords, come, nurse; I'll to my wedding-bed;1860
1927      And death, not Romeo, take my maidenhead!
1928      Nurse. Hie to your chamber: I'll find Romeo
1929      To comfort you: I wot well where he is.
1930      Hark ye, your Romeo will be here at night:
1931      I'll to him; he is hid at Laurence' cell.1865
1932      Juliet. O, find him! give this ring to my true knight,
1933      And bid him come to take his last farewell.
1934      [Exeunt]

1935       	     	
1936      Act III, Scene 3

1937      Friar Laurence’s cell.

1938           	 
1939      [Enter FRIAR LAURENCE]

1940      Friar Laurence. Romeo, come forth; come forth, thou fearful man:1870
1941      Affliction is enamour'd of thy parts,
1942      And thou art wedded to calamity.
1943      [Enter ROMEO]

1944      Romeo. Father, what news? what is the prince's doom?
1945      What sorrow craves acquaintance at my hand,1875
1946      That I yet know not?
1947      Friar Laurence. Too familiar
1948      Is my dear son with such sour company:
1949      I bring thee tidings of the prince's doom.
1950      Romeo. What less than dooms-day is the prince's doom?1880
1951      Friar Laurence. A gentler judgment vanish'd from his lips,
1952      Not body's death, but body's banishment.
1953      Romeo. Ha, banishment! be merciful, say 'death;'
1954      For exile hath more terror in his look,
1955      Much more than death: do not say 'banishment.'1885
1956      Friar Laurence. Hence from Verona art thou banished:
1957      Be patient, for the world is broad and wide.
1958      Romeo. There is no world without Verona walls,
1959      But purgatory, torture, hell itself.
1960      Hence-banished is banish'd from the world,1890
1961      And world's exile is death: then banished,
1962      Is death mis-term'd: calling death banishment,
1963      Thou cutt'st my head off with a golden axe,
1964      And smilest upon the stroke that murders me.
1965      Friar Laurence. O deadly sin! O rude unthankfulness!1895
1966      Thy fault our law calls death; but the kind prince,
1967      Taking thy part, hath rush'd aside the law,
1968      And turn'd that black word death to banishment:
1969      This is dear mercy, and thou seest it not.
1970      Romeo. 'Tis torture, and not mercy: heaven is here,1900
1971      Where Juliet lives; and every cat and dog
1972      And little mouse, every unworthy thing,
1973      Live here in heaven and may look on her;
1974      But Romeo may not: more validity,
1975      More honourable state, more courtship lives1905
1976      In carrion-flies than Romeo: they my seize
1977      On the white wonder of dear Juliet's hand
1978      And steal immortal blessing from her lips,
1979      Who even in pure and vestal modesty,
1980      Still blush, as thinking their own kisses sin;1910
1981      But Romeo may not; he is banished:
1982      Flies may do this, but I from this must fly:
1983      They are free men, but I am banished.
1984      And say'st thou yet that exile is not death?
1985      Hadst thou no poison mix'd, no sharp-ground knife,1915
1986      No sudden mean of death, though ne'er so mean,
1987      But 'banished' to kill me?—'banished'?
1988      O friar, the damned use that word in hell;
1989      Howlings attend it: how hast thou the heart,
1990      Being a divine, a ghostly confessor,1920
1991      A sin-absolver, and my friend profess'd,
1992      To mangle me with that word 'banished'?
1993      Friar Laurence. Thou fond mad man, hear me but speak a word.
1994      Romeo. O, thou wilt speak again of banishment.
1995      Friar Laurence. I'll give thee armour to keep off that word:1925
1996      Adversity's sweet milk, philosophy,
1997      To comfort thee, though thou art banished.
1998      Romeo. Yet 'banished'? Hang up philosophy!
1999      Unless philosophy can make a Juliet,
2000      Displant a town, reverse a prince's doom,1930
2001      It helps not, it prevails not: talk no more.
2002      Friar Laurence. O, then I see that madmen have no ears.
2003      Romeo. How should they, when that wise men have no eyes?
2004      Friar Laurence. Let me dispute with thee of thy estate.
2005      Romeo. Thou canst not speak of that thou dost not feel:1935
2006      Wert thou as young as I, Juliet thy love,
2007      An hour but married, Tybalt murdered,
2008      Doting like me and like me banished,
2009      Then mightst thou speak, then mightst thou tear thy hair,
2010      And fall upon the ground, as I do now,1940
2011      Taking the measure of an unmade grave.
2012      [Knocking within]

2013      Friar Laurence. Arise; one knocks; good Romeo, hide thyself.
2014      Romeo. Not I; unless the breath of heartsick groans,
2015      Mist-like, infold me from the search of eyes.1945
2016      [Knocking]

2017      Friar Laurence. Hark, how they knock! Who's there? Romeo, arise;
2018      Thou wilt be taken. Stay awhile! Stand up;
2019      [Knocking]
2020      Run to my study. By and by! God's will,1950
2021      What simpleness is this! I come, I come!
2022      [Knocking]
2023      Who knocks so hard? whence come you? what's your will?
2024      Nurse. [Within] Let me come in, and you shall know
2025      my errand;1955
2026      I come from Lady Juliet.
2027      Friar Laurence. Welcome, then.
2028      [Enter Nurse]

2029      Nurse. O holy friar, O, tell me, holy friar,
2030      Where is my lady's lord, where's Romeo?1960
2031      Friar Laurence. There on the ground, with his own tears made drunk.
2032      Nurse. O, he is even in my mistress' case,
2033      Just in her case! O woful sympathy!
2034      Piteous predicament! Even so lies she,
2035      Blubbering and weeping, weeping and blubbering.1965
2036      Stand up, stand up; stand, and you be a man:
2037      For Juliet's sake, for her sake, rise and stand;
2038      Why should you fall into so deep an O?
2039      Romeo. Nurse!
2040      Nurse. Ah sir! ah sir! Well, death's the end of all.1970
2041      Romeo. Spakest thou of Juliet? how is it with her?
2042      Doth she not think me an old murderer,
2043      Now I have stain'd the childhood of our joy
2044      With blood removed but little from her own?
2045      Where is she? and how doth she? and what says1975
2046      My conceal'd lady to our cancell'd love?
2047      Nurse. O, she says nothing, sir, but weeps and weeps;
2048      And now falls on her bed; and then starts up,
2049      And Tybalt calls; and then on Romeo cries,
2050      And then down falls again.1980
2051      Romeo. As if that name,
2052      Shot from the deadly level of a gun,
2053      Did murder her; as that name's cursed hand
2054      Murder'd her kinsman. O, tell me, friar, tell me,
2055      In what vile part of this anatomy1985
2056      Doth my name lodge? tell me, that I may sack
2057      The hateful mansion.
2058      [Drawing his sword]

2059      Friar Laurence. Hold thy desperate hand:
2060      Art thou a man? thy form cries out thou art:1990
2061      Thy tears are womanish; thy wild acts denote
2062      The unreasonable fury of a beast:
2063      Unseemly woman in a seeming man!
2064      Or ill-beseeming beast in seeming both!
2065      Thou hast amazed me: by my holy order,1995
2066      I thought thy disposition better temper'd.
2067      Hast thou slain Tybalt? wilt thou slay thyself?
2068      And stay thy lady too that lives in thee,
2069      By doing damned hate upon thyself?
2070      Why rail'st thou on thy birth, the heaven, and earth?2000
2071      Since birth, and heaven, and earth, all three do meet
2072      In thee at once; which thou at once wouldst lose.
2073      Fie, fie, thou shamest thy shape, thy love, thy wit;
2074      Which, like a usurer, abound'st in all,
2075      And usest none in that true use indeed2005
2076      Which should bedeck thy shape, thy love, thy wit:
2077      Thy noble shape is but a form of wax,
2078      Digressing from the valour of a man;
2079      Thy dear love sworn but hollow perjury,
2080      Killing that love which thou hast vow'd to cherish;2010
2081      Thy wit, that ornament to shape and love,
2082      Misshapen in the conduct of them both,
2083      Like powder in a skitless soldier's flask,
2084      Is set afire by thine own ignorance,
2085      And thou dismember'd with thine own defence.2015
2086      What, rouse thee, man! thy Juliet is alive,
2087      For whose dear sake thou wast but lately dead;
2088      There art thou happy: Tybalt would kill thee,
2089      But thou slew'st Tybalt; there are thou happy too:
2090      The law that threaten'd death becomes thy friend2020
2091      And turns it to exile; there art thou happy:
2092      A pack of blessings lights up upon thy back;
2093      Happiness courts thee in her best array;
2094      But, like a misbehaved and sullen wench,
2095      Thou pout'st upon thy fortune and thy love:2025
2096      Take heed, take heed, for such die miserable.
2097      Go, get thee to thy love, as was decreed,
2098      Ascend her chamber, hence and comfort her:
2099      But look thou stay not till the watch be set,
2100      For then thou canst not pass to Mantua;2030
2101      Where thou shalt live, till we can find a time
2102      To blaze your marriage, reconcile your friends,
2103      Beg pardon of the prince, and call thee back
2104      With twenty hundred thousand times more joy
2105      Than thou went'st forth in lamentation.2035
2106      Go before, nurse: commend me to thy lady;
2107      And bid her hasten all the house to bed,
2108      Which heavy sorrow makes them apt unto:
2109      Romeo is coming.
2110      Nurse. O Lord, I could have stay'd here all the night2040
2111      To hear good counsel: O, what learning is!
2112      My lord, I'll tell my lady you will come.
2113      Romeo. Do so, and bid my sweet prepare to chide.
2114      Nurse. Here, sir, a ring she bid me give you, sir:
2115      Hie you, make haste, for it grows very late.2045
2116      [Exit]

2117      Romeo. How well my comfort is revived by this!
2118      Friar Laurence. Go hence; good night; and here stands all your state:
2119      Either be gone before the watch be set,
2120      Or by the break of day disguised from hence:2050
2121      Sojourn in Mantua; I'll find out your man,
2122      And he shall signify from time to time
2123      Every good hap to you that chances here:
2124      Give me thy hand; 'tis late: farewell; good night.
2125      Romeo. But that a joy past joy calls out on me,2055
2126      It were a grief, so brief to part with thee: Farewell.
2127      [Exeunt]

2129      Act III, Scene 4

2130      A room in Capulet’s house.

2131           	 
2132      [Enter CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, and PARIS]

2133      Capulet. Things have fall'n out, sir, so unluckily,
2134      That we have had no time to move our daughter:2060
2135      Look you, she loved her kinsman Tybalt dearly,
2136      And so did I:—Well, we were born to die.
2137      'Tis very late, she'll not come down to-night:
2138      I promise you, but for your company,
2139      I would have been a-bed an hour ago.2065
2140      Paris. These times of woe afford no time to woo.
2141      Madam, good night: commend me to your daughter.
2142      Lady Capulet. I will, and know her mind early to-morrow;
2143      To-night she is mew'd up to her heaviness.
2144      Capulet. Sir Paris, I will make a desperate tender2070
2145      Of my child's love: I think she will be ruled
2146      In all respects by me; nay, more, I doubt it not.
2147      Wife, go you to her ere you go to bed;
2148      Acquaint her here of my son Paris' love;
2149      And bid her, mark you me, on Wednesday next—2075
2150      But, soft! what day is this?
2151      Paris. Monday, my lord,
2152      Capulet. Monday! ha, ha! Well, Wednesday is too soon,
2153      O' Thursday let it be: o' Thursday, tell her,
2154      She shall be married to this noble earl.2080
2155      Will you be ready? do you like this haste?
2156      We'll keep no great ado,—a friend or two;
2157      For, hark you, Tybalt being slain so late,
2158      It may be thought we held him carelessly,
2159      Being our kinsman, if we revel much:2085
2160      Therefore we'll have some half a dozen friends,
2161      And there an end. But what say you to Thursday?
2162      Paris. My lord, I would that Thursday were to-morrow.
2163      Capulet. Well get you gone: o' Thursday be it, then.
2164      Go you to Juliet ere you go to bed,2090
2165      Prepare her, wife, against this wedding-day.
2166      Farewell, my lord. Light to my chamber, ho!
2167      Afore me! it is so very very late,
2168      That we may call it early by and by.
2169      Good night.2095

2172      Act III, Scene 5

2173      Capulet’s orchard.

2174           	 
2175      [Enter ROMEO and JULIET above, at the window]

2176      Juliet. Wilt thou be gone? it is not yet near day:
2177      It was the nightingale, and not the lark,
2178      That pierced the fearful hollow of thine ear;2100
2179      Nightly she sings on yon pomegranate-tree:
2180      Believe me, love, it was the nightingale.
2181      Romeo. It was the lark, the herald of the morn,
2182      No nightingale: look, love, what envious streaks
2183      Do lace the severing clouds in yonder east:2105
2184      Night's candles are burnt out, and jocund day
2185      Stands tiptoe on the misty mountain tops.
2186      I must be gone and live, or stay and die.
2187      Juliet. Yon light is not day-light, I know it, I:
2188      It is some meteor that the sun exhales,2110
2189      To be to thee this night a torch-bearer,
2190      And light thee on thy way to Mantua:
2191      Therefore stay yet; thou need'st not to be gone.
2192      Romeo. Let me be ta'en, let me be put to death;
2193      I am content, so thou wilt have it so.2115
2194      I'll say yon grey is not the morning's eye,
2195      'Tis but the pale reflex of Cynthia's brow;
2196      Nor that is not the lark, whose notes do beat
2197      The vaulty heaven so high above our heads:
2198      I have more care to stay than will to go:2120
2199      Come, death, and welcome! Juliet wills it so.
2200      How is't, my soul? let's talk; it is not day.
2201      Juliet. It is, it is: hie hence, be gone, away!
2202      It is the lark that sings so out of tune,
2203      Straining harsh discords and unpleasing sharps.2125
2204      Some say the lark makes sweet division;
2205      This doth not so, for she divideth us:
2206      Some say the lark and loathed toad change eyes,
2207      O, now I would they had changed voices too!
2208      Since arm from arm that voice doth us affray,2130
2209      Hunting thee hence with hunt's-up to the day,
2210      O, now be gone; more light and light it grows.
2211      Romeo. More light and light; more dark and dark our woes!
2212      [Enter Nurse, to the chamber]

2213      Nurse. Madam!2135
2214      Juliet. Nurse?
2215      Nurse. Your lady mother is coming to your chamber:
2216      The day is broke; be wary, look about.
2217      [Exit]

2218      Juliet. Then, window, let day in, and let life out.2140
2219      Romeo. Farewell, farewell! one kiss, and I'll descend.
2220      [He goeth down]

2221      Juliet. Art thou gone so? love, lord, ay, husband, friend!
2222      I must hear from thee every day in the hour,
2223      For in a minute there are many days:2145
2224      O, by this count I shall be much in years
2225      Ere I again behold my Romeo!
2226      Romeo. Farewell!
2227      I will omit no opportunity
2228      That may convey my greetings, love, to thee.2150
2229      Juliet. O think'st thou we shall ever meet again?
2230      Romeo. I doubt it not; and all these woes shall serve
2231      For sweet discourses in our time to come.
2232      Juliet. O God, I have an ill-divining soul!
2233      Methinks I see thee, now thou art below,2155
2234      As one dead in the bottom of a tomb:
2235      Either my eyesight fails, or thou look'st pale.
2236      Romeo. And trust me, love, in my eye so do you:
2237      Dry sorrow drinks our blood. Adieu, adieu!
2238      [Exit]

2239      Juliet. O fortune, fortune! all men call thee fickle:
2240      If thou art fickle, what dost thou with him.
2241      That is renown'd for faith? Be fickle, fortune;
2242      For then, I hope, thou wilt not keep him long,
2243      But send him back.2165
2244      Lady Capulet. [Within] Ho, daughter! are you up?
2245      Juliet. Who is't that calls? is it my lady mother?
2246      Is she not down so late, or up so early?
2247      What unaccustom'd cause procures her hither?
2248      [Enter LADY CAPULET]

2249      Lady Capulet. Why, how now, Juliet!
2250      Juliet. Madam, I am not well.
2251      Lady Capulet. Evermore weeping for your cousin's death?
2252      What, wilt thou wash him from his grave with tears?
2253      An if thou couldst, thou couldst not make him live;2175
2254      Therefore, have done: some grief shows much of love;
2255      But much of grief shows still some want of wit.
2256      Juliet. Yet let me weep for such a feeling loss.
2257      Lady Capulet. So shall you feel the loss, but not the friend
2258      Which you weep for.2180
2259      Juliet. Feeling so the loss,
2260      Cannot choose but ever weep the friend.
2261      Lady Capulet. Well, girl, thou weep'st not so much for his death,
2262      As that the villain lives which slaughter'd him.
2263      Juliet. What villain madam?2185
2264      Lady Capulet. That same villain, Romeo.
2265      Juliet. [Aside] Villain and he be many miles asunder.—
2266      God Pardon him! I do, with all my heart;
2267      And yet no man like he doth grieve my heart.
2268      Lady Capulet. That is, because the traitor murderer lives.2190
2269      Juliet. Ay, madam, from the reach of these my hands:
2270      Would none but I might venge my cousin's death!
2271      Lady Capulet. We will have vengeance for it, fear thou not:
2272      Then weep no more. I'll send to one in Mantua,
2273      Where that same banish'd runagate doth live,2195
2274      Shall give him such an unaccustom'd dram,
2275      That he shall soon keep Tybalt company:
2276      And then, I hope, thou wilt be satisfied.
2277      Juliet. Indeed, I never shall be satisfied
2278      With Romeo, till I behold him—dead—2200
2279      Is my poor heart for a kinsman vex'd.
2280      Madam, if you could find out but a man
2281      To bear a poison, I would temper it;
2282      That Romeo should, upon receipt thereof,
2283      Soon sleep in quiet. O, how my heart abhors2205
2284      To hear him named, and cannot come to him.
2285      To wreak the love I bore my cousin
2286      Upon his body that slaughter'd him!
2287      Lady Capulet. Find thou the means, and I'll find such a man.
2288      But now I'll tell thee joyful tidings, girl.2210
2289      Juliet. And joy comes well in such a needy time:
2290      What are they, I beseech your ladyship?
2291      Lady Capulet. Well, well, thou hast a careful father, child;
2292      One who, to put thee from thy heaviness,
2293      Hath sorted out a sudden day of joy,2215
2294      That thou expect'st not nor I look'd not for.
2295      Juliet. Madam, in happy time, what day is that?
2296      Lady Capulet. Marry, my child, early next Thursday morn,
2297      The gallant, young and noble gentleman,
2298      The County Paris, at Saint Peter's Church,2220
2299      Shall happily make thee there a joyful bride.
2300      Juliet. Now, by Saint Peter's Church and Peter too,
2301      He shall not make me there a joyful bride.
2302      I wonder at this haste; that I must wed
2303      Ere he, that should be husband, comes to woo.2225
2304      I pray you, tell my lord and father, madam,
2305      I will not marry yet; and, when I do, I swear,
2306      It shall be Romeo, whom you know I hate,
2307      Rather than Paris. These are news indeed!
2308      Lady Capulet. Here comes your father; tell him so yourself,2230
2309      And see how he will take it at your hands.
2310      [Enter CAPULET and Nurse]

2311      Capulet. When the sun sets, the air doth drizzle dew;
2312      But for the sunset of my brother's son
2313      It rains downright.2235
2314      How now! a conduit, girl? what, still in tears?
2315      Evermore showering? In one little body
2316      Thou counterfeit'st a bark, a sea, a wind;
2317      For still thy eyes, which I may call the sea,
2318      Do ebb and flow with tears; the bark thy body is,2240
2319      Sailing in this salt flood; the winds, thy sighs;
2320      Who, raging with thy tears, and they with them,
2321      Without a sudden calm, will overset
2322      Thy tempest-tossed body. How now, wife!
2323      Have you deliver'd to her our decree?2245
2324      Lady Capulet. Ay, sir; but she will none, she gives you thanks.
2325      I would the fool were married to her grave!
2326      Capulet. Soft! take me with you, take me with you, wife.
2327      How! will she none? doth she not give us thanks?
2328      Is she not proud? doth she not count her blest,2250
2329      Unworthy as she is, that we have wrought
2330      So worthy a gentleman to be her bridegroom?
2331      Juliet. Not proud, you have; but thankful, that you have:
2332      Proud can I never be of what I hate;
2333      But thankful even for hate, that is meant love.2255
2334      Capulet. How now, how now, chop-logic! What is this?
2335      'Proud,' and 'I thank you,' and 'I thank you not;'
2336      And yet 'not proud,' mistress minion, you,
2337      Thank me no thankings, nor, proud me no prouds,
2338      But fettle your fine joints 'gainst Thursday next,2260
2339      To go with Paris to Saint Peter's Church,
2340      Or I will drag thee on a hurdle thither.
2341      Out, you green-sickness carrion! out, you baggage!
2342      You tallow-face!
2343      Lady Capulet. Fie, fie! what, are you mad?2265
2344      Juliet. Good father, I beseech you on my knees,
2345      Hear me with patience but to speak a word.
2346      Capulet. Hang thee, young baggage! disobedient wretch!
2347      I tell thee what: get thee to church o' Thursday,
2348      Or never after look me in the face:2270
2349      Speak not, reply not, do not answer me;
2350      My fingers itch. Wife, we scarce thought us blest
2351      That God had lent us but this only child;
2352      But now I see this one is one too much,
2353      And that we have a curse in having her:2275
2354      Out on her, hilding!
2355      Nurse. God in heaven bless her!
2356      You are to blame, my lord, to rate her so.
2357      Capulet. And why, my lady wisdom? hold your tongue,
2358      Good prudence; smatter with your gossips, go.2280
2359      Nurse. I speak no treason.
2360      Capulet. O, God ye god-den.
2361      Nurse. May not one speak?
2362      Capulet. Peace, you mumbling fool!
2363      Utter your gravity o'er a gossip's bowl;2285
2364      For here we need it not.
2365      Lady Capulet. You are too hot.
2366      Capulet. God's bread! it makes me mad:
2367      Day, night, hour, tide, time, work, play,
2368      Alone, in company, still my care hath been2290
2369      To have her match'd: and having now provided
2370      A gentleman of noble parentage,
2371      Of fair demesnes, youthful, and nobly train'd,
2372      Stuff'd, as they say, with honourable parts,
2373      Proportion'd as one's thought would wish a man;2295
2374      And then to have a wretched puling fool,
2375      A whining mammet, in her fortune's tender,
2376      To answer 'I'll not wed; I cannot love,
2377      I am too young; I pray you, pardon me.'
2378      But, as you will not wed, I'll pardon you:2300
2379      Graze where you will you shall not house with me:
2380      Look to't, think on't, I do not use to jest.
2381      Thursday is near; lay hand on heart, advise:
2382      An you be mine, I'll give you to my friend;
2383      And you be not, hang, beg, starve, die in2305
2384      the streets,
2385      For, by my soul, I'll ne'er acknowledge thee,
2386      Nor what is mine shall never do thee good:
2387      Trust to't, bethink you; I'll not be forsworn.
2388      [Exit]

2389      Juliet. Is there no pity sitting in the clouds,
2390      That sees into the bottom of my grief?
2391      O, sweet my mother, cast me not away!
2392      Delay this marriage for a month, a week;
2393      Or, if you do not, make the bridal bed2315
2394      In that dim monument where Tybalt lies.
2395      Lady Capulet. Talk not to me, for I'll not speak a word:
2396      Do as thou wilt, for I have done with thee.
2397      [Exit]

2398      Juliet. O God!—O nurse, how shall this be prevented?2320
2399      My husband is on earth, my faith in heaven;
2400      How shall that faith return again to earth,
2401      Unless that husband send it me from heaven
2402      By leaving earth? comfort me, counsel me.
2403      Alack, alack, that heaven should practise stratagems2325
2404      Upon so soft a subject as myself!
2405      What say'st thou? hast thou not a word of joy?
2406      Some comfort, nurse.
2407      Nurse. Faith, here it is.
2408      Romeo is banish'd; and all the world to nothing,2330
2409      That he dares ne'er come back to challenge you;
2410      Or, if he do, it needs must be by stealth.
2411      Then, since the case so stands as now it doth,
2412      I think it best you married with the county.
2413      O, he's a lovely gentleman!2335
2414      Romeo's a dishclout to him: an eagle, madam,
2415      Hath not so green, so quick, so fair an eye
2416      As Paris hath. Beshrew my very heart,
2417      I think you are happy in this second match,
2418      For it excels your first: or if it did not,2340
2419      Your first is dead; or 'twere as good he were,
2420      As living here and you no use of him.
2421      Juliet. Speakest thou from thy heart?
2422      Nurse. And from my soul too;
2423      Or else beshrew them both.2345
2424      Juliet. Amen!
2425      Nurse. What?
2426      Juliet. Well, thou hast comforted me marvellous much.
2427      Go in: and tell my lady I am gone,
2428      Having displeased my father, to Laurence' cell,2350
2429      To make confession and to be absolved.
2430      Nurse. Marry, I will; and this is wisely done.
2431      [Exit]

2432      Juliet. Ancient damnation! O most wicked fiend!
2433      Is it more sin to wish me thus forsworn,2355
2434      Or to dispraise my lord with that same tongue
2435      Which she hath praised him with above compare
2436      So many thousand times? Go, counsellor;
2437      Thou and my bosom henceforth shall be twain.
2438      I'll to the friar, to know his remedy:2360
2439      If all else fail, myself have power to die.
2440      [Exit]

2442      Act IV, Scene 1

2443      Friar Laurence’s cell.

2444           	 
2445      [Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and PARIS]

2446      Friar Laurence. On Thursday, sir? the time is very short.
2447      Paris. My father Capulet will have it so;2365
2448      And I am nothing slow to slack his haste.
2449      Friar Laurence. You say you do not know the lady's mind:
2450      Uneven is the course, I like it not.
2451      Paris. Immoderately she weeps for Tybalt's death,
2452      And therefore have I little talk'd of love;2370
2453      For Venus smiles not in a house of tears.
2454      Now, sir, her father counts it dangerous
2455      That she doth give her sorrow so much sway,
2456      And in his wisdom hastes our marriage,
2457      To stop the inundation of her tears;2375
2458      Which, too much minded by herself alone,
2459      May be put from her by society:
2460      Now do you know the reason of this haste.
2461      Friar Laurence. [Aside] I would I knew not why it should be slow'd.
2462      Look, sir, here comes the lady towards my cell.2380
2463      [Enter JULIET]

2464      Paris. Happily met, my lady and my wife!
2465      Juliet. That may be, sir, when I may be a wife.
2466      Paris. That may be must be, love, on Thursday next.
2467      Juliet. What must be shall be.2385
2468      Friar Laurence. That's a certain text.
2469      Paris. Come you to make confession to this father?
2470      Juliet. To answer that, I should confess to you.
2471      Paris. Do not deny to him that you love me.
2472      Juliet. I will confess to you that I love him.2390
2473      Paris. So will ye, I am sure, that you love me.
2474      Juliet. If I do so, it will be of more price,
2475      Being spoke behind your back, than to your face.
2476      Paris. Poor soul, thy face is much abused with tears.
2477      Juliet. The tears have got small victory by that;2395
2478      For it was bad enough before their spite.
2479      Paris. Thou wrong'st it, more than tears, with that report.
2480      Juliet. That is no slander, sir, which is a truth;
2481      And what I spake, I spake it to my face.
2482      Paris. Thy face is mine, and thou hast slander'd it.2400
2483      Juliet. It may be so, for it is not mine own.
2484      Are you at leisure, holy father, now;
2485      Or shall I come to you at evening mass?
2486      Friar Laurence. My leisure serves me, pensive daughter, now.
2487      My lord, we must entreat the time alone.2405
2488      Paris. God shield I should disturb devotion!
2489      Juliet, on Thursday early will I rouse ye:
2490      Till then, adieu; and keep this holy kiss.
2491      [Exit]

2492      Juliet. O shut the door! and when thou hast done so,2410
2493      Come weep with me; past hope, past cure, past help!
2494      Friar Laurence. Ah, Juliet, I already know thy grief;
2495      It strains me past the compass of my wits:
2496      I hear thou must, and nothing may prorogue it,
2497      On Thursday next be married to this county.2415
2498      Juliet. Tell me not, friar, that thou hear'st of this,
2499      Unless thou tell me how I may prevent it:
2500      If, in thy wisdom, thou canst give no help,
2501      Do thou but call my resolution wise,
2502      And with this knife I'll help it presently.2420
2503      God join'd my heart and Romeo's, thou our hands;
2504      And ere this hand, by thee to Romeo seal'd,
2505      Shall be the label to another deed,
2506      Or my true heart with treacherous revolt
2507      Turn to another, this shall slay them both:2425
2508      Therefore, out of thy long-experienced time,
2509      Give me some present counsel, or, behold,
2510      'Twixt my extremes and me this bloody knife
2511      Shall play the umpire, arbitrating that
2512      Which the commission of thy years and art2430
2513      Could to no issue of true honour bring.
2514      Be not so long to speak; I long to die,
2515      If what thou speak'st speak not of remedy.
2516      Friar Laurence. Hold, daughter: I do spy a kind of hope,
2517      Which craves as desperate an execution.2435
2518      As that is desperate which we would prevent.
2519      If, rather than to marry County Paris,
2520      Thou hast the strength of will to slay thyself,
2521      Then is it likely thou wilt undertake
2522      A thing like death to chide away this shame,2440
2523      That copest with death himself to scape from it:
2524      And, if thou darest, I'll give thee remedy.
2525      Juliet. O, bid me leap, rather than marry Paris,
2526      From off the battlements of yonder tower;
2527      Or walk in thievish ways; or bid me lurk2445
2528      Where serpents are; chain me with roaring bears;
2529      Or shut me nightly in a charnel-house,
2530      O'er-cover'd quite with dead men's rattling bones,
2531      With reeky shanks and yellow chapless skulls;
2532      Or bid me go into a new-made grave2450
2533      And hide me with a dead man in his shroud;
2534      Things that, to hear them told, have made me tremble;
2535      And I will do it without fear or doubt,
2536      To live an unstain'd wife to my sweet love.
2537      Friar Laurence. Hold, then; go home, be merry, give consent2455
2538      To marry Paris: Wednesday is to-morrow:
2539      To-morrow night look that thou lie alone;
2540      Let not thy nurse lie with thee in thy chamber:
2541      Take thou this vial, being then in bed,
2542      And this distilled liquor drink thou off;2460
2543      When presently through all thy veins shall run
2544      A cold and drowsy humour, for no pulse
2545      Shall keep his native progress, but surcease:
2546      No warmth, no breath, shall testify thou livest;
2547      The roses in thy lips and cheeks shall fade2465
2548      To paly ashes, thy eyes' windows fall,
2549      Like death, when he shuts up the day of life;
2550      Each part, deprived of supple government,
2551      Shall, stiff and stark and cold, appear like death:
2552      And in this borrow'd likeness of shrunk death2470
2553      Thou shalt continue two and forty hours,
2554      And then awake as from a pleasant sleep.
2555      Now, when the bridegroom in the morning comes
2556      To rouse thee from thy bed, there art thou dead:
2557      Then, as the manner of our country is,2475
2558      In thy best robes uncover'd on the bier
2559      Thou shalt be borne to that same ancient vault
2560      Where all the kindred of the Capulets lie.
2561      In the mean time, against thou shalt awake,
2562      Shall Romeo by my letters know our drift,2480
2563      And hither shall he come: and he and I
2564      Will watch thy waking, and that very night
2565      Shall Romeo bear thee hence to Mantua.
2566      And this shall free thee from this present shame;
2567      If no inconstant toy, nor womanish fear,2485
2568      Abate thy valour in the acting it.
2569      Juliet. Give me, give me! O, tell not me of fear!
2570      Friar Laurence. Hold; get you gone, be strong and prosperous
2571      In this resolve: I'll send a friar with speed
2572      To Mantua, with my letters to thy lord.2490
2573      Juliet. Love give me strength! and strength shall help afford.
2574      Farewell, dear father!
2575      [Exeunt]

2576       	     	
2577      Act IV, Scene 2

2578      Hall in Capulet’s house.

2579           	 
2580      [Enter CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, Nurse, and two Servingmen]

2581      Capulet. So many guests invite as here are writ.2495
2582      [Exit First Servant]
2583      Sirrah, go hire me twenty cunning cooks.
2584      Second Servant. You shall have none ill, sir; for I'll try if they
2585      can lick their fingers.
2586      Capulet. How canst thou try them so?2500
2587      Second Servant. Marry, sir, 'tis an ill cook that cannot lick his
2588      own fingers: therefore he that cannot lick his
2589      fingers goes not with me.
2590      Capulet. Go, be gone.
2591      [Exit Second Servant]2505
2592      We shall be much unfurnished for this time.
2593      What, is my daughter gone to Friar Laurence?
2594      Nurse. Ay, forsooth.
2595      Capulet. Well, he may chance to do some good on her:
2596      A peevish self-will'd harlotry it is.2510
2597      Nurse. See where she comes from shrift with merry look.
2598      [Enter JULIET]

2599      Capulet. How now, my headstrong! where have you been gadding?
2600      Juliet. Where I have learn'd me to repent the sin
2601      Of disobedient opposition2515
2602      To you and your behests, and am enjoin'd
2603      By holy Laurence to fall prostrate here,
2604      And beg your pardon: pardon, I beseech you!
2605      Henceforward I am ever ruled by you.
2606      Capulet. Send for the county; go tell him of this:2520
2607      I'll have this knot knit up to-morrow morning.
2608      Juliet. I met the youthful lord at Laurence' cell;
2609      And gave him what becomed love I might,
2610      Not step o'er the bounds of modesty.
2611      Capulet. Why, I am glad on't; this is well: stand up:2525
2612      This is as't should be. Let me see the county;
2613      Ay, marry, go, I say, and fetch him hither.
2614      Now, afore God! this reverend holy friar,
2615      Our whole city is much bound to him.
2616      Juliet. Nurse, will you go with me into my closet,2530
2617      To help me sort such needful ornaments
2618      As you think fit to furnish me to-morrow?
2619      Lady Capulet. No, not till Thursday; there is time enough.
2620      Capulet. Go, nurse, go with her: we'll to church to-morrow.
2621      [Exeunt JULIET and Nurse]

2622      Lady Capulet. We shall be short in our provision:
2623      'Tis now near night.
2624      Capulet. Tush, I will stir about,
2625      And all things shall be well, I warrant thee, wife:
2626      Go thou to Juliet, help to deck up her;2540
2627      I'll not to bed to-night; let me alone;
2628      I'll play the housewife for this once. What, ho!
2629      They are all forth. Well, I will walk myself
2630      To County Paris, to prepare him up
2631      Against to-morrow: my heart is wondrous light,2545
2632      Since this same wayward girl is so reclaim'd.
2633      [Exeunt]

2634       	     	
2635      Act IV, Scene 3

2636      Juliet’s chamber.

2637           	 
2638      [Enter JULIET and Nurse]

2639      Juliet. Ay, those attires are best: but, gentle nurse,
2640      I pray thee, leave me to myself to-night,2550
2641      For I have need of many orisons
2642      To move the heavens to smile upon my state,
2643      Which, well thou know'st, is cross, and full of sin.
2644      [Enter LADY CAPULET]

2645      Lady Capulet. What, are you busy, ho? need you my help?2555
2646      Juliet. No, madam; we have cull'd such necessaries
2647      As are behoveful for our state to-morrow:
2648      So please you, let me now be left alone,
2649      And let the nurse this night sit up with you;
2650      For, I am sure, you have your hands full all,2560
2651      In this so sudden business.
2652      Lady Capulet. Good night:
2653      Get thee to bed, and rest; for thou hast need.
2654      [Exeunt LADY CAPULET and Nurse]

2655      Juliet. Farewell! God knows when we shall meet again.2565
2656      I have a faint cold fear thrills through my veins,
2657      That almost freezes up the heat of life:
2658      I'll call them back again to comfort me:
2659      Nurse! What should she do here?
2660      My dismal scene I needs must act alone.2570
2661      Come, vial.
2662      What if this mixture do not work at all?
2663      Shall I be married then to-morrow morning?
2664      No, no: this shall forbid it: lie thou there.
2665      [Laying down her dagger]2575
2666      What if it be a poison, which the friar
2667      Subtly hath minister'd to have me dead,
2668      Lest in this marriage he should be dishonour'd,
2669      Because he married me before to Romeo?
2670      I fear it is: and yet, methinks, it should not,2580
2671      For he hath still been tried a holy man.
2672      How if, when I am laid into the tomb,
2673      I wake before the time that Romeo
2674      Come to redeem me? there's a fearful point!
2675      Shall I not, then, be stifled in the vault,2585
2676      To whose foul mouth no healthsome air breathes in,
2677      And there die strangled ere my Romeo comes?
2678      Or, if I live, is it not very like,
2679      The horrible conceit of death and night,
2680      Together with the terror of the place,—2590
2681      As in a vault, an ancient receptacle,
2682      Where, for these many hundred years, the bones
2683      Of all my buried ancestors are packed:
2684      Where bloody Tybalt, yet but green in earth,
2685      Lies festering in his shroud; where, as they say,2595
2686      At some hours in the night spirits resort;—
2687      Alack, alack, is it not like that I,
2688      So early waking, what with loathsome smells,
2689      And shrieks like mandrakes' torn out of the earth,
2690      That living mortals, hearing them, run mad:—2600
2691      O, if I wake, shall I not be distraught,
2692      Environed with all these hideous fears?
2693      And madly play with my forefather's joints?
2694      And pluck the mangled Tybalt from his shroud?
2695      And, in this rage, with some great kinsman's bone,2605
2696      As with a club, dash out my desperate brains?
2697      O, look! methinks I see my cousin's ghost
2698      Seeking out Romeo, that did spit his body
2699      Upon a rapier's point: stay, Tybalt, stay!
2700      Romeo, I come! this do I drink to thee.2610
2701      [She falls upon her bed, within the curtains]

2703      Act IV, Scene 4

2704      Hall in Capulet’s house.

2705           	 
2706      [Enter LADY CAPULET and Nurse]

2707      Lady Capulet. Hold, take these keys, and fetch more spices, nurse.
2708      Nurse. They call for dates and quinces in the pastry.
2709      [Enter CAPULET]

2710      Capulet. Come, stir, stir, stir! the second cock hath crow'd,
2711      The curfew-bell hath rung, 'tis three o'clock:
2712      Look to the baked meats, good Angelica:
2713      Spare not for the cost.
2714      Nurse. Go, you cot-quean, go,2620
2715      Get you to bed; faith, You'll be sick to-morrow
2716      For this night's watching.
2717      Capulet. No, not a whit: what! I have watch'd ere now
2718      All night for lesser cause, and ne'er been sick.
2719      Lady Capulet. Ay, you have been a mouse-hunt in your time;2625
2720      But I will watch you from such watching now.
2721      [Exeunt LADY CAPULET and Nurse]

2722      Capulet. A jealous hood, a jealous hood!
2723      [Enter three or four Servingmen, with spits, logs, and baskets]

2724      Capulet. Now, fellow,2630
2725      What's there?
2726      First Servant. Things for the cook, sir; but I know not what.
2727      Capulet. Make haste, make haste.
2728      [Exit First Servant]
2729      Sirrah, fetch drier logs:2635
2730      Call Peter, he will show thee where they are.
2731      Second Servant. I have a head, sir, that will find out logs,
2732      And never trouble Peter for the matter.
2733      [Exit]

2734      Capulet. Mass, and well said; a merry whoreson, ha!2640
2735      Thou shalt be logger-head. Good faith, 'tis day:
2736      The county will be here with music straight,
2737      For so he said he would: I hear him near.

2739      Nurse! Wife! What, ho! What, nurse, I say!2645
2740      [Re-enter Nurse]

2742      I'll go and chat with Paris: hie, make haste,

2744      Make haste, I say.2650
