2747      Act IV, Scene 5

2748      Juliet’s chamber.

2749           	 
2750      [Enter Nurse]

2751      Nurse. Mistress! what, mistress! Juliet! fast, I warrant her, she:
2752      Why, lamb! why, lady! fie, you slug-a-bed!
2753      Why, love, I say! madam! sweet-heart! why, bride!2655
2754      What, not a word? you take your pennyworths now;
2755      Sleep for a week; for the next night, I warrant,
2756      The County Paris hath set up his rest,
2757      That you shall rest but little. God forgive me,
2758      Marry, and amen, how sound is she asleep!2660
2759      I must needs wake her. Madam, madam, madam!
2760      Ay, let the county take you in your bed;
2761      He'll fright you up, i' faith. Will it not be?
2762      [Undraws the curtains]
2763      What, dress'd! and in your clothes! and down again!2665
2764      I must needs wake you; Lady! lady! lady!
2765      Alas, alas! Help, help! my lady's dead!
2766      O, well-a-day, that ever I was born!
2767      Some aqua vitae, ho! My lord! my lady!
2768      [Enter LADY CAPULET]

2769      Lady Capulet. What noise is here?
2770      Nurse. O lamentable day!
2771      Lady Capulet. What is the matter?
2772      Nurse. Look, look! O heavy day!
2773      Lady Capulet. O me, O me! My child, my only life,2675
2774      Revive, look up, or I will die with thee!
2775      Help, help! Call help.
2776      [Enter CAPULET]

2777      Capulet. For shame, bring Juliet forth; her lord is come.
2778      Nurse. She's dead, deceased, she's dead; alack the day!2680
2779      Lady Capulet. Alack the day, she's dead, she's dead, she's dead!
2780      Capulet. Ha! let me see her: out, alas! she's cold:
2781      Her blood is settled, and her joints are stiff;
2782      Life and these lips have long been separated:
2783      Death lies on her like an untimely frost2685
2784      Upon the sweetest flower of all the field.
2785      Nurse. O lamentable day!
2786      Lady Capulet. O woful time!
2787      Capulet. Death, that hath ta'en her hence to make me wail,
2788      Ties up my tongue, and will not let me speak.2690
2789      [Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and PARIS, with Musicians]

2790      Friar Laurence. Come, is the bride ready to go to church?
2791      Capulet. Ready to go, but never to return.
2792      O son! the night before thy wedding-day
2793      Hath Death lain with thy wife. There she lies,2695
2794      Flower as she was, deflowered by him.
2795      Death is my son-in-law, Death is my heir;
2796      My daughter he hath wedded: I will die,
2797      And leave him all; life, living, all is Death's.
2798      Paris. Have I thought long to see this morning's face,2700
2799      And doth it give me such a sight as this?
2800      Lady Capulet. Accursed, unhappy, wretched, hateful day!
2801      Most miserable hour that e'er time saw
2802      In lasting labour of his pilgrimage!
2803      But one, poor one, one poor and loving child,2705
2804      But one thing to rejoice and solace in,
2805      And cruel death hath catch'd it from my sight!
2806      Nurse. O woe! O woful, woful, woful day!
2807      Most lamentable day, most woful day,
2808      That ever, ever, I did yet behold!2710
2809      O day! O day! O day! O hateful day!
2810      Never was seen so black a day as this:
2811      O woful day, O woful day!
2812      Paris. Beguiled, divorced, wronged, spited, slain!
2813      Most detestable death, by thee beguil'd,2715
2814      By cruel cruel thee quite overthrown!
2815      O love! O life! not life, but love in death!
2816      Capulet. Despised, distressed, hated, martyr'd, kill'd!
2817      Uncomfortable time, why camest thou now
2818      To murder, murder our solemnity?2720
2819      O child! O child! my soul, and not my child!
2820      Dead art thou! Alack! my child is dead;
2821      And with my child my joys are buried.
2822      Friar Laurence. Peace, ho, for shame! confusion's cure lives not
2823      In these confusions. Heaven and yourself2725
2824      Had part in this fair maid; now heaven hath all,
2825      And all the better is it for the maid:
2826      Your part in her you could not keep from death,
2827      But heaven keeps his part in eternal life.
2828      The most you sought was her promotion;2730
2829      For 'twas your heaven she should be advanced:
2830      And weep ye now, seeing she is advanced
2831      Above the clouds, as high as heaven itself?
2832      O, in this love, you love your child so ill,
2833      That you run mad, seeing that she is well:2735
2834      She's not well married that lives married long;
2835      But she's best married that dies married young.
2836      Dry up your tears, and stick your rosemary
2837      On this fair corse; and, as the custom is,
2838      In all her best array bear her to church:2740
2839      For though fond nature bids us an lament,
2840      Yet nature's tears are reason's merriment.
2841      Capulet. All things that we ordained festival,
2842      Turn from their office to black funeral;
2843      Our instruments to melancholy bells,2745
2844      Our wedding cheer to a sad burial feast,
2845      Our solemn hymns to sullen dirges change,
2846      Our bridal flowers serve for a buried corse,
2847      And all things change them to the contrary.
2848      Friar Laurence. Sir, go you in; and, madam, go with him;2750
2849      And go, Sir Paris; every one prepare
2850      To follow this fair corse unto her grave:
2851      The heavens do lour upon you for some ill;
2852      Move them no more by crossing their high will.
2853      [Exeunt CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, PARIS, and FRIAR LAURENCE]

2854      First Musician. Faith, we may put up our pipes, and be gone.
2855      Nurse. Honest goodfellows, ah, put up, put up;
2856      For, well you know, this is a pitiful case.
2857      [Exit]

2858      First Musician. Ay, by my troth, the case may be amended.2760
2859      [Enter PETER]

2860      Peter. Musicians, O, musicians, 'Heart's ease, Heart's
2861      ease:' O, an you will have me live, play 'Heart's ease.'
2862      First Musician. Why 'Heart's ease?'
2863      Peter. O, musicians, because my heart itself plays 'My2765
2864      heart is full of woe:' O, play me some merry dump,
2865      to comfort me.
2866      First Musician. Not a dump we; 'tis no time to play now.
2867      Peter. You will not, then?
2868      First Musician. No.2770
2869      Peter. I will then give it you soundly.
2870      First Musician. What will you give us?
2871      Peter. No money, on my faith, but the gleek;
2872      I will give you the minstrel.
2873      First Musician. Then I will give you the serving-creature.2775
2874      Peter. Then will I lay the serving-creature's dagger on
2875      your pate. I will carry no crotchets: I'll re you,
2876      I'll fa you; do you note me?
2877      First Musician. An you re us and fa us, you note us.
2878      Second Musician. Pray you, put up your dagger, and put out your wit.2780
2879      Peter. Then have at you with my wit! I will dry-beat you
2880      with an iron wit, and put up my iron dagger. Answer
2881      me like men:
2882      'When griping grief the heart doth wound,
2883      And doleful dumps the mind oppress,2785
2884      Then music with her silver sound'—
2885      why 'silver sound'? why 'music with her silver
2886      sound'? What say you, Simon Catling?
2887      First Musician. Marry, sir, because silver hath a sweet sound.
2888      Peter. Pretty! What say you, Hugh Rebeck?2790
2889      Second Musician. I say 'silver sound,' because musicians sound for silver.
2890      Peter. Pretty too! What say you, James Soundpost?
2891      Third Musician. Faith, I know not what to say.
2892      Peter. O, I cry you mercy; you are the singer: I will say
2893      for you. It is 'music with her silver sound,'2795
2894      because musicians have no gold for sounding:
2895      'Then music with her silver sound
2896      With speedy help doth lend redress.'
2897      [Exit]

2898      First Musician. What a pestilent knave is this same!2800
2899      Second Musician. Hang him, Jack! Come, we'll in here; tarry for the
2900      mourners, and stay dinner.
2901      [Exeunt]

2902       	     	
2903      Act V, Scene 1

2904      Mantua. A street.

2905           	 
2906      [Enter ROMEO]

2907      Romeo. If I may trust the flattering truth of sleep,2805
2908      My dreams presage some joyful news at hand:
2909      My bosom's lord sits lightly in his throne;
2910      And all this day an unaccustom'd spirit
2911      Lifts me above the ground with cheerful thoughts.
2912      I dreamt my lady came and found me dead—2810
2913      Strange dream, that gives a dead man leave
2914      to think!—
2915      And breathed such life with kisses in my lips,
2916      That I revived, and was an emperor.
2917      Ah me! how sweet is love itself possess'd,2815
2918      When but love's shadows are so rich in joy!
2919      [Enter BALTHASAR, booted]
2920      News from Verona!—How now, Balthasar!
2921      Dost thou not bring me letters from the friar?
2922      How doth my lady? Is my father well?2820
2923      How fares my Juliet? that I ask again;
2924      For nothing can be ill, if she be well.
2925      Balthasar. Then she is well, and nothing can be ill:
2926      Her body sleeps in Capel's monument,
2927      And her immortal part with angels lives.2825
2928      I saw her laid low in her kindred's vault,
2929      And presently took post to tell it you:
2930      O, pardon me for bringing these ill news,
2931      Since you did leave it for my office, sir.
2932      Romeo. Is it even so? then I defy you, stars!2830
2933      Thou know'st my lodging: get me ink and paper,
2934      And hire post-horses; I will hence to-night.
2935      Balthasar. I do beseech you, sir, have patience:
2936      Your looks are pale and wild, and do import
2937      Some misadventure.2835
2938      Romeo. Tush, thou art deceived:
2939      Leave me, and do the thing I bid thee do.
2940      Hast thou no letters to me from the friar?
2941      Balthasar. No, my good lord.
2942      Romeo. No matter: get thee gone,2840
2943      And hire those horses; I'll be with thee straight.
2944      [Exit BALTHASAR]
2945      Well, Juliet, I will lie with thee to-night.
2946      Let's see for means: O mischief, thou art swift
2947      To enter in the thoughts of desperate men!2845
2948      I do remember an apothecary,—
2949      And hereabouts he dwells,—which late I noted
2950      In tatter'd weeds, with overwhelming brows,
2951      Culling of simples; meagre were his looks,
2952      Sharp misery had worn him to the bones:2850
2953      And in his needy shop a tortoise hung,
2954      An alligator stuff'd, and other skins
2955      Of ill-shaped fishes; and about his shelves
2956      A beggarly account of empty boxes,
2957      Green earthen pots, bladders and musty seeds,2855
2958      Remnants of packthread and old cakes of roses,
2959      Were thinly scatter'd, to make up a show.
2960      Noting this penury, to myself I said
2961      'An if a man did need a poison now,
2962      Whose sale is present death in Mantua,2860
2963      Here lives a caitiff wretch would sell it him.'
2964      O, this same thought did but forerun my need;
2965      And this same needy man must sell it me.
2966      As I remember, this should be the house.
2967      Being holiday, the beggar's shop is shut.2865
2968      What, ho! apothecary!
2969      [Enter Apothecary]

2970      Apothecary. Who calls so loud?
2971      Romeo. Come hither, man. I see that thou art poor:
2972      Hold, there is forty ducats: let me have2870
2973      A dram of poison, such soon-speeding gear
2974      As will disperse itself through all the veins
2975      That the life-weary taker may fall dead
2976      And that the trunk may be discharged of breath
2977      As violently as hasty powder fired2875
2978      Doth hurry from the fatal cannon's womb.
2979      Apothecary. Such mortal drugs I have; but Mantua's law
2980      Is death to any he that utters them.
2981      Romeo. Art thou so bare and full of wretchedness,
2982      And fear'st to die? famine is in thy cheeks,2880
2983      Need and oppression starveth in thine eyes,
2984      Contempt and beggary hangs upon thy back;
2985      The world is not thy friend nor the world's law;
2986      The world affords no law to make thee rich;
2987      Then be not poor, but break it, and take this.2885
2988      Apothecary. My poverty, but not my will, consents.
2989      Romeo. I pay thy poverty, and not thy will.
2990      Apothecary. Put this in any liquid thing you will,
2991      And drink it off; and, if you had the strength
2992      Of twenty men, it would dispatch you straight.2890
2993      Romeo. There is thy gold, worse poison to men's souls,
2994      Doing more murders in this loathsome world,
2995      Than these poor compounds that thou mayst not sell.
2996      I sell thee poison; thou hast sold me none.
2997      Farewell: buy food, and get thyself in flesh.2895
2998      Come, cordial and not poison, go with me
2999      To Juliet's grave; for there must I use thee.
3000      [Exeunt]

3002      Act V, Scene 2

3003      Friar Laurence’s cell.

3004           	 
3005      [Enter FRIAR JOHN]

3006      Friar John. Holy Franciscan friar! brother, ho!2900
3007      [Enter FRIAR LAURENCE]

3008      Friar Laurence. This same should be the voice of Friar John.
3009      Welcome from Mantua: what says Romeo?
3010      Or, if his mind be writ, give me his letter.
3011      Friar John. Going to find a bare-foot brother out2905
3012      One of our order, to associate me,
3013      Here in this city visiting the sick,
3014      And finding him, the searchers of the town,
3015      Suspecting that we both were in a house
3016      Where the infectious pestilence did reign,2910
3017      Seal'd up the doors, and would not let us forth;
3018      So that my speed to Mantua there was stay'd.
3019      Friar Laurence. Who bare my letter, then, to Romeo?
3020      Friar John. I could not send it,—here it is again,—
3021      Nor get a messenger to bring it thee,2915
3022      So fearful were they of infection.
3023      Friar Laurence. Unhappy fortune! by my brotherhood,
3024      The letter was not nice but full of charge
3025      Of dear import, and the neglecting it
3026      May do much danger. Friar John, go hence;2920
3027      Get me an iron crow, and bring it straight
3028      Unto my cell.
3029      Friar John. Brother, I'll go and bring it thee.
3030      [Exit]

3031      Friar Laurence. Now must I to the monument alone;2925
3032      Within three hours will fair Juliet wake:
3033      She will beshrew me much that Romeo
3034      Hath had no notice of these accidents;
3035      But I will write again to Mantua,
3036      And keep her at my cell till Romeo come;2930
3037      Poor living corse, closed in a dead man's tomb!
3038      [Exit]

3040      Act V, Scene 3

3041      A churchyard; in it a tomb belonging to the Capulets.

3042           	 
3043      [Enter PARIS, and his Page bearing flowers and a torch]

3044      Paris. Give me thy torch, boy: hence, and stand aloof:
3045      Yet put it out, for I would not be seen.2935
3046      Under yond yew-trees lay thee all along,
3047      Holding thine ear close to the hollow ground;
3048      So shall no foot upon the churchyard tread,
3049      Being loose, unfirm, with digging up of graves,
3050      But thou shalt hear it: whistle then to me,2940
3051      As signal that thou hear'st something approach.
3052      Give me those flowers. Do as I bid thee, go.
3053      Page. [Aside] I am almost afraid to stand alone
3054      Here in the churchyard; yet I will adventure.
3055      [Retires]

3056      Paris. Sweet flower, with flowers thy bridal bed I strew,—
3057      O woe! thy canopy is dust and stones;—
3058      Which with sweet water nightly I will dew,
3059      Or, wanting that, with tears distill'd by moans:
3060      The obsequies that I for thee will keep2950
3061      Nightly shall be to strew thy grave and weep.
3062      [The Page whistles]
3063      The boy gives warning something doth approach.
3064      What cursed foot wanders this way to-night,
3065      To cross my obsequies and true love's rite?2955
3066      What with a torch! muffle me, night, awhile.
3067      [Retires]

3068      [Enter ROMEO and BALTHASAR, with a torch, mattock, &c]

3069      Romeo. Give me that mattock and the wrenching iron.
3070      Hold, take this letter; early in the morning2960
3071      See thou deliver it to my lord and father.
3072      Give me the light: upon thy life, I charge thee,
3073      Whate'er thou hear'st or seest, stand all aloof,
3074      And do not interrupt me in my course.
3075      Why I descend into this bed of death,2965
3076      Is partly to behold my lady's face;
3077      But chiefly to take thence from her dead finger
3078      A precious ring, a ring that I must use
3079      In dear employment: therefore hence, be gone:
3080      But if thou, jealous, dost return to pry2970
3081      In what I further shall intend to do,
3082      By heaven, I will tear thee joint by joint
3083      And strew this hungry churchyard with thy limbs:
3084      The time and my intents are savage-wild,
3085      More fierce and more inexorable far2975
3086      Than empty tigers or the roaring sea.
3087      Balthasar. I will be gone, sir, and not trouble you.
3088      Romeo. So shalt thou show me friendship. Take thou that:
3089      Live, and be prosperous: and farewell, good fellow.
3090      Balthasar. [Aside] For all this same, I'll hide me hereabout:2980
3091      His looks I fear, and his intents I doubt.
3092      [Retires]

3093      Romeo. Thou detestable maw, thou womb of death,
3094      Gorged with the dearest morsel of the earth,
3095      Thus I enforce thy rotten jaws to open,2985
3096      And, in despite, I'll cram thee with more food!
3097      [Opens the tomb]

3098      Paris. This is that banish'd haughty Montague,
3099      That murder'd my love's cousin, with which grief,
3100      It is supposed, the fair creature died;2990
3101      And here is come to do some villanous shame
3102      To the dead bodies: I will apprehend him.
3103      [Comes forward]
3104      Stop thy unhallow'd toil, vile Montague!
3105      Can vengeance be pursued further than death?2995
3106      Condemned villain, I do apprehend thee:
3107      Obey, and go with me; for thou must die.
3108      Romeo. I must indeed; and therefore came I hither.
3109      Good gentle youth, tempt not a desperate man;
3110      Fly hence, and leave me: think upon these gone;3000
3111      Let them affright thee. I beseech thee, youth,
3112      Put not another sin upon my head,
3113      By urging me to fury: O, be gone!
3114      By heaven, I love thee better than myself;
3115      For I come hither arm'd against myself:3005
3116      Stay not, be gone; live, and hereafter say,
3117      A madman's mercy bade thee run away.
3118      Paris. I do defy thy conjurations,
3119      And apprehend thee for a felon here.
3120      Romeo. Wilt thou provoke me? then have at thee, boy!3010
3121      [They fight]

3122      Page. O Lord, they fight! I will go call the watch.
3123      [Exit]

3124      Paris. O, I am slain!
3125      [Falls]3015
3126      If thou be merciful,
3127      Open the tomb, lay me with Juliet.
3128      [Dies]

3129      Romeo. In faith, I will. Let me peruse this face.
3130      Mercutio's kinsman, noble County Paris!3020
3131      What said my man, when my betossed soul
3132      Did not attend him as we rode? I think
3133      He told me Paris should have married Juliet:
3134      Said he not so? or did I dream it so?
3135      Or am I mad, hearing him talk of Juliet,3025
3136      To think it was so? O, give me thy hand,
3137      One writ with me in sour misfortune's book!
3138      I'll bury thee in a triumphant grave;
3139      A grave? O no! a lantern, slaughter'd youth,
3140      For here lies Juliet, and her beauty makes3030
3141      This vault a feasting presence full of light.
3142      Death, lie thou there, by a dead man interr'd.
3143      [Laying PARIS in the tomb]
3144      How oft when men are at the point of death
3145      Have they been merry! which their keepers call3035
3146      A lightning before death: O, how may I
3147      Call this a lightning? O my love! my wife!
3148      Death, that hath suck'd the honey of thy breath,
3149      Hath had no power yet upon thy beauty:
3150      Thou art not conquer'd; beauty's ensign yet3040
3151      Is crimson in thy lips and in thy cheeks,
3152      And death's pale flag is not advanced there.
3153      Tybalt, liest thou there in thy bloody sheet?
3154      O, what more favour can I do to thee,
3155      Than with that hand that cut thy youth in twain3045
3156      To sunder his that was thine enemy?
3157      Forgive me, cousin! Ah, dear Juliet,
3158      Why art thou yet so fair? shall I believe
3159      That unsubstantial death is amorous,
3160      And that the lean abhorred monster keeps3050
3161      Thee here in dark to be his paramour?
3162      For fear of that, I still will stay with thee;
3163      And never from this palace of dim night
3164      Depart again: here, here will I remain
3165      With worms that are thy chamber-maids; O, here3055
3166      Will I set up my everlasting rest,
3167      And shake the yoke of inauspicious stars
3168      From this world-wearied flesh. Eyes, look your last!
3169      Arms, take your last embrace! and, lips, O you
3170      The doors of breath, seal with a righteous kiss3060
3171      A dateless bargain to engrossing death!
3172      Come, bitter conduct, come, unsavoury guide!
3173      Thou desperate pilot, now at once run on
3174      The dashing rocks thy sea-sick weary bark!
3175      Here's to my love!3065
3176      [Drinks]
3177      O true apothecary!
3178      Thy drugs are quick. Thus with a kiss I die.
3179      [Dies]

3181      LAURENCE, with a lantern, crow, and spade]

3184      Balthasar. Here's one, a friend, and one that knows you well.
3185      Friar Laurence. Bliss be upon you! Tell me, good my friend,3075
3186      What torch is yond, that vainly lends his light

3188      It burneth in the Capel's monument.
3189      Balthasar. It doth so, holy sir; and there's my master,
3190      One that you love.3080
3191      Friar Laurence. Who is it?
3192      Balthasar. Romeo.
3193      Friar Laurence. How long hath he been there?
3194      Balthasar. Full half an hour.
3195      Friar Laurence. Go with me to the vault.3085
3196      Balthasar. I dare not, sir

3198      And fearfully did menace me with death,
3199      If I did stay to look on his intents.
3200      Friar Laurence. Stay, then; I'll go alone. Fear comes upon me:3090
3201      O, much I fear some ill unlucky thing.
3202      Balthasar. As I did sleep under this yew-tree here,

3204      And that my master slew him.
3205      Friar Laurence. Romeo!3095

3210      To lie discolour'd by this place of peace?3100
3211      [Enters the tomb]
3212      Romeo! O, pale! Who else? what, Paris too?

3214      Is guilty of this lamentable chance!
3215      The lady stirs.3105
3216      [JULIET wakes]

3217      Juliet. O comfortable friar! where is my lord?
3218      I do remember well where I should be,
3219      And there I am. Where is my Romeo?
3220      [Noise within]

3221      Friar Laurence. I hear some noise. Lady, come from that nest
3222      Of death, contagion, and unnatural sleep:
3223      A greater power than we can contradict
3224      Hath thwarted our intents. Come, come away.

3230      [Noise again]3120

3233      [Exit FRIAR LAURENCE]
3234      What's here? a cup, closed in my true love's hand?
3235      Poison, I see, hath been his timeless end:3125
3236      O churl! drunk all, and left no friendly drop
3237      To help me after? I will kiss thy lips;
3238      Haply some poison yet doth hang on them,
3239      To make die with a restorative.
3240      [Kisses him]3130
3241      Thy lips are warm.

3244      [Snatching ROMEO's dagger]

3246      [Stabs herself]
3247      there rust, and let me die.

3249      [Enter Watch, with the Page of PARIS]

3250      Page. This is the place; there, where the torch doth burn.3140
3251      First Watchman. The ground is bloody; search about the churchyard:
3252      Go, some of you, whoe'er you find attach.
3253      Pitiful sight! here lies the county slain,
3254      And Juliet bleeding, warm, and newly dead,
3255      Who here hath lain these two days buried.3145
3256      Go, tell the prince: run to the Capulets:
3257      Raise up the Montagues: some others search:
3258      We see the ground whereon these woes do lie;
3259      But the true ground of all these piteous woes
3260      We cannot without circumstance descry.3150
3261      [Re-enter some of the Watch, with BALTHASAR]

3262      Second Watchman. Here's Romeo's man; we found him in the churchyard.
3263      First Watchman. Hold him in safety, till the prince come hither.
3264      [Re-enter others of the Watch, with FRIAR LAURENCE]

3265      Third Watchman. Here is a friar, that trembles, sighs and weeps:3155
3266      We took this mattock and this spade from him,
3267      As he was coming from this churchyard side.
3268      First Watchman. A great suspicion: stay the friar too.
3269      [Enter the PRINCE and Attendants]

3270      Prince Escalus. What misadventure is so early up,3160
3271      That calls our person from our morning's rest?
3272      [Enter CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, and others]

3273      Capulet. What should it be, that they so shriek abroad?
3274      Lady Capulet. The people in the street cry Romeo,
3275      Some Juliet, and some Paris; and all run,3165
3276      With open outcry toward our monument.
3277      Prince Escalus. What fear is this which startles in our ears?
3278      First Watchman. Sovereign, here lies the County Paris slain;
3279      And Romeo dead; and Juliet, dead before,
3280      Warm and new kill'd.3170
3281      Prince Escalus. Search, seek, and know how this foul murder comes.
3282      First Watchman. Here is a friar, and slaughter'd Romeo's man;
3283      With instruments upon them, fit to open
3284      These dead men's tombs.
3285      Capulet. O heavens! O wife, look how our daughter bleeds!3175
3286      This dagger hath mista'en—for, lo, his house
3287      Is empty on the back of Montague,—
3288      And it mis-sheathed in my daughter's bosom!

3290      That warns my old age to a sepulchre.3180
3291      [Enter MONTAGUE and others]

3292      Prince Escalus. Come, Montague; for thou art early up,
3293      To see thy son and heir more early down.
3294      Montague. Alas, my liege, my wife is dead to-night;
3295      Grief of my son's exile hath stopp'd her breath:3185
3296      What further woe conspires against mine age?
3297      Prince Escalus. Look, and thou shalt see.
3298      Montague. O thou untaught! what manners is in this?
3299      To press before thy father to a grave?
3300      Prince Escalus. Seal up the mouth of outrage for a while,3190
3301      Till we can clear these ambiguities,
3302      And know their spring, their head, their
3303      true descent;
3304      And then will I be general of your woes,
3305      And lead you even to death: meantime forbear,3195
3306      And let mischance be slave to patience.
3307      Bring forth the parties of suspicion.
3308      Friar Laurence. I am the greatest, able to do least,
3309      Yet most suspected, as the time and place
3310      Doth make against me of this direful murder;3200
3311      And here I stand, both to impeach and purge
3312      Myself condemned and myself excused.
3313      Prince Escalus. Then say at once what thou dost know in this.
3314      Friar Laurence. I will be brief, for my short date of breath
3315      Is not so long as is a tedious tale.3205
3316      Romeo, there dead, was husband to that Juliet;
3317      And she, there dead, that Romeo's faithful wife:
3318      I married them; and their stol'n marriage-day
3319      Was Tybalt's dooms-day, whose untimely death
3320      Banish'd the new-made bridegroom from the city,3210
3321      For whom, and not for Tybalt, Juliet pined.
3322      You, to remove that siege of grief from her,
3323      Betroth'd and would have married her perforce
3324      To County Paris: then comes she to me,
3325      And, with wild looks, bid me devise some mean3215
3326      To rid her from this second marriage,
3327      Or in my cell there would she kill herself.
3328      Then gave I her, so tutor'd by my art,
3329      A sleeping potion; which so took effect
3330      As I intended, for it wrought on her3220
3331      The form of death: meantime I writ to Romeo,
3332      That he should hither come as this dire night,
3333      To help to take her from her borrow'd grave,
3334      Being the time the potion's force should cease.
3335      But he which bore my letter, Friar John,3225
3336      Was stay'd by accident, and yesternight
3337      Return'd my letter back. Then all alone
3338      At the prefixed hour of her waking,
3339      Came I to take her from her kindred's vault;
3340      Meaning to keep her closely at my cell,3230
3341      Till I conveniently could send to Romeo:
3342      But when I came, some minute ere the time
3343      Of her awaking, here untimely lay
3344      The noble Paris and true Romeo dead.
3345      She wakes; and I entreated her come forth,3235
3346      And bear this work of heaven with patience:
3347      But then a noise did scare me from the tomb;
3348      And she, too desperate, would not go with me,
3349      But, as it seems, did violence on herself.
3350      All this I know; and to the marriage3240
3351      Her nurse is privy: and, if aught in this
3352      Miscarried by my fault, let my old life
3353      Be sacrificed, some hour before his time,
3354      Unto the rigour of severest law.
3355      Prince Escalus. We still have known thee for a holy man.3245
3356      Where's Romeo's man? what can he say in this?
3357      Balthasar. I brought my master news of Juliet's death;
3358      And then in post he came from Mantua
3359      To this same place, to this same monument.
3360      This letter he early bid me give his father,3250
3361      And threatened me with death, going in the vault,
3362      I departed not and left him there.
3363      Prince Escalus. Give me the letter; I will look on it.
3364      Where is the county's page, that raised the watch?
3365      Sirrah, what made your master in this place?3255
3366      Page. He came with flowers to strew his lady's grave;
3367      And bid me stand aloof, and so I did:
3368      Anon comes one with light to ope the tomb;
3369      And by and by my master drew on him;
3370      And then I ran away to call the watch.3260
3371      Prince Escalus. This letter doth make good the friar's words,
3372      Their course of love, the tidings of her death:
3373      And here he writes that he did buy a poison
3374      Of a poor 'pothecary, and therewithal
3375      Came to this vault to die, and lie with Juliet.3265
3376      Where be these enemies? Capulet! Montague!
3377      See, what a scourge is laid upon your hate,
3378      That heaven finds means to kill your joys with love.
3379      And I for winking at your discords too
3380      Have lost a brace of kinsmen: all are punish'd.3270
3381      Capulet. O brother Montague, give me thy hand:
3382      This is my daughter's jointure, for no more
3383      Can I demand.
3384      Montague. But I can give thee more:
3385      For I will raise her statue in pure gold;3275
3386      That while Verona by that name is known,
3387      There shall no figure at such rate be set
3388      As that of true and faithful Juliet.
3389      Capulet. As rich shall Romeo's by his lady's lie;
3390      Poor sacrifices of our enmity!3280
3391      Prince Escalus. A glooming peace this morning with it brings;
3392      The sun, for sorrow, will not show his head:
3393      Go hence, to have more talk of these sad things;
3394      Some shall be pardon'd, and some punished:
3395      For never was a story of more woe3285
3396      Than this of Juliet and her Romeo.
3397      [Exeunt]
3398      Which, but their children's end, nought could remove,
3399      Is now the two hours' traffic of our stage;
3400      The which if you with patient ears attend,
3401      What here shall miss, our toil shall strive to mend.
3402      SCENE I. Verona. A public place.
3403      Enter SAMPSON and GREGORY, of the house of Capulet, armed with swords and bucklers
3404      SAMPSON
3405      Gregory, o' my word, we'll not carry coals.
3406      GREGORY
3407      No, for then we should be colliers.
3408      SAMPSON
3409      I mean, an we be in choler, we'll draw.
3410      GREGORY
3411      Ay, while you live, draw your neck out o' the collar.
3412      SAMPSON
3413      I strike quickly, being moved.
3414      GREGORY
3415      But thou art not quickly moved to strike.
3416      SAMPSON
3417      A dog of the house of Montague moves me.
3418      GREGORY
3419      To move is to stir; and to be valiant is to stand:
3420      therefore, if thou art moved, thou runn'st away.
3421      SAMPSON
3422      A dog of that house shall move me to stand: I will
3423      take the wall of any man or maid of Montague's.
3424      GREGORY
3425      That shows thee a weak slave; for the weakest goes
3426      to the wall.
3427      SAMPSON
3428      True; and therefore women, being the weaker vessels,
3429      are ever thrust to the wall: therefore I will push
3430      Montague's men from the wall, and thrust his maids
3431      to the wall.
3432      GREGORY
3433      The quarrel is between our masters and us their men.
3434      SAMPSON
3435      'Tis all one, I will show myself a tyrant: when I
3436      have fought with the men, I will be cruel with the
3437      maids, and cut off their heads.
3438      GREGORY
3439      The heads of the maids?
3440      SAMPSON
3441      Ay, the heads of the maids, or their maidenheads;
3442      take it in what sense thou wilt.
3443      GREGORY
3444      They must take it in sense that feel it.
3445      SAMPSON
3446      Me they shall feel while I am able to stand: and
3447      'tis known I am a pretty piece of flesh.
3448      GREGORY
3449      'Tis well thou art not fish; if thou hadst, thou
3450      hadst been poor John. Draw thy tool! here comes
3451      two of the house of the Montagues.
3452      SAMPSON
3453      My naked weapon is out: quarrel, I will back thee.
3454      GREGORY
3455      How! turn thy back and run?
3456      SAMPSON
3457      Fear me not.
3458      GREGORY
3459      No, marry; I fear thee!
3460      SAMPSON
3461      Let us take the law of our sides; let them begin.
3462      GREGORY
3463      I will frown as I pass by, and let them take it as
3464      they list.
3465      SAMPSON
3466      Nay, as they dare. I will bite my thumb at them;
3467      which is a disgrace to them, if they bear it.
3468      Enter ABRAHAM and BALTHASAR

3469      ABRAHAM
3470      Do you bite your thumb at us, sir?
3471      SAMPSON
3472      I do bite my thumb, sir.
3473      ABRAHAM
3474      Do you bite your thumb at us, sir?
3475      SAMPSON
3476      [Aside to GREGORY] Is the law of our side, if I say
3477      ay?
3478      GREGORY
3479      No.
3480      SAMPSON
3481      No, sir, I do not bite my thumb at you, sir, but I
3482      bite my thumb, sir.
3483      GREGORY
3484      Do you quarrel, sir?
3485      ABRAHAM
3486      Quarrel sir! no, sir.
3487      SAMPSON
3488      If you do, sir, I am for you: I serve as good a man as you.
3489      ABRAHAM
3490      No better.
3491      SAMPSON
3492      Well, sir.
3493      GREGORY
3494      Say 'better:' here comes one of my master's kinsmen.
3495      SAMPSON
3496      Yes, better, sir.
3497      ABRAHAM
3498      You lie.
3499      SAMPSON
3500      Draw, if you be men. Gregory, remember thy swashing blow.
3501      They fight

3502      Enter BENVOLIO

3503      BENVOLIO
3504      Part, fools!
3505      Put up your swords; you know not what you do.
3506      Beats down their swords

3507      Enter TYBALT

3508      TYBALT
3509      What, art thou drawn among these heartless hinds?
3510      Turn thee, Benvolio, look upon thy death.
3511      BENVOLIO
3512      I do but keep the peace: put up thy sword,
3513      Or manage it to part these men with me.
3514      TYBALT
3515      What, drawn, and talk of peace! I hate the word,
3516      As I hate hell, all Montagues, and thee:
3517      Have at thee, coward!
3518      They fight

3519      Enter, several of both houses, who join the fray; then enter Citizens, with clubs

3520      First Citizen
3521      Clubs, bills, and partisans! strike! beat them down!
3522      Down with the Capulets! down with the Montagues!
3523      Enter CAPULET in his gown, and LADY CAPULET

3524      CAPULET
3525      What noise is this? Give me my long sword, ho!
3526      LADY CAPULET
3527      A crutch, a crutch! why call you for a sword?
3528      CAPULET
3529      My sword, I say! Old Montague is come,
3530      And flourishes his blade in spite of me.
3531      Enter MONTAGUE and LADY MONTAGUE

3532      MONTAGUE
3533      Thou villain Capulet,--Hold me not, let me go.
3534      LADY MONTAGUE
3535      Thou shalt not stir a foot to seek a foe.
3536      Enter PRINCE, with Attendants

3537      PRINCE
3538      Rebellious subjects, enemies to peace,
3539      Profaners of this neighbour-stained steel,--
3540      Will they not hear? What, ho! you men, you beasts,
3541      That quench the fire of your pernicious rage
3542      With purple fountains issuing from your veins,
3543      On pain of torture, from those bloody hands
3544      Throw your mistemper'd weapons to the ground,
3545      And hear the sentence of your moved prince.
3546      Three civil brawls, bred of an airy word,
3547      By thee, old Capulet, and Montague,
3548      Have thrice disturb'd the quiet of our streets,
3549      And made Verona's ancient citizens
3550      Cast by their grave beseeming ornaments,
3551      To wield old partisans, in hands as old,
3552      Canker'd with peace, to part your canker'd hate:
3553      If ever you disturb our streets again,
3554      Your lives shall pay the forfeit of the peace.
3555      For this time, all the rest depart away:
3556      You Capulet; shall go along with me:
3557      And, Montague, come you this afternoon,
3558      To know our further pleasure in this case,
3559      To old Free-town, our common judgment-place.
3560      Once more, on pain of death, all men depart.
3561      Exeunt all but MONTAGUE, LADY MONTAGUE, and BENVOLIO

3562      MONTAGUE
3563      Who set this ancient quarrel new abroach?
3564      Speak, nephew, were you by when it began?
3565      BENVOLIO
3566      Here were the servants of your adversary,
3567      And yours, close fighting ere I did approach:
3568      I drew to part them: in the instant came
3569      The fiery Tybalt, with his sword prepared,
3570      Which, as he breathed defiance to my ears,
3571      He swung about his head and cut the winds,
3572      Who nothing hurt withal hiss'd him in scorn:
3573      While we were interchanging thrusts and blows,
3574      Came more and more and fought on part and part,
3575      Till the prince came, who parted either part.
3576      LADY MONTAGUE
3577      O, where is Romeo? saw you him to-day?
3578      Right glad I am he was not at this fray.
3579      BENVOLIO
3580      Madam, an hour before the worshipp'd sun
3581      Peer'd forth the golden window of the east,
3582      A troubled mind drave me to walk abroad;
3583      Where, underneath the grove of sycamore
3584      That westward rooteth from the city's side,
3585      So early walking did I see your son:
3586      Towards him I made, but he was ware of me
3587      And stole into the covert of the wood:
3588      I, measuring his affections by my own,
3589      That most are busied when they're most alone,
3590      Pursued my humour not pursuing his,
3591      And gladly shunn'd who gladly fled from me.
3592      MONTAGUE
3593      Many a morning hath he there been seen,
3594      With tears augmenting the fresh morning dew.
3595      Adding to clouds more clouds with his deep sighs;
3596      But all so soon as the all-cheering sun
3597      Should in the furthest east begin to draw
3598      The shady curtains from Aurora's bed,
3599      Away from the light steals home my heavy son,
3600      And private in his chamber pens himself,
3601      Shuts up his windows, locks far daylight out
3602      And makes himself an artificial night:
3603      Black and portentous must this humour prove,
3604      Unless good counsel may the cause remove.
3605      BENVOLIO
3606      My noble uncle, do you know the cause?
3607      MONTAGUE
3608      I neither know it nor can learn of him.
3609      BENVOLIO
3610      Have you importuned him by any means?
3611      MONTAGUE
3612      Both by myself and many other friends:
3613      But he, his own affections' counsellor,
3614      Is to himself--I will not say how true--
3615      But to himself so secret and so close,
3616      So far from sounding and discovery,
3617      As is the bud bit with an envious worm,
3618      Ere he can spread his sweet leaves to the air,
3619      Or dedicate his beauty to the sun.
3620      Could we but learn from whence his sorrows grow.
3621      We would as willingly give cure as know.
3622      Enter ROMEO

3623      BENVOLIO
3624      See, where he comes: so please you, step aside;
3625      I'll know his grievance, or be much denied.
3626      MONTAGUE
3627      I would thou wert so happy by thy stay,
3628      To hear true shrift. Come, madam, let's away.
3629      Exeunt MONTAGUE and LADY MONTAGUE

3630      BENVOLIO
3631      Good-morrow, cousin.
3632      ROMEO
3633      Is the day so young?
3634      BENVOLIO
3635      But new struck nine.
3636      ROMEO
3637      Ay me! sad hours seem long.
3638      Was that my father that went hence so fast?
3639      BENVOLIO
3640      It was. What sadness lengthens Romeo's hours?
3641      ROMEO
3642      Not having that, which, having, makes them short.
3643      BENVOLIO
3644      In love?
3645      ROMEO
3646      Out--
3647      BENVOLIO
3648      Of love?
3649      ROMEO
3650      Out of her favour, where I am in love.
3651      BENVOLIO
3652      Alas, that love, so gentle in his view,
3653      Should be so tyrannous and rough in proof!
3654      ROMEO
3655      Alas, that love, whose view is muffled still,
3656      Should, without eyes, see pathways to his will!
3657      Where shall we dine? O me! What fray was here?
3658      Yet tell me not, for I have heard it all.
3659      Here's much to do with hate, but more with love.
3660      Why, then, O brawling love! O loving hate!
3661      O any thing, of nothing first create!
3662      O heavy lightness! serious vanity!
3663      Mis-shapen chaos of well-seeming forms!
3664      Feather of lead, bright smoke, cold fire,
3665      sick health!
3666      Still-waking sleep, that is not what it is!
3667      This love feel I, that feel no love in this.
3668      Dost thou not laugh?
3669      BENVOLIO
3670      No, coz, I rather weep.
3671      ROMEO
3672      Good heart, at what?
3673      BENVOLIO
3674      At thy good heart's oppression.
3675      ROMEO
3676      Why, such is love's transgression.
3677      Griefs of mine own lie heavy in my breast,
3678      Which thou wilt propagate, to have it prest
3679      With more of thine: this love that thou hast shown
3680      Doth add more grief to too much of mine own.
3681      Love is a smoke raised with the fume of sighs;
3682      Being purged, a fire sparkling in lovers' eyes;
3683      Being vex'd a sea nourish'd with lovers' tears:
3684      What is it else? a madness most discreet,
3685      A choking gall and a preserving sweet.
3686      Farewell, my coz.
3687      BENVOLIO
3688      Soft! I will go along;
3689      An if you leave me so, you do me wrong.
3690      ROMEO
3691      Tut, I have lost myself; I am not here;
3692      This is not Romeo, he's some other where.
3693      BENVOLIO
3694      Tell me in sadness, who is that you love.
3695      ROMEO
3696      What, shall I groan and tell thee?
3697      BENVOLIO
3698      Groan! why, no.
3699      But sadly tell me who.
3700      ROMEO
3701      Bid a sick man in sadness make his will:
3702      Ah, word ill urged to one that is so ill!
3703      In sadness, cousin, I do love a woman.
3704      BENVOLIO
3705      I aim'd so near, when I supposed you loved.
3706      ROMEO
3707      A right good mark-man! And she's fair I love.
3708      BENVOLIO
3709      A right fair mark, fair coz, is soonest hit.
3710      ROMEO
3711      Well, in that hit you miss: she'll not be hit
3712      With Cupid's arrow; she hath Dian's wit;
3713      And, in strong proof of chastity well arm'd,
3714      From love's weak childish bow she lives unharm'd.
3715      She will not stay the siege of loving terms,
3716      Nor bide the encounter of assailing eyes,
3717      Nor ope her lap to saint-seducing gold:
3718      O, she is rich in beauty, only poor,
3719      That when she dies with beauty dies her store.
3720      BENVOLIO
3721      Then she hath sworn that she will still live chaste?
3722      ROMEO
3723      She hath, and in that sparing makes huge waste,
3724      For beauty starved with her severity
3725      Cuts beauty off from all posterity.
3726      She is too fair, too wise, wisely too fair,
3727      To merit bliss by making me despair:
3728      She hath forsworn to love, and in that vow
3729      Do I live dead that live to tell it now.
3730      BENVOLIO
3731      Be ruled by me, forget to think of her.
3732      ROMEO
3733      O, teach me how I should forget to think.
3734      BENVOLIO
3735      By giving liberty unto thine eyes;
3736      Examine other beauties.
3737      ROMEO
3738      'Tis the way
3739      To call hers exquisite, in question more:
3740      These happy masks that kiss fair ladies' brows
3741      Being black put us in mind they hide the fair;
3742      He that is strucken blind cannot forget
3743      The precious treasure of his eyesight lost:
3744      Show me a mistress that is passing fair,
3745      What doth her beauty serve, but as a note
3746      Where I may read who pass'd that passing fair?
3747      Farewell: thou canst not teach me to forget.
3748      BENVOLIO
3749      I'll pay that doctrine, or else die in debt.
3750      Exeunt

3751      SCENE II. A street.
3752      Enter CAPULET, PARIS, and Servant
3753      CAPULET
3754      But Montague is bound as well as I,
3755      In penalty alike; and 'tis not hard, I think,
3756      For men so old as we to keep the peace.
3757      PARIS
3758      Of honourable reckoning are you both;
3759      And pity 'tis you lived at odds so long.
3760      But now, my lord, what say you to my suit?
3761      CAPULET
3762      But saying o'er what I have said before:
3763      My child is yet a stranger in the world;
3764      She hath not seen the change of fourteen years,
3765      Let two more summers wither in their pride,
3766      Ere we may think her ripe to be a bride.
3767      PARIS
3768      Younger than she are happy mothers made.
3769      CAPULET
3770      And too soon marr'd are those so early made.
3771      The earth hath swallow'd all my hopes but she,
3772      She is the hopeful lady of my earth:
3773      But woo her, gentle Paris, get her heart,
3774      My will to her consent is but a part;
3775      An she agree, within her scope of choice
3776      Lies my consent and fair according voice.
3777      This night I hold an old accustom'd feast,
3778      Whereto I have invited many a guest,
3779      Such as I love; and you, among the store,
3780      One more, most welcome, makes my number more.
3781      At my poor house look to behold this night
3782      Earth-treading stars that make dark heaven light:
3783      Such comfort as do lusty young men feel
3784      When well-apparell'd April on the heel
3785      Of limping winter treads, even such delight
3786      Among fresh female buds shall you this night
3787      Inherit at my house; hear all, all see,
3788      And like her most whose merit most shall be:
3789      Which on more view, of many mine being one
3790      May stand in number, though in reckoning none,
3791      Come, go with me.
3792      To Servant, giving a paper

3793      Go, sirrah, trudge about
3794      Through fair Verona; find those persons out
3795      Whose names are written there, and to them say,
3796      My house and welcome on their pleasure stay.
3797      Exeunt CAPULET and PARIS

3798      Servant
3799      Find them out whose names are written here! It is
3800      written, that the shoemaker should meddle with his
3801      yard, and the tailor with his last, the fisher with
3802      his pencil, and the painter with his nets; but I am
3803      sent to find those persons whose names are here
3804      writ, and can never find what names the writing
3805      person hath here writ. I must to the learned.--In good time.
3806      Enter BENVOLIO and ROMEO

3807      BENVOLIO
3808      Tut, man, one fire burns out another's burning,
3809      One pain is lessen'd by another's anguish;
3810      Turn giddy, and be holp by backward turning;
3811      One desperate grief cures with another's languish:
3812      Take thou some new infection to thy eye,
3813      And the rank poison of the old will die.
3814      ROMEO
3815      Your plaintain-leaf is excellent for that.
3816      BENVOLIO
3817      For what, I pray thee?
3818      ROMEO
3819      For your broken shin.
3820      BENVOLIO
3821      Why, Romeo, art thou mad?
3822      ROMEO
3823      Not mad, but bound more than a mad-man is;
3824      Shut up in prison, kept without my food,
3825      Whipp'd and tormented and--God-den, good fellow.
3826      Servant
3827      God gi' god-den. I pray, sir, can you read?
3828      ROMEO
3829      Ay, mine own fortune in my misery.
3830      Servant
3831      Perhaps you have learned it without book: but, I
3832      pray, can you read any thing you see?
3833      ROMEO
3834      Ay, if I know the letters and the language.
3835      Servant
3836      Ye say honestly: rest you merry!
3837      ROMEO
3838      Stay, fellow; I can read.
3839      Reads

3840      'Signior Martino and his wife and daughters;
3841      County Anselme and his beauteous sisters; the lady
3842      widow of Vitravio; Signior Placentio and his lovely
3843      nieces; Mercutio and his brother Valentine; mine
3844      uncle Capulet, his wife and daughters; my fair niece
3845      Rosaline; Livia; Signior Valentio and his cousin
3846      Tybalt, Lucio and the lively Helena.' A fair
3847      assembly: whither should they come?
3848      Servant
3849      Up.
3850      ROMEO
3851      Whither?
3852      Servant
3853      To supper; to our house.
3854      ROMEO
3855      Whose house?
3856      Servant
3857      My master's.
3858      ROMEO
3859      Indeed, I should have ask'd you that before.
3860      Servant
3861      Now I'll tell you without asking: my master is the
3862      great rich Capulet; and if you be not of the house
3863      of Montagues, I pray, come and crush a cup of wine.
3864      Rest you merry!
3865      Exit

3866      BENVOLIO
3867      At this same ancient feast of Capulet's
3868      Sups the fair Rosaline whom thou so lovest,
3869      With all the admired beauties of Verona:
3870      Go thither; and, with unattainted eye,
3871      Compare her face with some that I shall show,
3872      And I will make thee think thy swan a crow.
3873      ROMEO
3874      When the devout religion of mine eye
3875      Maintains such falsehood, then turn tears to fires;
3876      And these, who often drown'd could never die,
3877      Transparent heretics, be burnt for liars!
3878      One fairer than my love! the all-seeing sun
3879      Ne'er saw her match since first the world begun.
3880      BENVOLIO
3881      Tut, you saw her fair, none else being by,
3882      Herself poised with herself in either eye:
3883      But in that crystal scales let there be weigh'd
3884      Your lady's love against some other maid
3885      That I will show you shining at this feast,
3886      And she shall scant show well that now shows best.
3887      ROMEO
3888      I'll go along, no such sight to be shown,
3889      But to rejoice in splendor of mine own.
3890      Exeunt

3891      SCENE III. A room in Capulet's house.
3892      Enter LADY CAPULET and Nurse
3893      LADY CAPULET
3894      Nurse, where's my daughter? call her forth to me.
3895      Nurse
3896      Now, by my maidenhead, at twelve year old,
3897      I bade her come. What, lamb! what, ladybird!
3898      God forbid! Where's this girl? What, Juliet!
3899      Enter JULIET

3900      JULIET
3901      How now! who calls?
3902      Nurse
3903      Your mother.
3904      JULIET
3905      Madam, I am here.
3906      What is your will?
3907      LADY CAPULET
3908      This is the matter:--Nurse, give leave awhile,
3909      We must talk in secret:--nurse, come back again;
3910      I have remember'd me, thou's hear our counsel.
3911      Thou know'st my daughter's of a pretty age.
3912      Nurse
3913      Faith, I can tell her age unto an hour.
3914      LADY CAPULET
3915      She's not fourteen.
3916      Nurse
3917      I'll lay fourteen of my teeth,--
3918      And yet, to my teeth be it spoken, I have but four--
3919      She is not fourteen. How long is it now
3920      To Lammas-tide?
3921      LADY CAPULET
3922      A fortnight and odd days.
3923      Nurse
3924      Even or odd, of all days in the year,
3925      Come Lammas-eve at night shall she be fourteen.
3926      Susan and she--God rest all Christian souls!--
3927      Were of an age: well, Susan is with God;
3928      She was too good for me: but, as I said,
3929      On Lammas-eve at night shall she be fourteen;
3930      That shall she, marry; I remember it well.
3931      'Tis since the earthquake now eleven years;
3932      And she was wean'd,--I never shall forget it,--
3933      Of all the days of the year, upon that day:
3934      For I had then laid wormwood to my dug,
3935      Sitting in the sun under the dove-house wall;
3936      My lord and you were then at Mantua:--
3937      Nay, I do bear a brain:--but, as I said,
3938      When it did taste the wormwood on the nipple
3939      Of my dug and felt it bitter, pretty fool,
3940      To see it tetchy and fall out with the dug!
3941      Shake quoth the dove-house: 'twas no need, I trow,
3942      To bid me trudge:
3943      And since that time it is eleven years;
3944      For then she could stand alone; nay, by the rood,
3945      She could have run and waddled all about;
3946      For even the day before, she broke her brow:
3947      And then my husband--God be with his soul!
3948      A' was a merry man--took up the child:
3949      'Yea,' quoth he, 'dost thou fall upon thy face?
3950      Thou wilt fall backward when thou hast more wit;
3951      Wilt thou not, Jule?' and, by my holidame,
3952      The pretty wretch left crying and said 'Ay.'
3953      To see, now, how a jest shall come about!
3954      I warrant, an I should live a thousand years,
3955      I never should forget it: 'Wilt thou not, Jule?' quoth he;
3956      And, pretty fool, it stinted and said 'Ay.'
3957      LADY CAPULET
3958      Enough of this; I pray thee, hold thy peace.
3959      Nurse
3960      Yes, madam: yet I cannot choose but laugh,
3961      To think it should leave crying and say 'Ay.'
3962      And yet, I warrant, it had upon its brow
3963      A bump as big as a young cockerel's stone;
3964      A parlous knock; and it cried bitterly:
3965      'Yea,' quoth my husband,'fall'st upon thy face?
3966      Thou wilt fall backward when thou comest to age;
3967      Wilt thou not, Jule?' it stinted and said 'Ay.'
3968      JULIET
3969      And stint thou too, I pray thee, nurse, say I.
3970      Nurse
3971      Peace, I have done. God mark thee to his grace!
3972      Thou wast the prettiest babe that e'er I nursed:
3973      An I might live to see thee married once,
3974      I have my wish.
3975      LADY CAPULET
3976      Marry, that 'marry' is the very theme
3977      I came to talk of. Tell me, daughter Juliet,
3978      How stands your disposition to be married?
3979      JULIET
3980      It is an honour that I dream not of.
3981      Nurse
3982      An honour! were not I thine only nurse,
3983      I would say thou hadst suck'd wisdom from thy teat.
3984      LADY CAPULET
3985      Well, think of marriage now; younger than you,
3986      Here in Verona, ladies of esteem,
3987      Are made already mothers: by my count,
3988      I was your mother much upon these years
3989      That you are now a maid. Thus then in brief:
3990      The valiant Paris seeks you for his love.
3991      Nurse
3992      A man, young lady! lady, such a man
3993      As all the world--why, he's a man of wax.
3994      LADY CAPULET
3995      Verona's summer hath not such a flower.
3996      Nurse
3997      Nay, he's a flower; in faith, a very flower.
3998      LADY CAPULET
3999      What say you? can you love the gentleman?
4000      This night you shall behold him at our feast;
4001      Read o'er the volume of young Paris' face,
4002      And find delight writ there with beauty's pen;
4003      Examine every married lineament,
4004      And see how one another lends content
4005      And what obscured in this fair volume lies
4006      Find written in the margent of his eyes.
4007      This precious book of love, this unbound lover,
4008      To beautify him, only lacks a cover:
4009      The fish lives in the sea, and 'tis much pride
4010      For fair without the fair within to hide:
4011      That book in many's eyes doth share the glory,
4012      That in gold clasps locks in the golden story;
4013      So shall you share all that he doth possess,
4014      By having him, making yourself no less.
4015      Nurse
4016      No less! nay, bigger; women grow by men.
4017      LADY CAPULET
4018      Speak briefly, can you like of Paris' love?
4019      JULIET
4020      I'll look to like, if looking liking move:
4021      But no more deep will I endart mine eye
4022      Than your consent gives strength to make it fly.
4023      Enter a Servant

4024      Servant
4025      Madam, the guests are come, supper served up, you
4026      called, my young lady asked for, the nurse cursed in
4027      the pantry, and every thing in extremity. I must
4028      hence to wait; I beseech you, follow straight.
4029      LADY CAPULET
4030      We follow thee.
4031      Exit Servant

4032      Juliet, the county stays.
4033      Nurse
4034      Go, girl, seek happy nights to happy days.
4035      Exeunt

4036      SCENE IV. A street.
4037      Enter ROMEO, MERCUTIO, BENVOLIO, with five or six Maskers, Torch-bearers, and others
4038      ROMEO
4039      What, shall this speech be spoke for our excuse?
4040      Or shall we on without a apology?
4041      BENVOLIO
4042      The date is out of such prolixity:
4043      We'll have no Cupid hoodwink'd with a scarf,
4044      Bearing a Tartar's painted bow of lath,
4045      Scaring the ladies like a crow-keeper;
4046      Nor no without-book prologue, faintly spoke
4047      After the prompter, for our entrance:
4048      But let them measure us by what they will;
4049      We'll measure them a measure, and be gone.
4050      ROMEO
4051      Give me a torch: I am not for this ambling;
4052      Being but heavy, I will bear the light.
4053      MERCUTIO
4054      Nay, gentle Romeo, we must have you dance.
4055      ROMEO
4056      Not I, believe me: you have dancing shoes
4057      With nimble soles: I have a soul of lead
4058      So stakes me to the ground I cannot move.
4059      MERCUTIO
4060      You are a lover; borrow Cupid's wings,
4061      And soar with them above a common bound.
4062      ROMEO
4063      I am too sore enpierced with his shaft
4064      To soar with his light feathers, and so bound,
4065      I cannot bound a pitch above dull woe:
4066      Under love's heavy burden do I sink.
4067      MERCUTIO
4068      And, to sink in it, should you burden love;
4069      Too great oppression for a tender thing.
4070      ROMEO
4071      Is love a tender thing? it is too rough,
4072      Too rude, too boisterous, and it pricks like thorn.
4073      MERCUTIO
4074      If love be rough with you, be rough with love;
4075      Prick love for pricking, and you beat love down.
4076      Give me a case to put my visage in:
4077      A visor for a visor! what care I
4078      What curious eye doth quote deformities?
4079      Here are the beetle brows shall blush for me.
4080      BENVOLIO
4081      Come, knock and enter; and no sooner in,
4082      But every man betake him to his legs.
4083      ROMEO
4084      A torch for me: let wantons light of heart
4085      Tickle the senseless rushes with their heels,
4086      For I am proverb'd with a grandsire phrase;
4087      I'll be a candle-holder, and look on.
4088      The game was ne'er so fair, and I am done.
4089      MERCUTIO
4090      Tut, dun's the mouse, the constable's own word:
4091      If thou art dun, we'll draw thee from the mire
4092      Of this sir-reverence love, wherein thou stick'st
4093      Up to the ears. Come, we burn daylight, ho!
4094      ROMEO
4095      Nay, that's not so.
4096      MERCUTIO
4097      I mean, sir, in delay
4098      We waste our lights in vain, like lamps by day.
4099      Take our good meaning, for our judgment sits
4100      Five times in that ere once in our five wits.
4101      ROMEO
4102      And we mean well in going to this mask;
4103      But 'tis no wit to go.
4104      MERCUTIO
4105      Why, may one ask?
4106      ROMEO
4107      I dream'd a dream to-night.
4108      MERCUTIO
4109      And so did I.
4110      ROMEO
4111      Well, what was yours?
4112      MERCUTIO
4113      That dreamers often lie.
4114      ROMEO
4115      In bed asleep, while they do dream things true.
4116      MERCUTIO
4117      O, then, I see Queen Mab hath been with you.
4118      She is the fairies' midwife, and she comes
4119      In shape no bigger than an agate-stone
4120      On the fore-finger of an alderman,
4121      Drawn with a team of little atomies
4122      Athwart men's noses as they lie asleep;
4123      Her wagon-spokes made of long spiders' legs,
4124      The cover of the wings of grasshoppers,
4125      The traces of the smallest spider's web,
4126      The collars of the moonshine's watery beams,
4127      Her whip of cricket's bone, the lash of film,
4128      Her wagoner a small grey-coated gnat,
4129      Not so big as a round little worm
4130      Prick'd from the lazy finger of a maid;
4131      Her chariot is an empty hazel-nut
4132      Made by the joiner squirrel or old grub,
4133      Time out o' mind the fairies' coachmakers.
4134      And in this state she gallops night by night
4135      Through lovers' brains, and then they dream of love;
4136      O'er courtiers' knees, that dream on court'sies straight,
4137      O'er lawyers' fingers, who straight dream on fees,
4138      O'er ladies ' lips, who straight on kisses dream,
4139      Which oft the angry Mab with blisters plagues,
4140      Because their breaths with sweetmeats tainted are:
4141      Sometime she gallops o'er a courtier's nose,
4142      And then dreams he of smelling out a suit;
4143      And sometime comes she with a tithe-pig's tail
4144      Tickling a parson's nose as a' lies asleep,
4145      Then dreams, he of another benefice:
4146      Sometime she driveth o'er a soldier's neck,
4147      And then dreams he of cutting foreign throats,
4148      Of breaches, ambuscadoes, Spanish blades,
4149      Of healths five-fathom deep; and then anon
4150      Drums in his ear, at which he starts and wakes,
4151      And being thus frighted swears a prayer or two
4152      And sleeps again. This is that very Mab
4153      That plats the manes of horses in the night,
4154      And bakes the elflocks in foul sluttish hairs,
4155      Which once untangled, much misfortune bodes:
4156      This is the hag, when maids lie on their backs,
4157      That presses them and learns them first to bear,
4158      Making them women of good carriage:
4159      This is she--
4160      ROMEO
4161      Peace, peace, Mercutio, peace!
4162      Thou talk'st of nothing.
4163      MERCUTIO
4164      True, I talk of dreams,
4165      Which are the children of an idle brain,
4166      Begot of nothing but vain fantasy,
4167      Which is as thin of substance as the air
4168      And more inconstant than the wind, who wooes
4169      Even now the frozen bosom of the north,
4170      And, being anger'd, puffs away from thence,
4171      Turning his face to the dew-dropping south.
4172      BENVOLIO
4173      This wind, you talk of, blows us from ourselves;
4174      Supper is done, and we shall come too late.
4175      ROMEO
4176      I fear, too early: for my mind misgives
4177      Some consequence yet hanging in the stars
4178      Shall bitterly begin his fearful date
4179      With this night's revels and expire the term
4180      Of a despised life closed in my breast
4181      By some vile forfeit of untimely death.
4182      But He, that hath the steerage of my course,
4183      Direct my sail! On, lusty gentlemen.
4184      BENVOLIO
4185      Strike, drum.
4186      Exeunt

4187      SCENE V. A hall in Capulet's house.
4188      Musicians waiting. Enter Servingmen with napkins
4189      First Servant
4190      Where's Potpan, that he helps not to take away? He
4191      shift a trencher? he scrape a trencher!
4192      Second Servant
4193      When good manners shall lie all in one or two men's
4194      hands and they unwashed too, 'tis a foul thing.
4195      First Servant
4196      Away with the joint-stools, remove the
4197      court-cupboard, look to the plate. Good thou, save
4198      me a piece of marchpane; and, as thou lovest me, let
4199      the porter let in Susan Grindstone and Nell.
4200      Antony, and Potpan!
4201      Second Servant
4202      Ay, boy, ready.
4203      First Servant
4204      You are looked for and called for, asked for and
4205      sought for, in the great chamber.
4206      Second Servant
4207      We cannot be here and there too. Cheerly, boys; be
4208      brisk awhile, and the longer liver take all.
4209      Enter CAPULET, with JULIET and others of his house, meeting the Guests and Maskers

4210      CAPULET
4211      Welcome, gentlemen! ladies that have their toes
4212      Unplagued with corns will have a bout with you.
4213      Ah ha, my mistresses! which of you all
4214      Will now deny to dance? she that makes dainty,
4215      She, I'll swear, hath corns; am I come near ye now?
4216      Welcome, gentlemen! I have seen the day
4217      That I have worn a visor and could tell
4218      A whispering tale in a fair lady's ear,
4219      Such as would please: 'tis gone, 'tis gone, 'tis gone:
4220      You are welcome, gentlemen! come, musicians, play.
4221      A hall, a hall! give room! and foot it, girls.
4222      Music plays, and they dance

4223      More light, you knaves; and turn the tables up,
4224      And quench the fire, the room is grown too hot.
4225      Ah, sirrah, this unlook'd-for sport comes well.
4226      Nay, sit, nay, sit, good cousin Capulet;
4227      For you and I are past our dancing days:
4228      How long is't now since last yourself and I
4229      Were in a mask?
4230      Second Capulet
4231      By'r lady, thirty years.
4232      CAPULET
4233      What, man! 'tis not so much, 'tis not so much:
4234      'Tis since the nuptials of Lucentio,
4235      Come pentecost as quickly as it will,
4236      Some five and twenty years; and then we mask'd.
4237      Second Capulet
4238      'Tis more, 'tis more, his son is elder, sir;
4239      His son is thirty.
4240      CAPULET
4241      Will you tell me that?
4242      His son was but a ward two years ago.
4243      ROMEO
4244      [To a Servingman] What lady is that, which doth
4245      enrich the hand
4246      Of yonder knight?
4247      Servant
4248      I know not, sir.
4249      ROMEO
4250      O, she doth teach the torches to burn bright!
4251      It seems she hangs upon the cheek of night
4252      Like a rich jewel in an Ethiope's ear;
4253      Beauty too rich for use, for earth too dear!
4254      So shows a snowy dove trooping with crows,
4255      As yonder lady o'er her fellows shows.
4256      The measure done, I'll watch her place of stand,
4257      And, touching hers, make blessed my rude hand.
4258      Did my heart love till now? forswear it, sight!
4259      For I ne'er saw true beauty till this night.
4260      TYBALT
4261      This, by his voice, should be a Montague.
4262      Fetch me my rapier, boy. What dares the slave
4263      Come hither, cover'd with an antic face,
4264      To fleer and scorn at our solemnity?
4265      Now, by the stock and honour of my kin,
4266      To strike him dead, I hold it not a sin.
4267      CAPULET
4268      Why, how now, kinsman! wherefore storm you so?
4269      TYBALT
4270      Uncle, this is a Montague, our foe,
4271      A villain that is hither come in spite,
4272      To scorn at our solemnity this night.
4273      CAPULET
4274      Young Romeo is it?
4275      TYBALT
4276      'Tis he, that villain Romeo.
4277      CAPULET
4278      Content thee, gentle coz, let him alone;
4279      He bears him like a portly gentleman;
4280      And, to say truth, Verona brags of him
4281      To be a virtuous and well-govern'd youth:
4282      I would not for the wealth of all the town
4283      Here in my house do him disparagement:
4284      Therefore be patient, take no note of him:
4285      It is my will, the which if thou respect,
4286      Show a fair presence and put off these frowns,
4287      And ill-beseeming semblance for a feast.
4288      TYBALT
4289      It fits, when such a villain is a guest:
4290      I'll not endure him.
4291      CAPULET
4292      He shall be endured:
4293      What, goodman boy! I say, he shall: go to;
4294      Am I the master here, or you? go to.
4295      You'll not endure him! God shall mend my soul!
4296      You'll make a mutiny among my guests!
4297      You will set cock-a-hoop! you'll be the man!
4298      TYBALT
4299      Why, uncle, 'tis a shame.
4300      CAPULET
4301      Go to, go to;
4302      You are a saucy boy: is't so, indeed?
4303      This trick may chance to scathe you, I know what:
4304      You must contrary me! marry, 'tis time.
4305      Well said, my hearts! You are a princox; go:
4306      Be quiet, or--More light, more light! For shame!
4307      I'll make you quiet. What, cheerly, my hearts!
4308      TYBALT
4309      Patience perforce with wilful choler meeting
4310      Makes my flesh tremble in their different greeting.
4311      I will withdraw: but this intrusion shall
4312      Now seeming sweet convert to bitter gall.
4313      Exit

4314      ROMEO
4315      [To JULIET] If I profane with my unworthiest hand
4316      This holy shrine, the gentle fine is this:
4317      My lips, two blushing pilgrims, ready stand
4318      To smooth that rough touch with a tender kiss.
4319      JULIET
4320      Good pilgrim, you do wrong your hand too much,
4321      Which mannerly devotion shows in this;
4322      For saints have hands that pilgrims' hands do touch,
4323      And palm to palm is holy palmers' kiss.
4324      ROMEO
4325      Have not saints lips, and holy palmers too?
4326      JULIET
4327      Ay, pilgrim, lips that they must use in prayer.
4328      ROMEO
4329      O, then, dear saint, let lips do what hands do;
4330      They pray, grant thou, lest faith turn to despair.
4331      JULIET
4332      Saints do not move, though grant for prayers' sake.
4333      ROMEO
4334      Then move not, while my prayer's effect I take.
4335      Thus from my lips, by yours, my sin is purged.
4336      JULIET
4337      Then have my lips the sin that they have took.
4338      ROMEO
4339      Sin from thy lips? O trespass sweetly urged!
4340      Give me my sin again.
4341      JULIET
4342      You kiss by the book.
4343      Nurse
4344      Madam, your mother craves a word with you.
4345      ROMEO
4346      What is her mother?
4347      Nurse
4348      Marry, bachelor,
4349      Her mother is the lady of the house,
4350      And a good lady, and a wise and virtuous
4351      I nursed her daughter, that you talk'd withal;
4352      I tell you, he that can lay hold of her
4353      Shall have the chinks.
4354      ROMEO
4355      Is she a Capulet?
4356      O dear account! my life is my foe's debt.
4357      BENVOLIO
4358      Away, begone; the sport is at the best.
4359      ROMEO
4360      Ay, so I fear; the more is my unrest.
4361      CAPULET
4362      Nay, gentlemen, prepare not to be gone;
4363      We have a trifling foolish banquet towards.
4364      Is it e'en so? why, then, I thank you all
4365      I thank you, honest gentlemen; good night.
4366      More torches here! Come on then, let's to bed.
4367      Ah, sirrah, by my fay, it waxes late:
4368      I'll to my rest.
4369      Exeunt all but JULIET and Nurse

4370      JULIET
4371      Come hither, nurse. What is yond gentleman?
4372      Nurse
4373      The son and heir of old Tiberio.
4374      JULIET
4375      What's he that now is going out of door?
4376      Nurse
4377      Marry, that, I think, be young Petrucio.
4378      JULIET
4379      What's he that follows there, that would not dance?
4380      Nurse
4381      I know not.
4382      JULIET
4383      Go ask his name: if he be married.
4384      My grave is like to be my wedding bed.
4385      Nurse
4386      His name is Romeo, and a Montague;
4387      The only son of your great enemy.
4388      JULIET
4389      My only love sprung from my only hate!
4390      Too early seen unknown, and known too late!
4391      Prodigious birth of love it is to me,
4392      That I must love a loathed enemy.
4393      Nurse
4394      What's this? what's this?
4395      JULIET
4396      A rhyme I learn'd even now
4397      Of one I danced withal.
4398      One calls within 'Juliet.'

4399      Nurse
4400      Anon, anon!
4401      Come, let's away; the strangers all are gone.
4402      Exeunt

4403      ACT II
4404      PROLOGUE
4405      Enter Chorus
4406      Chorus
4407      Now old desire doth in his death-bed lie,
4408      And young affection gapes to be his heir;
4409      That fair for which love groan'd for and would die,
4410      With tender Juliet match'd, is now not fair.
4411      Now Romeo is beloved and loves again,
4412      Alike betwitched by the charm of looks,
4413      But to his foe supposed he must complain,
4414      And she steal love's sweet bait from fearful hooks:
4415      Being held a foe, he may not have access
4416      To breathe such vows as lovers use to swear;
4417      And she as much in love, her means much less
4418      To meet her new-beloved any where:
4419      But passion lends them power, time means, to meet
4420      Tempering extremities with extreme sweet.
4421      Exit

4422      SCENE I. A lane by the wall of Capulet's orchard.
4423      Enter ROMEO
4424      ROMEO
4425      Can I go forward when my heart is here?
4426      Turn back, dull earth, and find thy centre out.
4427      He climbs the wall, and leaps down within it

4428      Enter BENVOLIO and MERCUTIO

4429      BENVOLIO
4430      Romeo! my cousin Romeo!
4431      MERCUTIO
4432      He is wise;
4433      And, on my lie, hath stol'n him home to bed.
4434      BENVOLIO
4435      He ran this way, and leap'd this orchard wall:
4436      Call, good Mercutio.
4437      MERCUTIO
4438      Nay, I'll conjure too.
4439      Romeo! humours! madman! passion! lover!
4440      Appear thou in the likeness of a sigh:
4441      Speak but one rhyme, and I am satisfied;
4442      Cry but 'Ay me!' pronounce but 'love' and 'dove;'
4443      Speak to my gossip Venus one fair word,
4444      One nick-name for her purblind son and heir,
4445      Young Adam Cupid, he that shot so trim,
4446      When King Cophetua loved the beggar-maid!
4447      He heareth not, he stirreth not, he moveth not;
4448      The ape is dead, and I must conjure him.
4449      I conjure thee by Rosaline's bright eyes,
4450      By her high forehead and her scarlet lip,
4451      By her fine foot, straight leg and quivering thigh
4452      And the demesnes that there adjacent lie,
4453      That in thy likeness thou appear to us!
4454      BENVOLIO
4455      And if he hear thee, thou wilt anger him.
4456      MERCUTIO
4457      This cannot anger him: 'twould anger him
4458      To raise a spirit in his mistress' circle
4459      Of some strange nature, letting it there stand
4460      Till she had laid it and conjured it down;
4461      That were some spite: my invocation
4462      Is fair and honest, and in his mistres s' name
4463      I conjure only but to raise up him.
4464      BENVOLIO
4465      Come, he hath hid himself among these trees,
4466      To be consorted with the humorous night:
4467      Blind is his love and best befits the dark.
4468      MERCUTIO
4469      If love be blind, love cannot hit the mark.
4470      Now will he sit under a medlar tree,
4471      And wish his mistress were that kind of fruit
4472      As maids call medlars, when they laugh alone.
4473      Romeo, that she were, O, that she were
4474      An open et caetera, thou a poperin pear!
4475      Romeo, good night: I'll to my truckle-bed;
4476      This field-bed is too cold for me to sleep:
4477      Come, shall we go?
4478      BENVOLIO
4479      Go, then; for 'tis in vain
4480      To seek him here that means not to be found.
4481      Exeunt

4482      SCENE II. Capulet's orchard.
4483      Enter ROMEO
4484      ROMEO
4485      He jests at scars that never felt a wound.
4486      JULIET appears above at a window

4487      But, soft! what light through yonder window breaks?
4488      It is the east, and Juliet is the sun.
4489      Arise, fair sun, and kill the envious moon,
4490      Who is already sick and pale with grief,
4491      That thou her maid art far more fair than she:
4492      Be not her maid, since she is envious;
4493      Her vestal livery is but sick and green
4494      And none but fools do wear it; cast it off.
4495      It is my lady, O, it is my love!
4496      O, that she knew she were!
4497      She speaks yet she says nothing: what of that?
4498      Her eye discourses; I will answer it.
4499      I am too bold, 'tis not to me she speaks:
4500      Two of the fairest stars in all the heaven,
4501      Having some business, do entreat her eyes
4502      To twinkle in their spheres till they return.
4503      What if her eyes were there, they in her head?
4504      The brightness of her cheek would shame those stars,
4505      As daylight doth a lamp; her eyes in heaven
4506      Would through the airy region stream so bright
4507      That birds would sing and think it were not night.
4508      See, how she leans her cheek upon her hand!
4509      O, that I were a glove upon that hand,
4510      That I might touch that cheek!
4511      JULIET
4512      Ay me!
4513      ROMEO
4514      She speaks:
