4515      O, speak again, bright angel! for thou art
4516      As glorious to this night, being o'er my head
4517      As is a winged messenger of heaven
4518      Unto the white-upturned wondering eyes
4519      Of mortals that fall back to gaze on him
4520      When he bestrides the lazy-pacing clouds
4521      And sails upon the bosom of the air.
4522      JULIET
4523      O Romeo, Romeo! wherefore art thou Romeo?
4524      Deny thy father and refuse thy name;
4525      Or, if thou wilt not, be but sworn my love,
4526      And I'll no longer be a Capulet.
4527      ROMEO
4528      [Aside] Shall I hear more, or shall I speak at this?
4529      JULIET
4530      'Tis but thy name that is my enemy;
4531      Thou art thyself, though not a Montague.
4532      What's Montague? it is nor hand, nor foot,
4533      Nor arm, nor face, nor any other part
4534      Belonging to a man. O, be some other name!
4535      What's in a name? that which we call a rose
4536      By any other name would smell as sweet;
4537      So Romeo would, were he not Romeo call'd,
4538      Retain that dear perfection which he owes
4539      Without that title. Romeo, doff thy name,
4540      And for that name which is no part of thee
4541      Take all myself.
4542      ROMEO
4543      I take thee at thy word:
4544      Call me but love, and I'll be new baptized;
4545      Henceforth I never will be Romeo.
4546      JULIET
4547      What man art thou that thus bescreen'd in night
4548      So stumblest on my counsel?
4549      ROMEO
4550      By a name
4551      I know not how to tell thee who I am:
4552      My name, dear saint, is hateful to myself,
4553      Because it is an enemy to thee;
4554      Had I it written, I would tear the word.
4555      JULIET
4556      My ears have not yet drunk a hundred words
4557      Of that tongue's utterance, yet I know the sound:
4558      Art thou not Romeo and a Montague?
4559      ROMEO
4560      Neither, fair saint, if either thee dislike.
4561      JULIET
4562      How camest thou hither, tell me, and wherefore?
4563      The orchard walls are high and hard to climb,
4564      And the place death, considering who thou art,
4565      If any of my kinsmen find thee here.
4566      ROMEO
4567      With love's light wings did I o'er-perch these walls;
4568      For stony limits cannot hold love out,
4569      And what love can do that dares love attempt;
4570      Therefore thy kinsmen are no let to me.
4571      JULIET
4572      If they do see thee, they will murder thee.
4573      ROMEO
4574      Alack, there lies more peril in thine eye
4575      Than twenty of their swords: look thou but sweet,
4576      And I am proof against their enmity.
4577      JULIET
4578      I would not for the world they saw thee here.
4579      ROMEO
4580      I have night's cloak to hide me from their sight;
4581      And but thou love me, let them find me here:
4582      My life were better ended by their hate,
4583      Than death prorogued, wanting of thy love.
4584      JULIET
4585      By whose direction found'st thou out this place?
4586      ROMEO
4587      By love, who first did prompt me to inquire;
4588      He lent me counsel and I lent him eyes.
4589      I am no pilot; yet, wert thou as far
4590      As that vast shore wash'd with the farthest sea,
4591      I would adventure for such merchandise.
4592      JULIET
4593      Thou know'st the mask of night is on my face,
4594      Else would a maiden blush bepaint my cheek
4595      For that which thou hast heard me speak to-night
4596      Fain would I dwell on form, fain, fain deny
4597      What I have spoke: but farewell compliment!
4598      Dost thou love me? I know thou wilt say 'Ay,'
4599      And I will take thy word: yet if thou swear'st,
4600      Thou mayst prove false; at lovers' perjuries
4601      Then say, Jove laughs. O gentle Romeo,
4602      If thou dost love, pronounce it faithfully:
4603      Or if thou think'st I am too quickly won,
4604      I'll frown and be perverse an say thee nay,
4605      So thou wilt woo; but else, not for the world.
4606      In truth, fair Montague, I am too fond,
4607      And therefore thou mayst think my 'havior light:
4608      But trust me, gentleman, I'll prove more true
4609      Than those that have more cunning to be strange.
4610      I should have been more strange, I must confess,
4611      But that thou overheard'st, ere I was ware,
4612      My true love's passion: therefore pardon me,
4613      And not impute this yielding to light love,
4614      Which the dark night hath so discovered.
4615      ROMEO
4616      Lady, by yonder blessed moon I swear
4617      That tips with silver all these fruit-tree tops--
4618      JULIET
4619      O, swear not by the moon, the inconstant moon,
4620      That monthly changes in her circled orb,
4621      Lest that thy love prove likewise variable.
4622      ROMEO
4623      What shall I swear by?
4624      JULIET
4625      Do not swear at all;
4626      Or, if thou wilt, swear by thy gracious self,
4627      Which is the god of my idolatry,
4628      And I'll believe thee.
4629      ROMEO
4630      If my heart's dear love--
4631      JULIET
4632      Well, do not swear: although I joy in thee,
4633      I have no joy of this contract to-night:
4634      It is too rash, too unadvised, too sudden;
4635      Too like the lightning, which doth cease to be
4636      Ere one can say 'It lightens.' Sweet, good night!
4637      This bud of love, by summer's ripening breath,
4638      May prove a beauteous flower when next we meet.
4639      Good night, good night! as sweet repose and rest
4640      Come to thy heart as that within my breast!
4641      ROMEO
4642      O, wilt thou leave me so unsatisfied?
4643      JULIET
4644      What satisfaction canst thou have to-night?
4645      ROMEO
4646      The exchange of thy love's faithful vow for mine.
4647      JULIET
4648      I gave thee mine before thou didst request it:
4649      And yet I would it were to give again.
4650      ROMEO
4651      Wouldst thou withdraw it? for what purpose, love?
4652      JULIET
4653      But to be frank, and give it thee again.
4654      And yet I wish but for the thing I have:
4655      My bounty is as boundless as the sea,
4656      My love as deep; the more I give to thee,
4657      The more I have, for both are infinite.
4658      Nurse calls within

4659      I hear some noise within; dear love, adieu!
4660      Anon, good nurse! Sweet Montague, be true.
4661      Stay but a little, I will come again.
4662      Exit, above

4663      ROMEO
4664      O blessed, blessed night! I am afeard.
4665      Being in night, all this is but a dream,
4666      Too flattering-sweet to be substantial.
4667      Re-enter JULIET, above

4668      JULIET
4669      Three words, dear Romeo, and good night indeed.
4670      If that thy bent of love be honourable,
4671      Thy purpose marriage, send me word to-morrow,
4672      By one that I'll procure to come to thee,
4673      Where and what time thou wilt perform the rite;
4674      And all my fortunes at thy foot I'll lay
4675      And follow thee my lord throughout the world.
4676      Nurse
4677      [Within] Madam!
4678      JULIET
4679      I come, anon.--But if thou mean'st not well,
4680      I do beseech thee--
4681      Nurse
4682      [Within] Madam!
4683      JULIET
4684      By and by, I come:--
4685      To cease thy suit, and leave me to my grief:
4686      To-morrow will I send.
4687      ROMEO
4688      So thrive my soul--
4689      JULIET
4690      A thousand times good night!
4691      Exit, above

4692      ROMEO
4693      A thousand times the worse, to want thy light.
4694      Love goes toward love, as schoolboys from
4695      their books,
4696      But love from love, toward school with heavy looks.
4697      Retiring

4698      Re-enter JULIET, above

4699      JULIET
4700      Hist! Romeo, hist! O, for a falconer's voice,
4701      To lure this tassel-gentle back again!
4702      Bondage is hoarse, and may not speak aloud;
4703      Else would I tear the cave where Echo lies,
4704      And make her airy tongue more hoarse than mine,
4705      With repetition of my Romeo's name.
4706      ROMEO
4707      It is my soul that calls upon my name:
4708      How silver-sweet sound lovers' tongues by night,
4709      Like softest music to attending ears!
4710      JULIET
4711      Romeo!
4712      ROMEO
4713      My dear?
4714      JULIET
4715      At what o'clock to-morrow
4716      Shall I send to thee?
4717      ROMEO
4718      At the hour of nine.
4719      JULIET
4720      I will not fail: 'tis twenty years till then.
4721      I have forgot why I did call thee back.
4722      ROMEO
4723      Let me stand here till thou remember it.
4724      JULIET
4725      I shall forget, to have thee still stand there,
4726      Remembering how I love thy company.
4727      ROMEO
4728      And I'll still stay, to have thee still forget,
4729      Forgetting any other home but this.
4730      JULIET
4731      'Tis almost morning; I would have thee gone:
4732      And yet no further than a wanton's bird;
4733      Who lets it hop a little from her hand,
4734      Like a poor prisoner in his twisted gyves,
4735      And with a silk thread plucks it back again,
4736      So loving-jealous of his liberty.
4737      ROMEO
4738      I would I were thy bird.
4739      JULIET
4740      Sweet, so would I:
4741      Yet I should kill thee with much cherishing.
4742      Good night, good night! parting is such
4743      sweet sorrow,
4744      That I shall say good night till it be morrow.
4745      Exit above

4746      ROMEO
4747      Sleep dwell upon thine eyes, peace in thy breast!
4748      Would I were sleep and peace, so sweet to rest!
4749      Hence will I to my ghostly father's cell,
4750      His help to crave, and my dear hap to tell.
4751      Exit

4752      SCENE III. Friar Laurence's cell.
4753      Enter FRIAR LAURENCE, with a basket
4754      FRIAR LAURENCE
4755      The grey-eyed morn smiles on the frowning night,
4756      Chequering the eastern clouds with streaks of light,
4757      And flecked darkness like a drunkard reels
4758      From forth day's path and Titan's fiery wheels:
4759      Now, ere the sun advance his burning eye,
4760      The day to cheer and night's dank dew to dry,
4761      I must up-fill this osier cage of ours
4762      With baleful weeds and precious-juiced flowers.
4763      The earth that's nature's mother is her tomb;
4764      What is her burying grave that is her womb,
4765      And from her womb children of divers kind
4766      We sucking on her natural bosom find,
4767      Many for many virtues excellent,
4768      None but for some and yet all different.
4769      O, mickle is the powerful grace that lies
4770      In herbs, plants, stones, and their true qualities:
4771      For nought so vile that on the earth doth live
4772      But to the earth some special good doth give,
4773      Nor aught so good but strain'd from that fair use
4774      Revolts from true birth, stumbling on abuse:
4775      Virtue itself turns vice, being misapplied;
4776      And vice sometimes by action dignified.
4777      Within the infant rind of this small flower
4778      Poison hath residence and medicine power:
4779      For this, being smelt, with that part cheers each part;
4780      Being tasted, slays all senses with the heart.
4781      Two such opposed kings encamp them still
4782      In man as well as herbs, grace and rude will;
4783      And where the worser is predominant,
4784      Full soon the canker death eats up that plant.
4785      Enter ROMEO

4786      ROMEO
4787      Good morrow, father.
4788      FRIAR LAURENCE
4789      Benedicite!
4790      What early tongue so sweet saluteth me?
4791      Young son, it argues a distemper'd head
4792      So soon to bid good morrow to thy bed:
4793      Care keeps his watch in every old man's eye,
4794      And where care lodges, sleep will never lie;
4795      But where unbruised youth with unstuff'd brain
4796      Doth couch his limbs, there golden sleep doth reign:
4797      Therefore thy earliness doth me assure
4798      Thou art up-roused by some distemperature;
4799      Or if not so, then here I hit it right,
4800      Our Romeo hath not been in bed to-night.
4801      ROMEO
4802      That last is true; the sweeter rest was mine.
4803      FRIAR LAURENCE
4804      God pardon sin! wast thou with Rosaline?
4805      ROMEO
4806      With Rosaline, my ghostly father? no;
4807      I have forgot that name, and that name's woe.
4808      FRIAR LAURENCE
4809      That's my good son: but where hast thou been, then?
4810      ROMEO
4811      I'll tell thee, ere thou ask it me again.
4812      I have been feasting with mine enemy,
4813      Where on a sudden one hath wounded me,
4814      That's by me wounded: both our remedies
4815      Within thy help and holy physic lies:
4816      I bear no hatred, blessed man, for, lo,
4817      My intercession likewise steads my foe.
4818      FRIAR LAURENCE
4819      Be plain, good son, and homely in thy drift;
4820      Riddling confession finds but riddling shrift.
4821      ROMEO
4822      Then plainly know my heart's dear love is set
4823      On the fair daughter of rich Capulet:
4824      As mine on hers, so hers is set on mine;
4825      And all combined, save what thou must combine
4826      By holy marriage: when and where and how
4827      We met, we woo'd and made exchange of vow,
4828      I'll tell thee as we pass; but this I pray,
4829      That thou consent to marry us to-day.
4830      FRIAR LAURENCE
4831      Holy Saint Francis, what a change is here!
4832      Is Rosaline, whom thou didst love so dear,
4833      So soon forsaken? young men's love then lies
4834      Not truly in their hearts, but in their eyes.
4835      Jesu Maria, what a deal of brine
4836      Hath wash'd thy sallow cheeks for Rosaline!
4837      How much salt water thrown away in waste,
4838      To season love, that of it doth not taste!
4839      The sun not yet thy sighs from heaven clears,
4840      Thy old groans ring yet in my ancient ears;
4841      Lo, here upon thy cheek the stain doth sit
4842      Of an old tear that is not wash'd off yet:
4843      If e'er thou wast thyself and these woes thine,
4844      Thou and these woes were all for Rosaline:
4845      And art thou changed? pronounce this sentence then,
4846      Women may fall, when there's no strength in men.
4847      ROMEO
4848      Thou chid'st me oft for loving Rosaline.
4849      FRIAR LAURENCE
4850      For doting, not for loving, pupil mine.
4851      ROMEO
4852      And bad'st me bury love.
4853      FRIAR LAURENCE
4854      Not in a grave,
4855      To lay one in, another out to have.
4856      ROMEO
4857      I pray thee, chide not; she whom I love now
4858      Doth grace for grace and love for love allow;
4859      The other did not so.
4860      FRIAR LAURENCE
4861      O, she knew well
4862      Thy love did read by rote and could not spell.
4863      But come, young waverer, come, go with me,
4864      In one respect I'll thy assistant be;
4865      For this alliance may so happy prove,
4866      To turn your households' rancour to pure love.
4867      ROMEO
4868      O, let us hence; I stand on sudden haste.
4869      FRIAR LAURENCE
4870      Wisely and slow; they stumble that run fast.
4871      Exeunt

4872      SCENE IV. A street.
4873      Enter BENVOLIO and MERCUTIO
4874      MERCUTIO
4875      Where the devil should this Romeo be?
4876      Came he not home to-night?
4877      BENVOLIO
4878      Not to his father's; I spoke with his man.
4879      MERCUTIO
4880      Ah, that same pale hard-hearted wench, that Rosaline.
4881      Torments him so, that he will sure run mad.
4882      BENVOLIO
4883      Tybalt, the kinsman of old Capulet,
4884      Hath sent a letter to his father's house.
4885      MERCUTIO
4886      A challenge, on my life.
4887      BENVOLIO
4888      Romeo will answer it.
4889      MERCUTIO
4890      Any man that can write may answer a letter.
4891      BENVOLIO
4892      Nay, he will answer the letter's master, how he
4893      dares, being dared.
4894      MERCUTIO
4895      Alas poor Romeo! he is already dead; stabbed with a
4896      white wench's black eye; shot through the ear with a
4897      love-song; the very pin of his heart cleft with the
4898      blind bow-boy's butt-shaft: and is he a man to
4899      encounter Tybalt?
4900      BENVOLIO
4901      Why, what is Tybalt?
4902      MERCUTIO
4903      More than prince of cats, I can tell you. O, he is
4904      the courageous captain of compliments. He fights as
4905      you sing prick-song, keeps time, distance, and
4906      proportion; rests me his minim rest, one, two, and
4907      the third in your bosom: the very butcher of a silk
4908      button, a duellist, a duellist; a gentleman of the
4909      very first house, of the first and second cause:
4910      ah, the immortal passado! the punto reverso! the
4911      hai!
4912      BENVOLIO
4913      The what?
4914      MERCUTIO
4915      The pox of such antic, lisping, affecting
4916      fantasticoes; these new tuners of accents! 'By Jesu,
4917      a very good blade! a very tall man! a very good
4918      whore!' Why, is not this a lamentable thing,
4919      grandsire, that we should be thus afflicted with
4920      these strange flies, these fashion-mongers, these
4921      perdona-mi's, who stand so much on the new form,
4922      that they cannot at ease on the old bench? O, their
4923      bones, their bones!
4924      Enter ROMEO

4925      BENVOLIO
4926      Here comes Romeo, here comes Romeo.
4927      MERCUTIO
4928      Without his roe, like a dried herring: flesh, flesh,
4929      how art thou fishified! Now is he for the numbers
4930      that Petrarch flowed in: Laura to his lady was but a
4931      kitchen-wench; marry, she had a better love to
4932      be-rhyme her; Dido a dowdy; Cleopatra a gipsy;
4933      Helen and Hero hildings and harlots; Thisbe a grey
4934      eye or so, but not to the purpose. Signior
4935      Romeo, bon jour! there's a French salutation
4936      to your French slop. You gave us the counterfeit
4937      fairly last night.
4938      ROMEO
4939      Good morrow to you both. What counterfeit did I give you?
4940      MERCUTIO
4941      The ship, sir, the slip; can you not conceive?
4942      ROMEO
4943      Pardon, good Mercutio, my business was great; and in
4944      such a case as mine a man may strain courtesy.
4945      MERCUTIO
4946      That's as much as to say, such a case as yours
4947      constrains a man to bow in the hams.
4948      ROMEO
4949      Meaning, to court'sy.
4950      MERCUTIO
4951      Thou hast most kindly hit it.
4952      ROMEO
4953      A most courteous exposition.
4954      MERCUTIO
4955      Nay, I am the very pink of courtesy.
4956      ROMEO
4957      Pink for flower.
4958      MERCUTIO
4959      Right.
4960      ROMEO
4961      Why, then is my pump well flowered.
4962      MERCUTIO
4963      Well said: follow me this jest now till thou hast
4964      worn out thy pump, that when the single sole of it
4965      is worn, the jest may remain after the wearing sole singular.
4966      ROMEO
4967      O single-soled jest, solely singular for the
4968      singleness.
4969      MERCUTIO
4970      Come between us, good Benvolio; my wits faint.
4971      ROMEO
4972      Switch and spurs, switch and spurs; or I'll cry a match.
4973      MERCUTIO
4974      Nay, if thy wits run the wild-goose chase, I have
4975      done, for thou hast more of the wild-goose in one of
4976      thy wits than, I am sure, I have in my whole five:
4977      was I with you there for the goose?
4978      ROMEO
4979      Thou wast never with me for any thing when thou wast
4980      not there for the goose.
4981      MERCUTIO
4982      I will bite thee by the ear for that jest.
4983      ROMEO
4984      Nay, good goose, bite not.
4985      MERCUTIO
4986      Thy wit is a very bitter sweeting; it is a most
4987      sharp sauce.
4988      ROMEO
4989      And is it not well served in to a sweet goose?
4990      MERCUTIO
4991      O here's a wit of cheveril, that stretches from an
4992      inch narrow to an ell broad!
4993      ROMEO
4994      I stretch it out for that word 'broad;' which added
4995      to the goose, proves thee far and wide a broad goose.
4996      MERCUTIO
4997      Why, is not this better now than groaning for love?
4998      now art thou sociable, now art thou Romeo; now art
4999      thou what thou art, by art as well as by nature:
5000      for this drivelling love is like a great natural,
5001      that runs lolling up and down to hide his bauble in a hole.
5002      BENVOLIO
5003      Stop there, stop there.
5004      MERCUTIO
5005      Thou desirest me to stop in my tale against the hair.
5006      BENVOLIO
5007      Thou wouldst else have made thy tale large.
5008      MERCUTIO
5009      O, thou art deceived; I would have made it short:
5010      for I was come to the whole depth of my tale; and
5011      meant, indeed, to occupy the argument no longer.
5012      ROMEO
5013      Here's goodly gear!
5014      Enter Nurse and PETER

5015      MERCUTIO
5016      A sail, a sail!
5017      BENVOLIO
5018      Two, two; a shirt and a smock.
5019      Nurse
5020      Peter!
5021      PETER
5022      Anon!
5023      Nurse
5024      My fan, Peter.
5025      MERCUTIO
5026      Good Peter, to hide her face; for her fan's the
5027      fairer face.
5028      Nurse
5029      God ye good morrow, gentlemen.
5030      MERCUTIO
5031      God ye good den, fair gentlewoman.
5032      Nurse
5033      Is it good den?
5034      MERCUTIO
5035      'Tis no less, I tell you, for the bawdy hand of the
5036      dial is now upon the prick of noon.
5037      Nurse
5038      Out upon you! what a man are you!
5039      ROMEO
5040      One, gentlewoman, that God hath made for himself to
5041      mar.
5042      Nurse
5043      By my troth, it is well said; 'for himself to mar,'
5044      quoth a'? Gentlemen, can any of you tell me where I
5045      may find the young Romeo?
5046      ROMEO
5047      I can tell you; but young Romeo will be older when
5048      you have found him than he was when you sought him:
5049      I am the youngest of that name, for fault of a worse.
5050      Nurse
5051      You say well.
5052      MERCUTIO
5053      Yea, is the worst well? very well took, i' faith;
5054      wisely, wisely.
5055      Nurse
5056      if you be he, sir, I desire some confidence with
5057      you.
5058      BENVOLIO
5059      She will indite him to some supper.
5060      MERCUTIO
5061      A bawd, a bawd, a bawd! so ho!
5062      ROMEO
5063      What hast thou found?
5064      MERCUTIO
5065      No hare, sir; unless a hare, sir, in a lenten pie,
5066      that is something stale and hoar ere it be spent.
5067      Sings

5068      An old hare hoar,
5069      And an old hare hoar,
5070      Is very good meat in lent
5071      But a hare that is hoar
5072      Is too much for a score,
5073      When it hoars ere it be spent.
5074      Romeo, will you come to your father's? we'll
5075      to dinner, thither.
5076      ROMEO
5077      I will follow you.
5078      MERCUTIO
5079      Farewell, ancient lady; farewell,
5080      Singing

5081      'lady, lady, lady.'
5082      Exeunt MERCUTIO and BENVOLIO

5083      Nurse
5084      Marry, farewell! I pray you, sir, what saucy
5085      merchant was this, that was so full of his ropery?
5086      ROMEO
5087      A gentleman, nurse, that loves to hear himself talk,
5088      and will speak more in a minute than he will stand
5089      to in a month.
5090      Nurse
5091      An a' speak any thing against me, I'll take him
5092      down, an a' were lustier than he is, and twenty such
5093      Jacks; and if I cannot, I'll find those that shall.
5094      Scurvy knave! I am none of his flirt-gills; I am
5095      none of his skains-mates. And thou must stand by
5096      too, and suffer every knave to use me at his pleasure?
5097      PETER
5098      I saw no man use you a pleasure; if I had, my weapon
5099      should quickly have been out, I warrant you: I dare
5100      draw as soon as another man, if I see occasion in a
5101      good quarrel, and the law on my side.
5102      Nurse
5103      Now, afore God, I am so vexed, that every part about
5104      me quivers. Scurvy knave! Pray you, sir, a word:
5105      and as I told you, my young lady bade me inquire you
5106      out; what she bade me say, I will keep to myself:
5107      but first let me tell ye, if ye should lead her into
5108      a fool's paradise, as they say, it were a very gross
5109      kind of behavior, as they say: for the gentlewoman
5110      is young; and, therefore, if you should deal double
5111      with her, truly it were an ill thing to be offered
5112      to any gentlewoman, and very weak dealing.
5113      ROMEO
5114      Nurse, commend me to thy lady and mistress. I
5115      protest unto thee--
5116      Nurse
5117      Good heart, and, i' faith, I will tell her as much:
5118      Lord, Lord, she will be a joyful woman.
5119      ROMEO
5120      What wilt thou tell her, nurse? thou dost not mark me.
5121      Nurse
5122      I will tell her, sir, that you do protest; which, as
5123      I take it, is a gentlemanlike offer.
5124      ROMEO
5125      Bid her devise
5126      Some means to come to shrift this afternoon;
5127      And there she shall at Friar Laurence' cell
5128      Be shrived and married. Here is for thy pains.
5129      Nurse
5130      No truly sir; not a penny.
5131      ROMEO
5132      Go to; I say you shall.
5133      Nurse
5134      This afternoon, sir? well, she shall be there.
5135      ROMEO
5136      And stay, good nurse, behind the abbey wall:
5137      Within this hour my man shall be with thee
5138      And bring thee cords made like a tackled stair;
5139      Which to the high top-gallant of my joy
5140      Must be my convoy in the secret night.
5141      Farewell; be trusty, and I'll quit thy pains:
5142      Farewell; commend me to thy mistress.
5143      Nurse
5144      Now God in heaven bless thee! Hark you, sir.
5145      ROMEO
5146      What say'st thou, my dear nurse?
5147      Nurse
5148      Is your man secret? Did you ne'er hear say,
5149      Two may keep counsel, putting one away?
5150      ROMEO
5151      I warrant thee, my man's as true as steel.
5152      NURSE
5153      Well, sir; my mistress is the sweetest lady--Lord,
5154      Lord! when 'twas a little prating thing:--O, there
5155      is a nobleman in town, one Paris, that would fain
5156      lay knife aboard; but she, good soul, had as lief
5157      see a toad, a very toad, as see him. I anger her
5158      sometimes and tell her that Paris is the properer
5159      man; but, I'll warrant you, when I say so, she looks
5160      as pale as any clout in the versal world. Doth not
5161      rosemary and Romeo begin both with a letter?
5162      ROMEO
5163      Ay, nurse; what of that? both with an R.
5164      Nurse
5165      Ah. mocker! that's the dog's name; R is for
5166      the--No; I know it begins with some other
5167      letter:--and she hath the prettiest sententious of
5168      it, of you and rosemary, that it would do you good
5169      to hear it.
5170      ROMEO
5171      Commend me to thy lady.
5172      Nurse
5173      Ay, a thousand times.
5174      Exit Romeo

5175      Peter!
5176      PETER
5177      Anon!
5178      Nurse
5179      Peter, take my fan, and go before and apace.
5180      Exeunt

5181      SCENE V. Capulet's orchard.
5182      Enter JULIET
5183      JULIET
5184      The clock struck nine when I did send the nurse;
5185      In half an hour she promised to return.
5186      Perchance she cannot meet him: that's not so.
5187      O, she is lame! love's heralds should be thoughts,
5188      Which ten times faster glide than the sun's beams,
5189      Driving back shadows over louring hills:
5190      Therefore do nimble-pinion'd doves draw love,
5191      And therefore hath the wind-swift Cupid wings.
5192      Now is the sun upon the highmost hill
5193      Of this day's journey, and from nine till twelve
5194      Is three long hours, yet she is not come.
5195      Had she affections and warm youthful blood,
5196      She would be as swift in motion as a ball;
5197      My words would bandy her to my sweet love,
5198      And his to me:
5199      But old folks, many feign as they were dead;
5200      Unwieldy, slow, heavy and pale as lead.
5201      O God, she comes!
5202      Enter Nurse and PETER

5203      O honey nurse, what news?
5204      Hast thou met with him? Send thy man away.
5205      Nurse
5206      Peter, stay at the gate.
5207      Exit PETER

5208      JULIET
5209      Now, good sweet nurse,--O Lord, why look'st thou sad?
5210      Though news be sad, yet tell them merrily;
5211      If good, thou shamest the music of sweet news
5212      By playing it to me with so sour a face.
5213      Nurse
5214      I am a-weary, give me leave awhile:
5215      Fie, how my bones ache! what a jaunt have I had!
5216      JULIET
5217      I would thou hadst my bones, and I thy news:
5218      Nay, come, I pray thee, speak; good, good nurse, speak.
5219      Nurse
5220      Jesu, what haste? can you not stay awhile?
5221      Do you not see that I am out of breath?
5222      JULIET
5223      How art thou out of breath, when thou hast breath
5224      To say to me that thou art out of breath?
5225      The excuse that thou dost make in this delay
5226      Is longer than the tale thou dost excuse.
5227      Is thy news good, or bad? answer to that;
5228      Say either, and I'll stay the circumstance:
5229      Let me be satisfied, is't good or bad?
5230      Nurse
5231      Well, you have made a simple choice; you know not
5232      how to choose a man: Romeo! no, not he; though his
5233      face be better than any man's, yet his leg excels
5234      all men's; and for a hand, and a foot, and a body,
5235      though they be not to be talked on, yet they are
5236      past compare: he is not the flower of courtesy,
5237      but, I'll warrant him, as gentle as a lamb. Go thy
5238      ways, wench; serve God. What, have you dined at home?
5239      JULIET
5240      No, no: but all this did I know before.
5241      What says he of our marriage? what of that?
5242      Nurse
5243      Lord, how my head aches! what a head have I!
5244      It beats as it would fall in twenty pieces.
5245      My back o' t' other side,--O, my back, my back!
5246      Beshrew your heart for sending me about,
5247      To catch my death with jaunting up and down!
5248      JULIET
5249      I' faith, I am sorry that thou art not well.
5250      Sweet, sweet, sweet nurse, tell me, what says my love?
5251      Nurse
5252      Your love says, like an honest gentleman, and a
5253      courteous, and a kind, and a handsome, and, I
5254      warrant, a virtuous,--Where is your mother?
5255      JULIET
5256      Where is my mother! why, she is within;
5257      Where should she be? How oddly thou repliest!
5258      'Your love says, like an honest gentleman,
5259      Where is your mother?'
5260      Nurse
5261      O God's lady dear!
5262      Are you so hot? marry, come up, I trow;
5263      Is this the poultice for my aching bones?
5264      Henceforward do your messages yourself.
5265      JULIET
5266      Here's such a coil! come, what says Romeo?
5267      Nurse
5268      Have you got leave to go to shrift to-day?
5269      JULIET
5270      I have.
5271      Nurse
5272      Then hie you hence to Friar Laurence' cell;
5273      There stays a husband to make you a wife:
5274      Now comes the wanton blood up in your cheeks,
5275      They'll be in scarlet straight at any news.
5276      Hie you to church; I must another way,
5277      To fetch a ladder, by the which your love
5278      Must climb a bird's nest soon when it is dark:
5279      I am the drudge and toil in your delight,
5280      But you shall bear the burden soon at night.
5281      Go; I'll to dinner: hie you to the cell.
5282      JULIET
5283      Hie to high fortune! Honest nurse, farewell.
5284      Exeunt

5285      SCENE VI. Friar Laurence's cell.
5286      Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and ROMEO
5287      FRIAR LAURENCE
5288      So smile the heavens upon this holy act,
5289      That after hours with sorrow chide us not!
5290      ROMEO
5291      Amen, amen! but come what sorrow can,
5292      It cannot countervail the exchange of joy
5293      That one short minute gives me in her sight:
5294      Do thou but close our hands with holy words,
5295      Then love-devouring death do what he dare;
5296      It is enough I may but call her mine.
5297      FRIAR LAURENCE
5298      These violent delights have violent ends
5299      And in their triumph die, like fire and powder,
5300      Which as they kiss consume: the sweetest honey
5301      Is loathsome in his own deliciousness
5302      And in the taste confounds the appetite:
5303      Therefore love moderately; long love doth so;
5304      Too swift arrives as tardy as too slow.
5305      Enter JULIET

5306      Here comes the lady: O, so light a foot
5307      Will ne'er wear out the everlasting flint:
5308      A lover may bestride the gossamer
5309      That idles in the wanton summer air,
5310      And yet not fall; so light is vanity.
5311      JULIET
5312      Good even to my ghostly confessor.
5313      FRIAR LAURENCE
5314      Romeo shall thank thee, daughter, for us both.
5315      JULIET
5316      As much to him, else is his thanks too much.
5317      ROMEO
5318      Ah, Juliet, if the measure of thy joy
5319      Be heap'd like mine and that thy skill be more
5320      To blazon it, then sweeten with thy breath
5321      This neighbour air, and let rich music's tongue
5322      Unfold the imagined happiness that both
5323      Receive in either by this dear encounter.
5324      JULIET
5325      Conceit, more rich in matter than in words,
5326      Brags of his substance, not of ornament:
5327      They are but beggars that can count their worth;
5328      But my true love is grown to such excess
5329      I cannot sum up sum of half my wealth.
5330      FRIAR LAURENCE
5331      Come, come with me, and we will make short work;
5332      For, by your leaves, you shall not stay alone
5333      Till holy church incorporate two in one.
5334      Exeunt

5335      ACT III
5336      SCENE I. A public place.
5337      Enter MERCUTIO, BENVOLIO, Page, and Servants
5338      BENVOLIO
5339      I pray thee, good Mercutio, let's retire:
5340      The day is hot, the Capulets abroad,
5341      And, if we meet, we shall not scape a brawl;
5342      For now, these hot days, is the mad blood stirring.
5343      MERCUTIO
5344      Thou art like one of those fellows that when he
5345      enters the confines of a tavern claps me his sword
5346      upon the table and says 'God send me no need of
5347      thee!' and by the operation of the second cup draws
5348      it on the drawer, when indeed there is no need.
5349      BENVOLIO
5350      Am I like such a fellow?
5351      MERCUTIO
5352      Come, come, thou art as hot a Jack in thy mood as
5353      any in Italy, and as soon moved to be moody, and as
5354      soon moody to be moved.
5355      BENVOLIO
5356      And what to?
5357      MERCUTIO
5358      Nay, an there were two such, we should have none
5359      shortly, for one would kill the other. Thou! why,
5360      thou wilt quarrel with a man that hath a hair more,
5361      or a hair less, in his beard, than thou hast: thou
5362      wilt quarrel with a man for cracking nuts, having no
5363      other reason but because thou hast hazel eyes: what
5364      eye but such an eye would spy out such a quarrel?
5365      Thy head is as fun of quarrels as an egg is full of
5366      meat, and yet thy head hath been beaten as addle as
5367      an egg for quarrelling: thou hast quarrelled with a
5368      man for coughing in the street, because he hath
5369      wakened thy dog that hath lain asleep in the sun:
5370      didst thou not fall out with a tailor for wearing
5371      his new doublet before Easter? with another, for
5372      tying his new shoes with old riband? and yet thou
5373      wilt tutor me from quarrelling!
5374      BENVOLIO
5375      An I were so apt to quarrel as thou art, any man
5376      should buy the fee-simple of my life for an hour and a quarter.
5377      MERCUTIO
5378      The fee-simple! O simple!
5379      BENVOLIO
5380      By my head, here come the Capulets.
5381      MERCUTIO
5382      By my heel, I care not.
5383      Enter TYBALT and others

5384      TYBALT
5385      Follow me close, for I will speak to them.
5386      Gentlemen, good den: a word with one of you.
5387      MERCUTIO
5388      And but one word with one of us? couple it with
5389      something; make it a word and a blow.
5390      TYBALT
5391      You shall find me apt enough to that, sir, an you
5392      will give me occasion.
5393      MERCUTIO
5394      Could you not take some occasion without giving?
5395      TYBALT
5396      Mercutio, thou consort'st with Romeo,--
5397      MERCUTIO
5398      Consort! what, dost thou make us minstrels? an
5399      thou make minstrels of us, look to hear nothing but
5400      discords: here's my fiddlestick; here's that shall
5401      make you dance. 'Zounds, consort!
5402      BENVOLIO
5403      We talk here in the public haunt of men:
5404      Either withdraw unto some private place,
5405      And reason coldly of your grievances,
5406      Or else depart; here all eyes gaze on us.
5407      MERCUTIO
5408      Men's eyes were made to look, and let them gaze;
5409      I will not budge for no man's pleasure, I.
5410      Enter ROMEO

5411      TYBALT
5412      Well, peace be with you, sir: here comes my man.
5413      MERCUTIO
5414      But I'll be hanged, sir, if he wear your livery:
5415      Marry, go before to field, he'll be your follower;
5416      Your worship in that sense may call him 'man.'
5417      TYBALT
5418      Romeo, the hate I bear thee can afford
5419      No better term than this,--thou art a villain.
5420      ROMEO
5421      Tybalt, the reason that I have to love thee
5422      Doth much excuse the appertaining rage
5423      To such a greeting: villain am I none;
5424      Therefore farewell; I see thou know'st me not.
5425      TYBALT
5426      Boy, this shall not excuse the injuries
5427      That thou hast done me; therefore turn and draw.
5428      ROMEO
5429      I do protest, I never injured thee,
5430      But love thee better than thou canst devise,
5431      Till thou shalt know the reason of my love:
5432      And so, good Capulet,--which name I tender
5433      As dearly as my own,--be satisfied.
5434      MERCUTIO
5435      O calm, dishonourable, vile submission!
5436      Alla stoccata carries it away.
5437      Draws

5438      Tybalt, you rat-catcher, will you walk?
5439      TYBALT
5440      What wouldst thou have with me?
5441      MERCUTIO
5442      Good king of cats, nothing but one of your nine
5443      lives; that I mean to make bold withal, and as you
5444      shall use me hereafter, drybeat the rest of the
5445      eight. Will you pluck your sword out of his pitcher
5446      by the ears? make haste, lest mine be about your
5447      ears ere it be out.
5448      TYBALT
5449      I am for you.
5450      Drawing

5451      ROMEO
5452      Gentle Mercutio, put thy rapier up.
5453      MERCUTIO
5454      Come, sir, your passado.
5455      They fight

5456      ROMEO
5457      Draw, Benvolio; beat down their weapons.
5458      Gentlemen, for shame, forbear this outrage!
5459      Tybalt, Mercutio, the prince expressly hath
5460      Forbidden bandying in Verona streets:
5461      Hold, Tybalt! good Mercutio!
5462      TYBALT under ROMEO's arm stabs MERCUTIO, and flies with his followers

5463      MERCUTIO
5464      I am hurt.
5465      A plague o' both your houses! I am sped.
5466      Is he gone, and hath nothing?
5467      BENVOLIO
5468      What, art thou hurt?
5469      MERCUTIO
5470      Ay, ay, a scratch, a scratch; marry, 'tis enough.
5471      Where is my page? Go, villain, fetch a surgeon.
5472      Exit Page

5473      ROMEO
5474      Courage, man; the hurt cannot be much.
5475      MERCUTIO
5476      No, 'tis not so deep as a well, nor so wide as a
5477      church-door; but 'tis enough,'twill serve: ask for
5478      me to-morrow, and you shall find me a grave man. I
5479      am peppered, I warrant, for this world. A plague o'
5480      both your houses! 'Zounds, a dog, a rat, a mouse, a
5481      cat, to scratch a man to death! a braggart, a
5482      rogue, a villain, that fights by the book of
5483      arithmetic! Why the devil came you between us? I
5484      was hurt under your arm.
5485      ROMEO
5486      I thought all for the best.
5487      MERCUTIO
5488      Help me into some house, Benvolio,
5489      Or I shall faint. A plague o' both your houses!
5490      They have made worms' meat of me: I have it,
5491      And soundly too: your houses!
5492      Exeunt MERCUTIO and BENVOLIO

5493      ROMEO
5494      This gentleman, the prince's near ally,
5495      My very friend, hath got his mortal hurt
5496      In my behalf; my reputation stain'd
5497      With Tybalt's slander,--Tybalt, that an hour
5498      Hath been my kinsman! O sweet Juliet,
5499      Thy beauty hath made me effeminate
5500      And in my temper soften'd valour's steel!
5501      Re-enter BENVOLIO

5502      BENVOLIO
5503      O Romeo, Romeo, brave Mercutio's dead!
5504      That gallant spirit hath aspired the clouds,
5505      Which too untimely here did scorn the earth.
5506      ROMEO
5507      This day's black fate on more days doth depend;
5508      This but begins the woe, others must end.
5509      BENVOLIO
5510      Here comes the furious Tybalt back again.
5511      ROMEO
5512      Alive, in triumph! and Mercutio slain!
5513      Away to heaven, respective lenity,
5514      And fire-eyed fury be my conduct now!
5515      Re-enter TYBALT

5516      Now, Tybalt, take the villain back again,
5517      That late thou gavest me; for Mercutio's soul
5518      Is but a little way above our heads,
5519      Staying for thine to keep him company:
5520      Either thou, or I, or both, must go with him.
5521      TYBALT
5522      Thou, wretched boy, that didst consort him here,
5523      Shalt with him hence.
5524      ROMEO
5525      This shall determine that.
5526      They fight; TYBALT falls

5527      BENVOLIO
5528      Romeo, away, be gone!
5529      The citizens are up, and Tybalt slain.
5530      Stand not amazed: the prince will doom thee death,
5531      If thou art taken: hence, be gone, away!
5532      ROMEO
5533      O, I am fortune's fool!
5534      BENVOLIO
5535      Why dost thou stay?
5536      Exit ROMEO

5537      Enter Citizens, & c

5538      First Citizen
5539      Which way ran he that kill'd Mercutio?
5540      Tybalt, that murderer, which way ran he?
5541      BENVOLIO
5542      There lies that Tybalt.
5543      First Citizen
5544      Up, sir, go with me;
5545      I charge thee in the princes name, obey.
5546      Enter Prince, attended; MONTAGUE, CAPULET, their Wives, and others

5547      PRINCE
5548      Where are the vile beginners of this fray?
5549      BENVOLIO
5550      O noble prince, I can discover all
5551      The unlucky manage of this fatal brawl:
5552      There lies the man, slain by young Romeo,
5553      That slew thy kinsman, brave Mercutio.
5554      LADY CAPULET
5555      Tybalt, my cousin! O my brother's child!
5556      O prince! O cousin! husband! O, the blood is spilt
5557      O my dear kinsman! Prince, as thou art true,
5558      For blood of ours, shed blood of Montague.
5559      O cousin, cousin!
5560      PRINCE
5561      Benvolio, who began this bloody fray?
5562      BENVOLIO
5563      Tybalt, here slain, whom Romeo's hand did slay;
5564      Romeo that spoke him fair, bade him bethink
5565      How nice the quarrel was, and urged withal
5566      Your high displeasure: all this uttered
5567      With gentle breath, calm look, knees humbly bow'd,
5568      Could not take truce with the unruly spleen
5569      Of Tybalt deaf to peace, but that he tilts
5570      With piercing steel at bold Mercutio's breast,
5571      Who all as hot, turns deadly point to point,
5572      And, with a martial scorn, with one hand beats
5573      Cold death aside, and with the other sends
5574      It back to Tybalt, whose dexterity,
5575      Retorts it: Romeo he cries aloud,
5576      'Hold, friends! friends, part!' and, swifter than
5577      his tongue,
5578      His agile arm beats down their fatal points,
5579      And 'twixt them rushes; underneath whose arm
5580      An envious thrust from Tybalt hit the life
5581      Of stout Mercutio, and then Tybalt fled;
5582      But by and by comes back to Romeo,
5583      Who had but newly entertain'd revenge,
5584      And to 't they go like lightning, for, ere I
5585      Could draw to part them, was stout Tybalt slain.
5586      And, as he fell, did Romeo turn and fly.
5587      This is the truth, or let Benvolio die.
5588      LADY CAPULET
5589      He is a kinsman to the Montague;
5590      Affection makes him false; he speaks not true:
5591      Some twenty of them fought in this black strife,
5592      And all those twenty could but kill one life.
5593      I beg for justice, which thou, prince, must give;
5594      Romeo slew Tybalt, Romeo must not live.
5595      PRINCE
5596      Romeo slew him, he slew Mercutio;
5597      Who now the price of his dear blood doth owe?
5598      MONTAGUE
5599      Not Romeo, prince, he was Mercutio's friend;
5600      His fault concludes but what the law should end,
5601      The life of Tybalt.
5602      PRINCE
5603      And for that offence
5604      Immediately we do exile him hence:
5605      I have an interest in your hate's proceeding,
5606      My blood for your rude brawls doth lie a-bleeding;
5607      But I'll amerce you with so strong a fine
5608      That you shall all repent the loss of mine:
5609      I will be deaf to pleading and excuses;
5610      Nor tears nor prayers shall purchase out abuses:
5611      Therefore use none: let Romeo hence in haste,
5612      Else, when he's found, that hour is his last.
5613      Bear hence this body and attend our will:
5614      Mercy but murders, pardoning those that kill.
5615      Exeunt

5616      SCENE II. Capulet's orchard.
5617      Enter JULIET
5618      JULIET
5619      Gallop apace, you fiery-footed steeds,
5620      Towards Phoebus' lodging: such a wagoner
5621      As Phaethon would whip you to the west,
5622      And bring in cloudy night immediately.
5623      Spread thy close curtain, love-performing night,
5624      That runaway's eyes may wink and Romeo
5625      Leap to these arms, untalk'd of and unseen.
5626      Lovers can see to do their amorous rites
5627      By their own beauties; or, if love be blind,
5628      It best agrees with night. Come, civil night,
5629      Thou sober-suited matron, all in black,
5630      And learn me how to lose a winning match,
5631      Play'd for a pair of stainless maidenhoods:
5632      Hood my unmann'd blood, bating in my cheeks,
5633      With thy black mantle; till strange love, grown bold,
5634      Think true love acted simple modesty.
5635      Come, night; come, Romeo; come, thou day in night;
5636      For thou wilt lie upon the wings of night
5637      Whiter than new snow on a raven's back.
5638      Come, gentle night, come, loving, black-brow'd night,
5639      Give me my Romeo; and, when he shall die,
5640      Take him and cut him out in little stars,
5641      And he will make the face of heaven so fine
5642      That all the world will be in love with night
5643      And pay no worship to the garish sun.
5644      O, I have bought the mansion of a love,
5645      But not possess'd it, and, though I am sold,
5646      Not yet enjoy'd: so tedious is this day
5647      As is the night before some festival
5648      To an impatient child that hath new robes
5649      And may not wear them. O, here comes my nurse,
5650      And she brings news; and every tongue that speaks
5651      But Romeo's name speaks heavenly eloquence.
5652      Enter Nurse, with cords

5653      Now, nurse, what news? What hast thou there? the cords
5654      That Romeo bid thee fetch?
5655      Nurse
5656      Ay, ay, the cords.
5657      Throws them down

5658      JULIET
5659      Ay me! what news? why dost thou wring thy hands?
5660      Nurse
5661      Ah, well-a-day! he's dead, he's dead, he's dead!
5662      We are undone, lady, we are undone!
5663      Alack the day! he's gone, he's kill'd, he's dead!
5664      JULIET
5665      Can heaven be so envious?
5666      Nurse
5667      Romeo can,
5668      Though heaven cannot: O Romeo, Romeo!
5669      Who ever would have thought it? Romeo!
5670      JULIET
5671      What devil art thou, that dost torment me thus?
5672      This torture should be roar'd in dismal hell.
5673      Hath Romeo slain himself? say thou but 'I,'
5674      And that bare vowel 'I' shall poison more
5675      Than the death-darting eye of cockatrice:
5676      I am not I, if there be such an I;
5677      Or those eyes shut, that make thee answer 'I.'
5678      If he be slain, say 'I'; or if not, no:
5679      Brief sounds determine of my weal or woe.
5680      Nurse
5681      I saw the wound, I saw it with mine eyes,--
5682      God save the mark!--here on his manly breast:
5683      A piteous corse, a bloody piteous corse;
5684      Pale, pale as ashes, all bedaub'd in blood,
5685      All in gore-blood; I swounded at the sight.
5686      JULIET
5687      O, break, my heart! poor bankrupt, break at once!
5688      To prison, eyes, ne'er look on liberty!
5689      Vile earth, to earth resign; end motion here;
5690      And thou and Romeo press one heavy bier!
5691      Nurse
5692      O Tybalt, Tybalt, the best friend I had!
5693      O courteous Tybalt! honest gentleman!
5694      That ever I should live to see thee dead!
5695      JULIET
5696      What storm is this that blows so contrary?
5697      Is Romeo slaughter'd, and is Tybalt dead?
5698      My dear-loved cousin, and my dearer lord?
5699      Then, dreadful trumpet, sound the general doom!
5700      For who is living, if those two are gone?
5701      Nurse
5702      Tybalt is gone, and Romeo banished;
5703      Romeo that kill'd him, he is banished.
5704      JULIET
5705      O God! did Romeo's hand shed Tybalt's blood?
5706      Nurse
5707      It did, it did; alas the day, it did!
5708      JULIET
5709      O serpent heart, hid with a flowering face!
5710      Did ever dragon keep so fair a cave?
5711      Beautiful tyrant! fiend angelical!
5712      Dove-feather'd raven! wolvish-ravening lamb!
5713      Despised substance of divinest show!
5714      Just opposite to what thou justly seem'st,
5715      A damned saint, an honourable villain!
5716      O nature, what hadst thou to do in hell,
5717      When thou didst bower the spirit of a fiend
5718      In moral paradise of such sweet flesh?
5719      Was ever book containing such vile matter
5720      So fairly bound? O that deceit should dwell
5721      In such a gorgeous palace!
5722      Nurse
5723      There's no trust,
5724      No faith, no honesty in men; all perjured,
5725      All forsworn, all naught, all dissemblers.
5726      Ah, where's my man? give me some aqua vitae:
5727      These griefs, these woes, these sorrows make me old.
5728      Shame come to Romeo!
5729      JULIET
5730      Blister'd be thy tongue
5731      For such a wish! he was not born to shame:
5732      Upon his brow shame is ashamed to sit;
5733      For 'tis a throne where honour may be crown'd
5734      Sole monarch of the universal earth.
5735      O, what a beast was I to chide at him!
5736      Nurse
5737      Will you speak well of him that kill'd your cousin?
5738      JULIET
5739      Shall I speak ill of him that is my husband?
5740      Ah, poor my lord, what tongue shall smooth thy name,
5741      When I, thy three-hours wife, have mangled it?
5742      But, wherefore, villain, didst thou kill my cousin?
5743      That villain cousin would have kill'd my husband:
5744      Back, foolish tears, back to your native spring;
5745      Your tributary drops belong to woe,
5746      Which you, mistaking, offer up to joy.
5747      My husband lives, that Tybalt would have slain;
5748      And Tybalt's dead, that would have slain my husband:
5749      All this is comfort; wherefore weep I then?
5750      Some word there was, worser than Tybalt's death,
5751      That murder'd me: I would forget it fain;
5752      But, O, it presses to my memory,
5753      Like damned guilty deeds to sinners' minds:
5754      'Tybalt is dead, and Romeo--banished;'
5755      That 'banished,' that one word 'banished,'
5756      Hath slain ten thousand Tybalts. Tybalt's death
5757      Was woe enough, if it had ended there:
5758      Or, if sour woe delights in fellowship
5759      And needly will be rank'd with other griefs,
5760      Why follow'd not, when she said 'Tybalt's dead,'
5761      Thy father, or thy mother, nay, or both,
5762      Which modern lamentations might have moved?
5763      But with a rear-ward following Tybalt's death,
5764      'Romeo is banished,' to speak that word,
5765      Is father, mother, Tybalt, Romeo, Juliet,
5766      All slain, all dead. 'Romeo is banished!'
5767      There is no end, no limit, measure, bound,
5768      In that word's death; no words can that woe sound.
5769      Where is my father, and my mother, nurse?
5770      Nurse
5771      Weeping and wailing over Tybalt's corse:
5772      Will you go to them? I will bring you thither.
5773      JULIET
5774      Wash they his wounds with tears: mine shall be spent,
5775      When theirs are dry, for Romeo's banishment.
5776      Take up those cords: poor ropes, you are beguiled,
5777      Both you and I; for Romeo is exiled:
5778      He made you for a highway to my bed;
5779      But I, a maid, die maiden-widowed.
5780      Come, cords, come, nurse; I'll to my wedding-bed;
5781      And death, not Romeo, take my maidenhead!
5782      Nurse
5783      Hie to your chamber: I'll find Romeo
5784      To comfort you: I wot well where he is.
5785      Hark ye, your Romeo will be here at night:
5786      I'll to him; he is hid at Laurence' cell.
5787      JULIET
5788      O, find him! give this ring to my true knight,
5789      And bid him come to take his last farewell.
5790      Exeunt

5791      SCENE III. Friar Laurence's cell.
5792      Enter FRIAR LAURENCE
5793      FRIAR LAURENCE
5794      Romeo, come forth; come forth, thou fearful man:
5795      Affliction is enamour'd of thy parts,
5796      And thou art wedded to calamity.
5797      Enter ROMEO

5798      ROMEO
5799      Father, what news? what is the prince's doom?
5800      What sorrow craves acquaintance at my hand,
5801      That I yet know not?
5802      FRIAR LAURENCE
5803      Too familiar
5804      Is my dear son with such sour company:
5805      I bring thee tidings of the prince's doom.
5806      ROMEO
5807      What less than dooms-day is the prince's doom?
5808      FRIAR LAURENCE
5809      A gentler judgment vanish'd from his lips,
5810      Not body's death, but body's banishment.
5811      ROMEO
5812      Ha, banishment! be merciful, say 'death;'
5813      For exile hath more terror in his look,
5814      Much more than death: do not say 'banishment.'
5815      FRIAR LAURENCE
5816      Hence from Verona art thou banished:
5817      Be patient, for the world is broad and wide.
5818      ROMEO
5819      There is no world without Verona walls,
5820      But purgatory, torture, hell itself.
5821      Hence-banished is banish'd from the world,
5822      And world's exile is death: then banished,
5823      Is death mis-term'd: calling death banishment,
5824      Thou cutt'st my head off with a golden axe,
5825      And smilest upon the stroke that murders me.
5826      FRIAR LAURENCE
5827      O deadly sin! O rude unthankfulness!
5828      Thy fault our law calls death; but the kind prince,
5829      Taking thy part, hath rush'd aside the law,
5830      And turn'd that black word death to banishment:
5831      This is dear mercy, and thou seest it not.
5832      ROMEO
5833      'Tis torture, and not mercy: heaven is here,
5834      Where Juliet lives; and every cat and dog
5835      And little mouse, every unworthy thing,
5836      Live here in heaven and may look on her;
5837      But Romeo may not: more validity,
5838      More honourable state, more courtship lives
5839      In carrion-flies than Romeo: they my seize
5840      On the white wonder of dear Juliet's hand
5841      And steal immortal blessing from her lips,
5842      Who even in pure and vestal modesty,
5843      Still blush, as thinking their own kisses sin;
5844      But Romeo may not; he is banished:
5845      Flies may do this, but I from this must fly:
5846      They are free men, but I am banished.
5847      And say'st thou yet that exile is not death?
5848      Hadst thou no poison mix'd, no sharp-ground knife,
5849      No sudden mean of death, though ne'er so mean,
5850      But 'banished' to kill me?--'banished'?
5851      O friar, the damned use that word in hell;
5852      Howlings attend it: how hast thou the heart,
5853      Being a divine, a ghostly confessor,
5854      A sin-absolver, and my friend profess'd,
5855      To mangle me with that word 'banished'?
5856      FRIAR LAURENCE
5857      Thou fond mad man, hear me but speak a word.
5858      ROMEO
5859      O, thou wilt speak again of banishment.
5860      FRIAR LAURENCE
5861      I'll give thee armour to keep off that word:
5862      Adversity's sweet milk, philosophy,
5863      To comfort thee, though thou art banished.
5864      ROMEO
5865      Yet 'banished'? Hang up philosophy!
5866      Unless philosophy can make a Juliet,
5867      Displant a town, reverse a prince's doom,
5868      It helps not, it prevails not: talk no more.
5869      FRIAR LAURENCE
5870      O, then I see that madmen have no ears.
5871      ROMEO
5872      How should they, when that wise men have no eyes?
5873      FRIAR LAURENCE
5874      Let me dispute with thee of thy estate.
5875      ROMEO
5876      Thou canst not speak of that thou dost not feel:
5877      Wert thou as young as I, Juliet thy love,
5878      An hour but married, Tybalt murdered,
5879      Doting like me and like me banished,
5880      Then mightst thou speak, then mightst thou tear thy hair,
5881      And fall upon the ground, as I do now,
5882      Taking the measure of an unmade grave.
5883      Knocking within

5884      FRIAR LAURENCE
5885      Arise; one knocks; good Romeo, hide thyself.
5886      ROMEO
5887      Not I; unless the breath of heartsick groans,
5888      Mist-like, infold me from the search of eyes.
5889      Knocking

5890      FRIAR LAURENCE
5891      Hark, how they knock! Who's there? Romeo, arise;
5892      Thou wilt be taken. Stay awhile! Stand up;
5893      Knocking

5894      Run to my study. By and by! God's will,
5895      What simpleness is this! I come, I come!
5896      Knocking

5897      Who knocks so hard? whence come you? what's your will?
5898      Nurse
5899      [Within] Let me come in, and you shall know
5900      my errand;
5901      I come from Lady Juliet.
5902      FRIAR LAURENCE
5903      Welcome, then.
5904      Enter Nurse

5905      Nurse
5906      O holy friar, O, tell me, holy friar,
5907      Where is my lady's lord, where's Romeo?
5908      FRIAR LAURENCE
5909      There on the ground, with his own tears made drunk.
5910      Nurse
5911      O, he is even in my mistress' case,
5912      Just in her case! O woful sympathy!
5913      Piteous predicament! Even so lies she,
5914      Blubbering and weeping, weeping and blubbering.
5915      Stand up, stand up; stand, and you be a man:
5916      For Juliet's sake, for her sake, rise and stand;
5917      Why should you fall into so deep an O?
5918      ROMEO
5919      Nurse!
5920      Nurse
5921      Ah sir! ah sir! Well, death's the end of all.
5922      ROMEO
5923      Spakest thou of Juliet? how is it with her?
5924      Doth she not think me an old murderer,
5925      Now I have stain'd the childhood of our joy
5926      With blood removed but little from her own?
5927      Where is she? and how doth she? and what says
5928      My conceal'd lady to our cancell'd love?
5929      Nurse
5930      O, she says nothing, sir, but weeps and weeps;
5931      And now falls on her bed; and then starts up,
5932      And Tybalt calls; and then on Romeo cries,
5933      And then down falls again.
5934      ROMEO
5935      As if that name,
5936      Shot from the deadly level of a gun,
5937      Did murder her; as that name's cursed hand
5938      Murder'd her kinsman. O, tell me, friar, tell me,
5939      In what vile part of this anatomy
5940      Doth my name lodge? tell me, that I may sack
5941      The hateful mansion.
5942      Drawing his sword

5943      FRIAR LAURENCE
5944      Hold thy desperate hand:
5945      Art thou a man? thy form cries out thou art:
5946      Thy tears are womanish; thy wild acts denote
5947      The unreasonable fury of a beast:
5948      Unseemly woman in a seeming man!
5949      Or ill-beseeming beast in seeming both!
5950      Thou hast amazed me: by my holy order,
5951      I thought thy disposition better temper'd.
5952      Hast thou slain Tybalt? wilt thou slay thyself?
5953      And stay thy lady too that lives in thee,
5954      By doing damned hate upon thyself?
5955      Why rail'st thou on thy birth, the heaven, and earth?
5956      Since birth, and heaven, and earth, all three do meet
5957      In thee at once; which thou at once wouldst lose.
5958      Fie, fie, thou shamest thy shape, thy love, thy wit;
5959      Which, like a usurer, abound'st in all,
5960      And usest none in that true use indeed
5961      Which should bedeck thy shape, thy love, thy wit:
5962      Thy noble shape is but a form of wax,
5963      Digressing from the valour of a man;
5964      Thy dear love sworn but hollow perjury,
5965      Killing that love which thou hast vow'd to cherish;
5966      Thy wit, that ornament to shape and love,
5967      Misshapen in the conduct of them both,
5968      Like powder in a skitless soldier's flask,
5969      Is set afire by thine own ignorance,
5970      And thou dismember'd with thine own defence.
5971      What, rouse thee, man! thy Juliet is alive,
5972      For whose dear sake thou wast but lately dead;
5973      There art thou happy: Tybalt would kill thee,
5974      But thou slew'st Tybalt; there are thou happy too:
5975      The law that threaten'd death becomes thy friend
5976      And turns it to exile; there art thou happy:
5977      A pack of blessings lights up upon thy back;
5978      Happiness courts thee in her best array;
5979      But, like a misbehaved and sullen wench,
5980      Thou pout'st upon thy fortune and thy love:
5981      Take heed, take heed, for such die miserable.
5982      Go, get thee to thy love, as was decreed,
5983      Ascend her chamber, hence and comfort her:
5984      But look thou stay not till the watch be set,
5985      For then thou canst not pass to Mantua;
5986      Where thou shalt live, till we can find a time
5987      To blaze your marriage, reconcile your friends,
5988      Beg pardon of the prince, and call thee back
5989      With twenty hundred thousand times more joy
5990      Than thou went'st forth in lamentation.
5991      Go before, nurse: commend me to thy lady;
5992      And bid her hasten all the house to bed,
5993      Which heavy sorrow makes them apt unto:
5994      Romeo is coming.
5995      Nurse
5996      O Lord, I could have stay'd here all the night
5997      To hear good counsel: O, what learning is!
5998      My lord, I'll tell my lady you will come.
5999      ROMEO
6000      Do so, and bid my sweet prepare to chide.
6001      Nurse
6002      Here, sir, a ring she bid me give you, sir:
6003      Hie you, make haste, for it grows very late.
6004      Exit

6005      ROMEO
6006      How well my comfort is revived by this!
6007      FRIAR LAURENCE
6008      Go hence; good night; and here stands all your state:
6009      Either be gone before the watch be set,
6010      Or by the break of day disguised from hence:
6011      Sojourn in Mantua; I'll find out your man,
6012      And he shall signify from time to time
6013      Every good hap to you that chances here:
6014      Give me thy hand; 'tis late: farewell; good night.
6015      ROMEO
6016      But that a joy past joy calls out on me,
6017      It were a grief, so brief to part with thee: Farewell.
6018      Exeunt

6019      SCENE IV. A room in Capulet's house.
6020      Enter CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, and PARIS
6021      CAPULET
6022      Things have fall'n out, sir, so unluckily,
6023      That we have had no time to move our daughter:
6024      Look you, she loved her kinsman Tybalt dearly,
6025      And so did I:--Well, we were born to die.
6026      'Tis very late, she'll not come down to-night:
6027      I promise you, but for your company,
6028      I would have been a-bed an hour ago.
6029      PARIS
6030      These times of woe afford no time to woo.
6031      Madam, good night: commend me to your daughter.
6032      LADY CAPULET
6033      I will, and know her mind early to-morrow;
6034      To-night she is mew'd up to her heaviness.
6035      CAPULET
6036      Sir Paris, I will make a desperate tender
6037      Of my child's love: I think she will be ruled
6038      In all respects by me; nay, more, I doubt it not.
6039      Wife, go you to her ere you go to bed;
6040      Acquaint her here of my son Paris' love;
6041      And bid her, mark you me, on Wednesday next--
6042      But, soft! what day is this?
6043      PARIS
6044      Monday, my lord,
6045      CAPULET
6046      Monday! ha, ha! Well, Wednesday is too soon,
6047      O' Thursday let it be: o' Thursday, tell her,
6048      She shall be married to this noble earl.
6049      Will you be ready? do you like this haste?
6050      We'll keep no great ado,--a friend or two;
6051      For, hark you, Tybalt being slain so late,
6052      It may be thought we held him carelessly,
6053      Being our kinsman, if we revel much:
6054      Therefore we'll have some half a dozen friends,
6055      And there an end. But what say you to Thursday?
6056      PARIS
6057      My lord, I would that Thursday were to-morrow.
6058      CAPULET
6059      Well get you gone: o' Thursday be it, then.
6060      Go you to Juliet ere you go to bed,
6061      Prepare her, wife, against this wedding-day.
6062      Farewell, my lord. Light to my chamber, ho!
6063      Afore me! it is so very very late,
6064      That we may call it early by and by.
6065      Good night.
6066      Exeunt

6067      SCENE V. Capulet's orchard.
6068      Enter ROMEO and JULIET above, at the window
6069      JULIET
6070      Wilt thou be gone? it is not yet near day:
6071      It was the nightingale, and not the lark,
6072      That pierced the fearful hollow of thine ear;
6073      Nightly she sings on yon pomegranate-tree:
6074      Believe me, love, it was the nightingale.
6075      ROMEO
6076      It was the lark, the herald of the morn,
6077      No nightingale: look, love, what envious streaks
6078      Do lace the severing clouds in yonder east:
6079      Night's candles are burnt out, and jocund day
6080      Stands tiptoe on the misty mountain tops.
6081      I must be gone and live, or stay and die.
6082      JULIET
6083      Yon light is not day-light, I know it, I:
6084      It is some meteor that the sun exhales,
6085      To be to thee this night a torch-bearer,
6086      And light thee on thy way to Mantua:
6087      Therefore stay yet; thou need'st not to be gone.
6088      ROMEO
6089      Let me be ta'en, let me be put to death;
6090      I am content, so thou wilt have it so.
6091      I'll say yon grey is not the morning's eye,
6092      'Tis but the pale reflex of Cynthia's brow;
6093      Nor that is not the lark, whose notes do beat
6094      The vaulty heaven so high above our heads:
6095      I have more care to stay than will to go:
6096      Come, death, and welcome! Juliet wills it so.
6097      How is't, my soul? let's talk; it is not day.
6098      JULIET
6099      It is, it is: hie hence, be gone, away!
6100      It is the lark that sings so out of tune,
6101      Straining harsh discords and unpleasing sharps.
6102      Some say the lark makes sweet division;
6103      This doth not so, for she divideth us:
6104      Some say the lark and loathed toad change eyes,
6105      O, now I would they had changed voices too!
6106      Since arm from arm that voice doth us affray,
6107      Hunting thee hence with hunt's-up to the day,
6108      O, now be gone; more light and light it grows.
6109      ROMEO
6110      More light and light; more dark and dark our woes!
6111      Enter Nurse, to the chamber

6112      Nurse
6113      Madam!
6114      JULIET
6115      Nurse?
6116      Nurse
6117      Your lady mother is coming to your chamber:
6118      The day is broke; be wary, look about.
6119      Exit

6120      JULIET
6121      Then, window, let day in, and let life out.
6122      ROMEO
6123      Farewell, farewell! one kiss, and I'll descend.
6124      He goeth down

6125      JULIET
6126      Art thou gone so? love, lord, ay, husband, friend!
6127      I must hear from thee every day in the hour,
6128      For in a minute there are many days:
6129      O, by this count I shall be much in years
6130      Ere I again behold my Romeo!
6131      ROMEO
6132      Farewell!
6133      I will omit no opportunity
6134      That may convey my greetings, love, to thee.
6135      JULIET
6136      O think'st thou we shall ever meet again?
6137      ROMEO
6138      I doubt it not; and all these woes shall serve
6139      For sweet discourses in our time to come.
6140      JULIET
6141      O God, I have an ill-divining soul!
6142      Methinks I see thee, now thou art below,
6143      As one dead in the bottom of a tomb:
6144      Either my eyesight fails, or thou look'st pale.
6145      ROMEO
6146      And trust me, love, in my eye so do you:
6147      Dry sorrow drinks our blood. Adieu, adieu!
6148      Exit

6149      JULIET
6150      O fortune, fortune! all men call thee fickle:
6151      If thou art fickle, what dost thou with him.
6152      That is renown'd for faith? Be fickle, fortune;
6153      For then, I hope, thou wilt not keep him long,
6154      But send him back.
6155      LADY CAPULET
6156      [Within] Ho, daughter! are you up?
6157      JULIET
6158      Who is't that calls? is it my lady mother?
6159      Is she not down so late, or up so early?
6160      What unaccustom'd cause procures her hither?
6161      Enter LADY CAPULET

6162      LADY CAPULET
6163      Why, how now, Juliet!
6164      JULIET
6165      Madam, I am not well.
6166      LADY CAPULET
6167      Evermore weeping for your cousin's death?
6168      What, wilt thou wash him from his grave with tears?
6169      An if thou couldst, thou couldst not make him live;
6170      Therefore, have done: some grief shows much of love;
6171      But much of grief shows still some want of wit.
6172      JULIET
6173      Yet let me weep for such a feeling loss.
6174      LADY CAPULET
6175      So shall you feel the loss, but not the friend
6176      Which you weep for.
6177      JULIET
6178      Feeling so the loss,
6179      Cannot choose but ever weep the friend.
6180      LADY CAPULET
6181      Well, girl, thou weep'st not so much for his death,
6182      As that the villain lives which slaughter'd him.
6183      JULIET
6184      What villain madam?
6185      LADY CAPULET
6186      That same villain, Romeo.
6187      JULIET
6188      [Aside] Villain and he be many miles asunder.--
6189      God Pardon him! I do, with all my heart;
6190      And yet no man like he doth grieve my heart.
6191      LADY CAPULET
6192      That is, because the traitor murderer lives.
6193      JULIET
6194      Ay, madam, from the reach of these my hands:
6195      Would none but I might venge my cousin's death!
6196      LADY CAPULET
6197      We will have vengeance for it, fear thou not:
6198      Then weep no more. I'll send to one in Mantua,
6199      Where that same banish'd runagate doth live,
6200      Shall give him such an unaccustom'd dram,
6201      That he shall soon keep Tybalt company:
6202      And then, I hope, thou wilt be satisfied.
6203      JULIET
6204      Indeed, I never shall be satisfied
6205      With Romeo, till I behold him--dead--
6206      Is my poor heart for a kinsman vex'd.
6207      Madam, if you could find out but a man
6208      To bear a poison, I would temper it;
6209      That Romeo should, upon receipt thereof,
6210      Soon sleep in quiet. O, how my heart abhors
6211      To hear him named, and cannot come to him.
6212      To wreak the love I bore my cousin
6213      Upon his body that slaughter'd him!
6214      LADY CAPULET
6215      Find thou the means, and I'll find such a man.
6216      But now I'll tell thee joyful tidings, girl.
6217      JULIET
6218      And joy comes well in such a needy time:
6219      What are they, I beseech your ladyship?
6220      LADY CAPULET
6221      Well, well, thou hast a careful father, child;
6222      One who, to put thee from thy heaviness,
6223      Hath sorted out a sudden day of joy,
6224      That thou expect'st not nor I look'd not for.
6225      JULIET
6226      Madam, in happy time, what day is that?
6227      LADY CAPULET
6228      Marry, my child, early next Thursday morn,
6229      The gallant, young and noble gentleman,
6230      The County Paris, at Saint Peter's Church,
6231      Shall happily make thee there a joyful bride.
6232      JULIET
6233      Now, by Saint Peter's Church and Peter too,
6234      He shall not make me there a joyful bride.
6235      I wonder at this haste; that I must wed
6236      Ere he, that should be husband, comes to woo.
6237      I pray you, tell my lord and father, madam,
6238      I will not marry yet; and, when I do, I swear,
6239      It shall be Romeo, whom you know I hate,
6240      Rather than Paris. These are news indeed!
6241      LADY CAPULET
6242      Here comes your father; tell him so yourself,
6243      And see how he will take it at your hands.
6244      Enter CAPULET and Nurse

6245      CAPULET
6246      When the sun sets, the air doth drizzle dew;
6247      But for the sunset of my brother's son
6248      It rains downright.
6249      How now! a conduit, girl? what, still in tears?
6250      Evermore showering? In one little body
6251      Thou counterfeit'st a bark, a sea, a wind;
6252      For still thy eyes, which I may call the sea,
6253      Do ebb and flow with tears; the bark thy body is,
6254      Sailing in this salt flood; the winds, thy sighs;
6255      Who, raging with thy tears, and they with them,
6256      Without a sudden calm, will overset
6257      Thy tempest-tossed body. How now, wife!
6258      Have you deliver'd to her our decree?
6259      LADY CAPULET
6260      Ay, sir; but she will none, she gives you thanks.
6261      I would the fool were married to her grave!
6262      CAPULET
6263      Soft! take me with you, take me with you, wife.
6264      How! will she none? doth she not give us thanks?
6265      Is she not proud? doth she not count her blest,
6266      Unworthy as she is, that we have wrought
6267      So worthy a gentleman to be her bridegroom?
6268      JULIET
6269      Not proud, you have; but thankful, that you have:
6270      Proud can I never be of what I hate;
6271      But thankful even for hate, that is meant love.
6272      CAPULET
6273      How now, how now, chop-logic! What is this?
6274      'Proud,' and 'I thank you,' and 'I thank you not;'
6275      And yet 'not proud,' mistress minion, you,
6276      Thank me no thankings, nor, proud me no prouds,
6277      But fettle your fine joints 'gainst Thursday next,
6278      To go with Paris to Saint Peter's Church,
6279      Or I will drag thee on a hurdle thither.
6280      Out, you green-sickness carrion! out, you baggage!
6281      You tallow-face!
6282      LADY CAPULET
6283      Fie, fie! what, are you mad?
6284      JULIET
6285      Good father, I beseech you on my knees,
6286      Hear me with patience but to speak a word.
6287      CAPULET
6288      Hang thee, young baggage! disobedient wretch!
6289      I tell thee what: get thee to church o' Thursday,
6290      Or never after look me in the face:
6291      Speak not, reply not, do not answer me;
6292      My fingers itch. Wife, we scarce thought us blest
6293      That God had lent us but this only child;
6294      But now I see this one is one too much,
6295      And that we have a curse in having her:
6296      Out on her, hilding!
6297      Nurse
6298      God in heaven bless her!
6299      You are to blame, my lord, to rate her so.
6300      CAPULET
6301      And why, my lady wisdom? hold your tongue,
6302      Good prudence; smatter with your gossips, go.
6303      Nurse
6304      I speak no treason.
6305      CAPULET
6306      O, God ye god-den.
6307      Nurse
6308      May not one speak?
6309      CAPULET
6310      Peace, you mumbling fool!
6311      Utter your gravity o'er a gossip's bowl;
6312      For here we need it not.
6313      LADY CAPULET
6314      You are too hot.
6315      CAPULET
6316      God's bread! it makes me mad:
6317      Day, night, hour, tide, time, work, play,
6318      Alone, in company, still my care hath been
6319      To have her match'd: and having now provided
6320      A gentleman of noble parentage,
6321      Of fair demesnes, youthful, and nobly train'd,
6322      Stuff'd, as they say, with honourable parts,
6323      Proportion'd as one's thought would wish a man;
6324      And then to have a wretched puling fool,
6325      A whining mammet, in her fortune's tender,
6326      To answer 'I'll not wed; I cannot love,
6327      I am too young; I pray you, pardon me.'
6328      But, as you will not wed, I'll pardon you:
6329      Graze where you will you shall not house with me:
6330      Look to't, think on't, I do not use to jest.
6331      Thursday is near; lay hand on heart, advise:
6332      An you be mine, I'll give you to my friend;
6333      And you be not, hang, beg, starve, die in
6334      the streets,
6335      For, by my soul, I'll ne'er acknowledge thee,
6336      Nor what is mine shall never do thee good:
6337      Trust to't, bethink you; I'll not be forsworn.
6338      Exit

6339      JULIET
6340      Is there no pity sitting in the clouds,
6341      That sees into the bottom of my grief?
6342      O, sweet my mother, cast me not away!
6343      Delay this marriage for a month, a week;
6344      Or, if you do not, make the bridal bed
6345      In that dim monument where Tybalt lies.
6346      LADY CAPULET
6347      Talk not to me, for I'll not speak a word:
6348      Do as thou wilt, for I have done with thee.
6349      Exit

6350      JULIET
6351      O God!--O nurse, how shall this be prevented?
6352      My husband is on earth, my faith in heaven;
6353      How shall that faith return again to earth,
6354      Unless that husband send it me from heaven
6355      By leaving earth? comfort me, counsel me.
6356      Alack, alack, that heaven should practise stratagems
