6357      Upon so soft a subject as myself!
6358      What say'st thou? hast thou not a word of joy?
6359      Some comfort, nurse.
6360      Nurse
6361      Faith, here it is.
6362      Romeo is banish'd; and all the world to nothing,
6363      That he dares ne'er come back to challenge you;
6364      Or, if he do, it needs must be by stealth.
6365      Then, since the case so stands as now it doth,
6366      I think it best you married with the county.
6367      O, he's a lovely gentleman!
6368      Romeo's a dishclout to him: an eagle, madam,
6369      Hath not so green, so quick, so fair an eye
6370      As Paris hath. Beshrew my very heart,
6371      I think you are happy in this second match,
6372      For it excels your first: or if it did not,
6373      Your first is dead; or 'twere as good he were,
6374      As living here and you no use of him.
6375      JULIET
6376      Speakest thou from thy heart?
6377      Nurse
6378      And from my soul too;
6379      Or else beshrew them both.
6380      JULIET
6381      Amen!
6382      Nurse
6383      What?
6384      JULIET
6385      Well, thou hast comforted me marvellous much.
6386      Go in: and tell my lady I am gone,
6387      Having displeased my father, to Laurence' cell,
6388      To make confession and to be absolved.
6389      Nurse
6390      Marry, I will; and this is wisely done.
6391      Exit

6392      JULIET
6393      Ancient damnation! O most wicked fiend!
6394      Is it more sin to wish me thus forsworn,
6395      Or to dispraise my lord with that same tongue
6396      Which she hath praised him with above compare
6397      So many thousand times? Go, counsellor;
6398      Thou and my bosom henceforth shall be twain.
6399      I'll to the friar, to know his remedy:
6400      If all else fail, myself have power to die.
6401      Exit

6402      ACT IV
6403      SCENE I. Friar Laurence's cell.
6404      Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and PARIS
6405      FRIAR LAURENCE
6406      On Thursday, sir? the time is very short.
6407      PARIS
6408      My father Capulet will have it so;
6409      And I am nothing slow to slack his haste.
6410      FRIAR LAURENCE
6411      You say you do not know the lady's mind:
6412      Uneven is the course, I like it not.
6413      PARIS
6414      Immoderately she weeps for Tybalt's death,
6415      And therefore have I little talk'd of love;
6416      For Venus smiles not in a house of tears.
6417      Now, sir, her father counts it dangerous
6418      That she doth give her sorrow so much sway,
6419      And in his wisdom hastes our marriage,
6420      To stop the inundation of her tears;
6421      Which, too much minded by herself alone,
6422      May be put from her by society:
6423      Now do you know the reason of this haste.
6424      FRIAR LAURENCE
6425      [Aside] I would I knew not why it should be slow'd.
6426      Look, sir, here comes the lady towards my cell.
6427      Enter JULIET

6428      PARIS
6429      Happily met, my lady and my wife!
6430      JULIET
6431      That may be, sir, when I may be a wife.
6432      PARIS
6433      That may be must be, love, on Thursday next.
6434      JULIET
6435      What must be shall be.
6436      FRIAR LAURENCE
6437      That's a certain text.
6438      PARIS
6439      Come you to make confession to this father?
6440      JULIET
6441      To answer that, I should confess to you.
6442      PARIS
6443      Do not deny to him that you love me.
6444      JULIET
6445      I will confess to you that I love him.
6446      PARIS
6447      So will ye, I am sure, that you love me.
6448      JULIET
6449      If I do so, it will be of more price,
6450      Being spoke behind your back, than to your face.
6451      PARIS
6452      Poor soul, thy face is much abused with tears.
6453      JULIET
6454      The tears have got small victory by that;
6455      For it was bad enough before their spite.
6456      PARIS
6457      Thou wrong'st it, more than tears, with that report.
6458      JULIET
6459      That is no slander, sir, which is a truth;
6460      And what I spake, I spake it to my face.
6461      PARIS
6462      Thy face is mine, and thou hast slander'd it.
6463      JULIET
6464      It may be so, for it is not mine own.
6465      Are you at leisure, holy father, now;
6466      Or shall I come to you at evening mass?
6467      FRIAR LAURENCE
6468      My leisure serves me, pensive daughter, now.
6469      My lord, we must entreat the time alone.
6470      PARIS
6471      God shield I should disturb devotion!
6472      Juliet, on Thursday early will I rouse ye:
6473      Till then, adieu; and keep this holy kiss.
6474      Exit

6475      JULIET
6476      O shut the door! and when thou hast done so,
6477      Come weep with me; past hope, past cure, past help!
6478      FRIAR LAURENCE
6479      Ah, Juliet, I already know thy grief;
6480      It strains me past the compass of my wits:
6481      I hear thou must, and nothing may prorogue it,
6482      On Thursday next be married to this county.
6483      JULIET
6484      Tell me not, friar, that thou hear'st of this,
6485      Unless thou tell me how I may prevent it:
6486      If, in thy wisdom, thou canst give no help,
6487      Do thou but call my resolution wise,
6488      And with this knife I'll help it presently.
6489      God join'd my heart and Romeo's, thou our hands;
6490      And ere this hand, by thee to Romeo seal'd,
6491      Shall be the label to another deed,
6492      Or my true heart with treacherous revolt
6493      Turn to another, this shall slay them both:
6494      Therefore, out of thy long-experienced time,
6495      Give me some present counsel, or, behold,
6496      'Twixt my extremes and me this bloody knife
6497      Shall play the umpire, arbitrating that
6498      Which the commission of thy years and art
6499      Could to no issue of true honour bring.
6500      Be not so long to speak; I long to die,
6501      If what thou speak'st speak not of remedy.
6502      FRIAR LAURENCE
6503      Hold, daughter: I do spy a kind of hope,
6504      Which craves as desperate an execution.
6505      As that is desperate which we would prevent.
6506      If, rather than to marry County Paris,
6507      Thou hast the strength of will to slay thyself,
6508      Then is it likely thou wilt undertake
6509      A thing like death to chide away this shame,
6510      That copest with death himself to scape from it:
6511      And, if thou darest, I'll give thee remedy.
6512      JULIET
6513      O, bid me leap, rather than marry Paris,
6514      From off the battlements of yonder tower;
6515      Or walk in thievish ways; or bid me lurk
6516      Where serpents are; chain me with roaring bears;
6517      Or shut me nightly in a charnel-house,
6518      O'er-cover'd quite with dead men's rattling bones,
6519      With reeky shanks and yellow chapless skulls;
6520      Or bid me go into a new-made grave
6521      And hide me with a dead man in his shroud;
6522      Things that, to hear them told, have made me tremble;
6523      And I will do it without fear or doubt,
6524      To live an unstain'd wife to my sweet love.
6525      FRIAR LAURENCE
6526      Hold, then; go home, be merry, give consent
6527      To marry Paris: Wednesday is to-morrow:
6528      To-morrow night look that thou lie alone;
6529      Let not thy nurse lie with thee in thy chamber:
6530      Take thou this vial, being then in bed,
6531      And this distilled liquor drink thou off;
6532      When presently through all thy veins shall run
6533      A cold and drowsy humour, for no pulse
6534      Shall keep his native progress, but surcease:
6535      No warmth, no breath, shall testify thou livest;
6536      The roses in thy lips and cheeks shall fade
6537      To paly ashes, thy eyes' windows fall,
6538      Like death, when he shuts up the day of life;
6539      Each part, deprived of supple government,
6540      Shall, stiff and stark and cold, appear like death:
6541      And in this borrow'd likeness of shrunk death
6542      Thou shalt continue two and forty hours,
6543      And then awake as from a pleasant sleep.
6544      Now, when the bridegroom in the morning comes
6545      To rouse thee from thy bed, there art thou dead:
6546      Then, as the manner of our country is,
6547      In thy best robes uncover'd on the bier
6548      Thou shalt be borne to that same ancient vault
6549      Where all the kindred of the Capulets lie.
6550      In the mean time, against thou shalt awake,
6551      Shall Romeo by my letters know our drift,
6552      And hither shall he come: and he and I
6553      Will watch thy waking, and that very night
6554      Shall Romeo bear thee hence to Mantua.
6555      And this shall free thee from this present shame;
6556      If no inconstant toy, nor womanish fear,
6557      Abate thy valour in the acting it.
6558      JULIET
6559      Give me, give me! O, tell not me of fear!
6560      FRIAR LAURENCE
6561      Hold; get you gone, be strong and prosperous
6562      In this resolve: I'll send a friar with speed
6563      To Mantua, with my letters to thy lord.
6564      JULIET
6565      Love give me strength! and strength shall help afford.
6566      Farewell, dear father!
6567      Exeunt

6568      SCENE II. Hall in Capulet's house.
6569      Enter CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, Nurse, and two Servingmen
6570      CAPULET
6571      So many guests invite as here are writ.
6572      Exit First Servant

6573      Sirrah, go hire me twenty cunning cooks.
6574      Second Servant
6575      You shall have none ill, sir; for I'll try if they
6576      can lick their fingers.
6577      CAPULET
6578      How canst thou try them so?
6579      Second Servant
6580      Marry, sir, 'tis an ill cook that cannot lick his
6581      own fingers: therefore he that cannot lick his
6582      fingers goes not with me.
6583      CAPULET
6584      Go, be gone.
6585      Exit Second Servant

6586      We shall be much unfurnished for this time.
6587      What, is my daughter gone to Friar Laurence?
6588      Nurse
6589      Ay, forsooth.
6590      CAPULET
6591      Well, he may chance to do some good on her:
6592      A peevish self-will'd harlotry it is.
6593      Nurse
6594      See where she comes from shrift with merry look.
6595      Enter JULIET

6596      CAPULET
6597      How now, my headstrong! where have you been gadding?
6598      JULIET
6599      Where I have learn'd me to repent the sin
6600      Of disobedient opposition
6601      To you and your behests, and am enjoin'd
6602      By holy Laurence to fall prostrate here,
6603      And beg your pardon: pardon, I beseech you!
6604      Henceforward I am ever ruled by you.
6605      CAPULET
6606      Send for the county; go tell him of this:
6607      I'll have this knot knit up to-morrow morning.
6608      JULIET
6609      I met the youthful lord at Laurence' cell;
6610      And gave him what becomed love I might,
6611      Not step o'er the bounds of modesty.
6612      CAPULET
6613      Why, I am glad on't; this is well: stand up:
6614      This is as't should be. Let me see the county;
6615      Ay, marry, go, I say, and fetch him hither.
6616      Now, afore God! this reverend holy friar,
6617      Our whole city is much bound to him.
6618      JULIET
6619      Nurse, will you go with me into my closet,
6620      To help me sort such needful ornaments
6621      As you think fit to furnish me to-morrow?
6622      LADY CAPULET
6623      No, not till Thursday; there is time enough.
6624      CAPULET
6625      Go, nurse, go with her: we'll to church to-morrow.
6626      Exeunt JULIET and Nurse

6627      LADY CAPULET
6628      We shall be short in our provision:
6629      'Tis now near night.
6630      CAPULET
6631      Tush, I will stir about,
6632      And all things shall be well, I warrant thee, wife:
6633      Go thou to Juliet, help to deck up her;
6634      I'll not to bed to-night; let me alone;
6635      I'll play the housewife for this once. What, ho!
6636      They are all forth. Well, I will walk myself
6637      To County Paris, to prepare him up
6638      Against to-morrow: my heart is wondrous light,
6639      Since this same wayward girl is so reclaim'd.
6640      Exeunt

6641      SCENE III. Juliet's chamber.
6642      Enter JULIET and Nurse
6643      JULIET
6644      Ay, those attires are best: but, gentle nurse,
6645      I pray thee, leave me to my self to-night,
6646      For I have need of many orisons
6647      To move the heavens to smile upon my state,
6648      Which, well thou know'st, is cross, and full of sin.
6649      Enter LADY CAPULET

6650      LADY CAPULET
6651      What, are you busy, ho? need you my help?
6652      JULIET
6653      No, madam; we have cull'd such necessaries
6654      As are behoveful for our state to-morrow:
6655      So please you, let me now be left alone,
6656      And let the nurse this night sit up with you;
6657      For, I am sure, you have your hands full all,
6658      In this so sudden business.
6659      LADY CAPULET
6660      Good night:
6661      Get thee to bed, and rest; for thou hast need.
6662      Exeunt LADY CAPULET and Nurse

6663      JULIET
6664      Farewell! God knows when we shall meet again.
6665      I have a faint cold fear thrills through my veins,
6666      That almost freezes up the heat of life:
6667      I'll call them back again to comfort me:
6668      Nurse! What should she do here?
6669      My dismal scene I needs must act alone.
6670      Come, vial.
6671      What if this mixture do not work at all?
6672      Shall I be married then to-morrow morning?
6673      No, no: this shall forbid it: lie thou there.
6674      Laying down her dagger

6675      What if it be a poison, which the friar
6676      Subtly hath minister'd to have me dead,
6677      Lest in this marriage he should be dishonour'd,
6678      Because he married me before to Romeo?
6679      I fear it is: and yet, methinks, it should not,
6680      For he hath still been tried a holy man.
6681      How if, when I am laid into the tomb,
6682      I wake before the time that Romeo
6683      Come to redeem me? there's a fearful point!
6684      Shall I not, then, be stifled in the vault,
6685      To whose foul mouth no healthsome air breathes in,
6686      And there die strangled ere my Romeo comes?
6687      Or, if I live, is it not very like,
6688      The horrible conceit of death and night,
6689      Together with the terror of the place,--
6690      As in a vault, an ancient receptacle,
6691      Where, for these many hundred years, the bones
6692      Of all my buried ancestors are packed:
6693      Where bloody Tybalt, yet but green in earth,
6694      Lies festering in his shroud; where, as they say,
6695      At some hours in the night spirits resort;--
6696      Alack, alack, is it not like that I,
6697      So early waking, what with loathsome smells,
6698      And shrieks like mandrakes' torn out of the earth,
6699      That living mortals, hearing them, run mad:--
6700      O, if I wake, shall I not be distraught,
6701      Environed with all these hideous fears?
6702      And madly play with my forefather's joints?
6703      And pluck the mangled Tybalt from his shroud?
6704      And, in this rage, with some great kinsman's bone,
6705      As with a club, dash out my desperate brains?
6706      O, look! methinks I see my cousin's ghost
6707      Seeking out Romeo, that did spit his body
6708      Upon a rapier's point: stay, Tybalt, stay!
6709      Romeo, I come! this do I drink to thee.
6710      She falls upon her bed, within the curtains

6711      SCENE IV. Hall in Capulet's house.
6712      Enter LADY CAPULET and Nurse
6713      LADY CAPULET
6714      Hold, take these keys, and fetch more spices, nurse.
6715      Nurse
6716      They call for dates and quinces in the pastry.
6717      Enter CAPULET

6718      CAPULET
6719      Come, stir, stir, stir! the second cock hath crow'd,
6720      The curfew-bell hath rung, 'tis three o'clock:
6721      Look to the baked meats, good Angelica:
6722      Spare not for the cost.
6723      Nurse
6724      Go, you cot-quean, go,
6725      Get you to bed; faith, You'll be sick to-morrow
6726      For this night's watching.
6727      CAPULET
6728      No, not a whit: what! I have watch'd ere now
6729      All night for lesser cause, and ne'er been sick.
6730      LADY CAPULET
6731      Ay, you have been a mouse-hunt in your time;
6732      But I will watch you from such watching now.
6733      Exeunt LADY CAPULET and Nurse

6734      CAPULET
6735      A jealous hood, a jealous hood!
6736      Enter three or four Servingmen, with spits, logs, and baskets

6737      Now, fellow,
6738      What's there?
6739      First Servant
6740      Things for the cook, sir; but I know not what.
6741      CAPULET
6742      Make haste, make haste.
6743      Exit First Servant

6744      Sirrah, fetch drier logs:
6745      Call Peter, he will show thee where they are.
6746      Second Servant
6747      I have a head, sir, that will find out logs,
6748      And never trouble Peter for the matter.
6749      Exit

6750      CAPULET
6751      Mass, and well said; a merry whoreson, ha!
6752      Thou shalt be logger-head. Good faith, 'tis day:
6753      The county will be here with music straight,
6754      For so he said he would: I hear him near.
6755      Music within

6756      Nurse! Wife! What, ho! What, nurse, I say!
6757      Re-enter Nurse

6758      Go waken Juliet, go and trim her up;
6759      I'll go and chat with Paris: hie, make haste,
6760      Make haste; the bridegroom he is come already:
6761      Make haste, I say.
6762      Exeunt

6763      SCENE V. Juliet's chamber.
6764      Enter Nurse
6765      Nurse
6766      Mistress! what, mistress! Juliet! fast, I warrant her, she:
6767      Why, lamb! why, lady! fie, you slug-a-bed!
6768      Why, love, I say! madam! sweet-heart! why, bride!
6769      What, not a word? you take your pennyworths now;
6770      Sleep for a week; for the next night, I warrant,
6771      The County Paris hath set up his rest,
6772      That you shall rest but little. God forgive me,
6773      Marry, and amen, how sound is she asleep!
6774      I must needs wake her. Madam, madam, madam!
6775      Ay, let the county take you in your bed;
6776      He'll fright you up, i' faith. Will it not be?
6777      Undraws the curtains

6778      What, dress'd! and in your clothes! and down again!
6779      I must needs wake you; Lady! lady! lady!
6780      Alas, alas! Help, help! my lady's dead!
6781      O, well-a-day, that ever I was born!
6782      Some aqua vitae, ho! My lord! my lady!
6783      Enter LADY CAPULET

6784      LADY CAPULET
6785      What noise is here?
6786      Nurse
6787      O lamentable day!
6788      LADY CAPULET
6789      What is the matter?
6790      Nurse
6791      Look, look! O heavy day!
6792      LADY CAPULET
6793      O me, O me! My child, my only life,
6794      Revive, look up, or I will die with thee!
6795      Help, help! Call help.
6796      Enter CAPULET

6797      CAPULET
6798      For shame, bring Juliet forth; her lord is come.
6799      Nurse
6800      She's dead, deceased, she's dead; alack the day!
6801      LADY CAPULET
6802      Alack the day, she's dead, she's dead, she's dead!
6803      CAPULET
6804      Ha! let me see her: out, alas! she's cold:
6805      Her blood is settled, and her joints are stiff;
6806      Life and these lips have long been separated:
6807      Death lies on her like an untimely frost
6808      Upon the sweetest flower of all the field.
6809      Nurse
6810      O lamentable day!
6811      LADY CAPULET
6812      O woful time!
6813      CAPULET
6814      Death, that hath ta'en her hence to make me wail,
6815      Ties up my tongue, and will not let me speak.
6816      Enter FRIAR LAURENCE and PARIS, with Musicians

6817      FRIAR LAURENCE
6818      Come, is the bride ready to go to church?
6819      CAPULET
6820      Ready to go, but never to return.
6821      O son! the night before thy wedding-day
6822      Hath Death lain with thy wife. There she lies,
6823      Flower as she was, deflowered by him.
6824      Death is my son-in-law, Death is my heir;
6825      My daughter he hath wedded: I will die,
6826      And leave him all; life, living, all is Death's.
6827      PARIS
6828      Have I thought long to see this morning's face,
6829      And doth it give me such a sight as this?
6830      LADY CAPULET
6831      Accursed, unhappy, wretched, hateful day!
6832      Most miserable hour that e'er time saw
6833      In lasting labour of his pilgrimage!
6834      But one, poor one, one poor and loving child,
6835      But one thing to rejoice and solace in,
6836      And cruel death hath catch'd it from my sight!
6837      Nurse
6838      O woe! O woful, woful, woful day!
6839      Most lamentable day, most woful day,
6840      That ever, ever, I did yet behold!
6841      O day! O day! O day! O hateful day!
6842      Never was seen so black a day as this:
6843      O woful day, O woful day!
6844      PARIS
6845      Beguiled, divorced, wronged, spited, slain!
6846      Most detestable death, by thee beguil'd,
6847      By cruel cruel thee quite overthrown!
6848      O love! O life! not life, but love in death!
6849      CAPULET
6850      Despised, distressed, hated, martyr'd, kill'd!
6851      Uncomfortable time, why camest thou now
6852      To murder, murder our solemnity?
6853      O child! O child! my soul, and not my child!
6854      Dead art thou! Alack! my child is dead;
6855      And with my child my joys are buried.
6856      FRIAR LAURENCE
6857      Peace, ho, for shame! confusion's cure lives not
6858      In these confusions. Heaven and yourself
6859      Had part in this fair maid; now heaven hath all,
6860      And all the better is it for the maid:
6861      Your part in her you could not keep from death,
6862      But heaven keeps his part in eternal life.
6863      The most you sought was her promotion;
6864      For 'twas your heaven she should be advanced:
6865      And weep ye now, seeing she is advanced
6866      Above the clouds, as high as heaven itself?
6867      O, in this love, you love your child so ill,
6868      That you run mad, seeing that she is well:
6869      She's not well married that lives married long;
6870      But she's best married that dies married young.
6871      Dry up your tears, and stick your rosemary
6872      On this fair corse; and, as the custom is,
6873      In all her best array bear her to church:
6874      For though fond nature bids us an lament,
6875      Yet nature's tears are reason's merriment.
6876      CAPULET
6877      All things that we ordained festival,
6878      Turn from their office to black funeral;
6879      Our instruments to melancholy bells,
6880      Our wedding cheer to a sad burial feast,
6881      Our solemn hymns to sullen dirges change,
6882      Our bridal flowers serve for a buried corse,
6883      And all things change them to the contrary.
6884      FRIAR LAURENCE
6885      Sir, go you in; and, madam, go with him;
6886      And go, Sir Paris; every one prepare
6887      To follow this fair corse unto her grave:
6888      The heavens do lour upon you for some ill;
6889      Move them no more by crossing their high will.
6890      Exeunt CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, PARIS, and FRIAR LAURENCE

6891      First Musician
6892      Faith, we may put up our pipes, and be gone.
6893      Nurse
6894      Honest goodfellows, ah, put up, put up;
6895      For, well you know, this is a pitiful case.
6896      Exit

6897      First Musician
6898      Ay, by my troth, the case may be amended.
6899      Enter PETER

6900      PETER
6901      Musicians, O, musicians, 'Heart's ease, Heart's
6902      ease:' O, an you will have me live, play 'Heart's ease.'
6903      First Musician
6904      Why 'Heart's ease?'
6905      PETER
6906      O, musicians, because my heart itself plays 'My
6907      heart is full of woe:' O, play me some merry dump,
6908      to comfort me.
6909      First Musician
6910      Not a dump we; 'tis no time to play now.
6911      PETER
6912      You will not, then?
6913      First Musician
6914      No.
6915      PETER
6916      I will then give it you soundly.
6917      First Musician
6918      What will you give us?
6919      PETER
6920      No money, on my faith, but the gleek;
6921      I will give you the minstrel.
6922      First Musician
6923      Then I will give you the serving-creature.
6924      PETER
6925      Then will I lay the serving-creature's dagger on
6926      your pate. I will carry no crotchets: I'll re you,
6927      I'll fa you; do you note me?
6928      First Musician
6929      An you re us and fa us, you note us.
6930      Second Musician
6931      Pray you, put up your dagger, and put out your wit.
6932      PETER
6933      Then have at you with my wit! I will dry-beat you
6934      with an iron wit, and put up my iron dagger. Answer
6935      me like men:
6936      'When griping grief the heart doth wound,
6937      And doleful dumps the mind oppress,
6938      Then music with her silver sound'--
6939      why 'silver sound'? why 'music with her silver
6940      sound'? What say you, Simon Catling?
6941      Musician
6942      Marry, sir, because silver hath a sweet sound.
6943      PETER
6944      Pretty! What say you, Hugh Rebeck?
6945      Second Musician
6946      I say 'silver sound,' because musicians sound for silver.
6947      PETER
6948      Pretty too! What say you, James Soundpost?
6949      Third Musician
6950      Faith, I know not what to say.
6951      PETER
6952      O, I cry you mercy; you are the singer: I will say
6953      for you. It is 'music with her silver sound,'
6954      because musicians have no gold for sounding:
6955      'Then music with her silver sound
6956      With speedy help doth lend redress.'
6957      Exit

6958      First Musician
6959      What a pestilent knave is this same!
6960      Second Musician
6961      Hang him, Jack! Come, we'll in here; tarry for the
6962      mourners, and stay dinner.
6963      Exeunt

6964      ACT V
6965      SCENE I. Mantua. A street.
6966      Enter ROMEO
6967      ROMEO
6968      If I may trust the flattering truth of sleep,
6969      My dreams presage some joyful news at hand:
6970      My bosom's lord sits lightly in his throne;
6971      And all this day an unaccustom'd spirit
6972      Lifts me above the ground with cheerful thoughts.
6973      I dreamt my lady came and found me dead--
6974      Strange dream, that gives a dead man leave
6975      to think!--
6976      And breathed such life with kisses in my lips,
6977      That I revived, and was an emperor.
6978      Ah me! how sweet is love itself possess'd,
6979      When but love's shadows are so rich in joy!
6980      Enter BALTHASAR, booted

6981      News from Verona!--How now, Balthasar!
6982      Dost thou not bring me letters from the friar?
6983      How doth my lady? Is my father well?
6984      How fares my Juliet? that I ask again;
6985      For nothing can be ill, if she be well.
6986      BALTHASAR
6987      Then she is well, and nothing can be ill:
6988      Her body sleeps in Capel's monument,
6989      And her immortal part with angels lives.
6990      I saw her laid low in her kindred's vault,
6991      And presently took post to tell it you:
6992      O, pardon me for bringing these ill news,
6993      Since you did leave it for my office, sir.
6994      ROMEO
6995      Is it even so? then I defy you, stars!
6996      Thou know'st my lodging: get me ink and paper,
6997      And hire post-horses; I will hence to-night.
6998      BALTHASAR
6999      I do beseech you, sir, have patience:
7000      Your looks are pale and wild, and do import
7001      Some misadventure.
7002      ROMEO
7003      Tush, thou art deceived:
7004      Leave me, and do the thing I bid thee do.
7005      Hast thou no letters to me from the friar?
7006      BALTHASAR
7007      No, my good lord.
7008      ROMEO
7009      No matter: get thee gone,
7010      And hire those horses; I'll be with thee straight.
7011      Exit BALTHASAR

7012      Well, Juliet, I will lie with thee to-night.
7013      Let's see for means: O mischief, thou art swift
7014      To enter in the thoughts of desperate men!
7015      I do remember an apothecary,--
7016      And hereabouts he dwells,--which late I noted
7017      In tatter'd weeds, with overwhelming brows,
7018      Culling of simples; meagre were his looks,
7019      Sharp misery had worn him to the bones:
7020      And in his needy shop a tortoise hung,
7021      An alligator stuff'd, and other skins
7022      Of ill-shaped fishes; and about his shelves
7023      A beggarly account of empty boxes,
7024      Green earthen pots, bladders and musty seeds,
7025      Remnants of packthread and old cakes of roses,
7026      Were thinly scatter'd, to make up a show.
7027      Noting this penury, to myself I said
7028      'An if a man did need a poison now,
7029      Whose sale is present death in Mantua,
7030      Here lives a caitiff wretch would sell it him.'
7031      O, this same thought did but forerun my need;
7032      And this same needy man must sell it me.
7033      As I remember, this should be the house.
7034      Being holiday, the beggar's shop is shut.
7035      What, ho! apothecary!
7036      Enter Apothecary

7037      Apothecary
7038      Who calls so loud?
7039      ROMEO
7040      Come hither, man. I see that thou art poor:
7041      Hold, there is forty ducats: let me have
7042      A dram of poison, such soon-speeding gear
7043      As will disperse itself through all the veins
7044      That the life-weary taker may fall dead
7045      And that the trunk may be discharged of breath
7046      As violently as hasty powder fired
7047      Doth hurry from the fatal cannon's womb.
7048      Apothecary
7049      Such mortal drugs I have; but Mantua's law
7050      Is death to any he that utters them.
7051      ROMEO
7052      Art thou so bare and full of wretchedness,
7053      And fear'st to die? famine is in thy cheeks,
7054      Need and oppression starveth in thine eyes,
7055      Contempt and beggary hangs upon thy back;
7056      The world is not thy friend nor the world's law;
7057      The world affords no law to make thee rich;
7058      Then be not poor, but break it, and take this.
7059      Apothecary
7060      My poverty, but not my will, consents.
7061      ROMEO
7062      I pay thy poverty, and not thy will.
7063      Apothecary
7064      Put this in any liquid thing you will,
7065      And drink it off; and, if you had the strength
7066      Of twenty men, it would dispatch you straight.
7067      ROMEO
7068      There is thy gold, worse poison to men's souls,
7069      Doing more murders in this loathsome world,
7070      Than these poor compounds that thou mayst not sell.
7071      I sell thee poison; thou hast sold me none.
7072      Farewell: buy food, and get thyself in flesh.
7073      Come, cordial and not poison, go with me
7074      To Juliet's grave; for there must I use thee.
7075      Exeunt

7076      SCENE II. Friar Laurence's cell.
7077      Enter FRIAR JOHN
7078      FRIAR JOHN
7079      Holy Franciscan friar! brother, ho!
7080      Enter FRIAR LAURENCE

7081      FRIAR LAURENCE
7082      This same should be the voice of Friar John.
7083      Welcome from Mantua: what says Romeo?
7084      Or, if his mind be writ, give me his letter.
7085      FRIAR JOHN
7086      Going to find a bare-foot brother out
7087      One of our order, to associate me,
7088      Here in this city visiting the sick,
7089      And finding him, the searchers of the town,
7090      Suspecting that we both were in a house
7091      Where the infectious pestilence did reign,
7092      Seal'd up the doors, and would not let us forth;
7093      So that my speed to Mantua there was stay'd.
7094      FRIAR LAURENCE
7095      Who bare my letter, then, to Romeo?
7096      FRIAR JOHN
7097      I could not send it,--here it is again,--
7098      Nor get a messenger to bring it thee,
7099      So fearful were they of infection.
7100      FRIAR LAURENCE
7101      Unhappy fortune! by my brotherhood,
7102      The letter was not nice but full of charge
7103      Of dear import, and the neglecting it
7104      May do much danger. Friar John, go hence;
7105      Get me an iron crow, and bring it straight
7106      Unto my cell.
7107      FRIAR JOHN
7108      Brother, I'll go and bring it thee.
7109      Exit

7110      FRIAR LAURENCE
7111      Now must I to the monument alone;
7112      Within three hours will fair Juliet wake:
7113      She will beshrew me much that Romeo
7114      Hath had no notice of these accidents;
7115      But I will write again to Mantua,
7116      And keep her at my cell till Romeo come;
7117      Poor living corse, closed in a dead man's tomb!
7118      Exit

7119      SCENE III. A churchyard; in it a tomb belonging to the Capulets.
7120      Enter PARIS, and his Page bearing flowers and a torch
7121      PARIS
7122      Give me thy torch, boy: hence, and stand aloof:
7123      Yet put it out, for I would not be seen.
7124      Under yond yew-trees lay thee all along,
7125      Holding thine ear close to the hollow ground;
7126      So shall no foot upon the churchyard tread,
7127      Being loose, unfirm, with digging up of graves,
7128      But thou shalt hear it: whistle then to me,
7129      As signal that thou hear'st something approach.
7130      Give me those flowers. Do as I bid thee, go.
7131      PAGE
7132      [Aside] I am almost afraid to stand alone
7133      Here in the churchyard; yet I will adventure.
7134      Retires

7135      PARIS
7136      Sweet flower, with flowers thy bridal bed I strew,--
7137      O woe! thy canopy is dust and stones;--
7138      Which with sweet water nightly I will dew,
7139      Or, wanting that, with tears distill'd by moans:
7140      The obsequies that I for thee will keep
7141      Nightly shall be to strew thy grave and weep.
7142      The Page whistles

7143      The boy gives warning something doth approach.
7144      What cursed foot wanders this way to-night,
7145      To cross my obsequies and true love's rite?
7146      What with a torch! muffle me, night, awhile.
7147      Retires

7148      Enter ROMEO and BALTHASAR, with a torch, mattock, & c

7149      ROMEO
7150      Give me that mattock and the wrenching iron.
7151      Hold, take this letter; early in the morning
7152      See thou deliver it to my lord and father.
7153      Give me the light: upon thy life, I charge thee,
7154      Whate'er thou hear'st or seest, stand all aloof,
7155      And do not interrupt me in my course.
7156      Why I descend into this bed of death,
7157      Is partly to behold my lady's face;
7158      But chiefly to take thence from her dead finger
7159      A precious ring, a ring that I must use
7160      In dear employment: therefore hence, be gone:
7161      But if thou, jealous, dost return to pry
7162      In what I further shall intend to do,
7163      By heaven, I will tear thee joint by joint
7164      And strew this hungry churchyard with thy limbs:
7165      The time and my intents are savage-wild,
7166      More fierce and more inexorable far
7167      Than empty tigers or the roaring sea.
7168      BALTHASAR
7169      I will be gone, sir, and not trouble you.
7170      ROMEO
7171      So shalt thou show me friendship. Take thou that:
7172      Live, and be prosperous: and farewell, good fellow.
7173      BALTHASAR
7174      [Aside] For all this same, I'll hide me hereabout:
7175      His looks I fear, and his intents I doubt.
7176      Retires

7177      ROMEO
7178      Thou detestable maw, thou womb of death,
7179      Gorged with the dearest morsel of the earth,
7180      Thus I enforce thy rotten jaws to open,
7181      And, in despite, I'll cram thee with more food!
7182      Opens the tomb

7183      PARIS
7184      This is that banish'd haughty Montague,
7185      That murder'd my love's cousin, with which grief,
7186      It is supposed, the fair creature died;
7187      And here is come to do some villanous shame
7188      To the dead bodies: I will apprehend him.
7189      Comes forward

7190      Stop thy unhallow'd toil, vile Montague!
7191      Can vengeance be pursued further than death?
7192      Condemned villain, I do apprehend thee:
7193      Obey, and go with me; for thou must die.
7194      ROMEO
7195      I must indeed; and therefore came I hither.
7196      Good gentle youth, tempt not a desperate man;
7197      Fly hence, and leave me: think upon these gone;
7198      Let them affright thee. I beseech thee, youth,
7199      Put not another sin upon my head,
7200      By urging me to fury: O, be gone!
7201      By heaven, I love thee better than myself;
7202      For I come hither arm'd against myself:
7203      Stay not, be gone; live, and hereafter say,
7204      A madman's mercy bade thee run away.
7205      PARIS
7206      I do defy thy conjurations,
7207      And apprehend thee for a felon here.
7208      ROMEO
7209      Wilt thou provoke me? then have at thee, boy!
7210      They fight

7211      PAGE
7212      O Lord, they fight! I will go call the watch.
7213      Exit

7214      PARIS
7215      O, I am slain!
7216      Falls

7217      If thou be merciful,
7218      Open the tomb, lay me with Juliet.
7219      Dies

7220      ROMEO
7221      In faith, I will. Let me peruse this face.
7222      Mercutio's kinsman, noble County Paris!
7223      What said my man, when my betossed soul
7224      Did not attend him as we rode? I think
7225      He told me Paris should have married Juliet:
7226      Said he not so? or did I dream it so?
7227      Or am I mad, hearing him talk of Juliet,
7228      To think it was so? O, give me thy hand,
7229      One writ with me in sour misfortune's book!
7230      I'll bury thee in a triumphant grave;
7231      A grave? O no! a lantern, slaughter'd youth,
7232      For here lies Juliet, and her beauty makes
7233      This vault a feasting presence full of light.
7234      Death, lie thou there, by a dead man interr'd.
7235      Laying PARIS in the tomb

7236      How oft when men are at the point of death
7237      Have they been merry! which their keepers call
7238      A lightning before death: O, how may I
7239      Call this a lightning? O my love! my wife!
7240      Death, that hath suck'd the honey of thy breath,
7241      Hath had no power yet upon thy beauty:
7242      Thou art not conquer'd; beauty's ensign yet
7243      Is crimson in thy lips and in thy cheeks,
7244      And death's pale flag is not advanced there.
7245      Tybalt, liest thou there in thy bloody sheet?
7246      O, what more favour can I do to thee,
7247      Than with that hand that cut thy youth in twain
7248      To sunder his that was thine enemy?
7249      Forgive me, cousin! Ah, dear Juliet,
7250      Why art thou yet so fair? shall I believe
7251      That unsubstantial death is amorous,
7252      And that the lean abhorred monster keeps
7253      Thee here in dark to be his paramour?
7254      For fear of that, I still will stay with thee;
7255      And never from this palace of dim night
7256      Depart again: here, here will I remain
7257      With worms that are thy chamber-maids; O, here
7258      Will I set up my everlasting rest,
7259      And shake the yoke of inauspicious stars
7260      From this world-wearied flesh. Eyes, look your last!
7261      Arms, take your last embrace! and, lips, O you
7262      The doors of breath, seal with a righteous kiss
7263      A dateless bargain to engrossing death!
7264      Come, bitter conduct, come, unsavoury guide!
7265      Thou desperate pilot, now at once run on
7266      The dashing rocks thy sea-sick weary bark!
7267      Here's to my love!
7268      Drinks

7269      O true apothecary!
7270      Thy drugs are quick. Thus with a kiss I die.
7271      Dies

7272      Enter, at the other end of the churchyard, FRIAR LAURENCE, with a lantern, crow, and spade

7273      FRIAR LAURENCE
7274      Saint Francis be my speed! how oft to-night
7275      Have my old feet stumbled at graves! Who's there?
7276      BALTHASAR
7277      Here's one, a friend, and one that knows you well.
7278      FRIAR LAURENCE
7279      Bliss be upon you! Tell me, good my friend,
7280      What torch is yond, that vainly lends his light
7281      To grubs and eyeless skulls? as I discern,
7282      It burneth in the Capel's monument.
7283      BALTHASAR
7284      It doth so, holy sir; and there's my master,
7285      One that you love.
7286      FRIAR LAURENCE
7287      Who is it?
7288      BALTHASAR
7289      Romeo.
7290      FRIAR LAURENCE
7291      How long hath he been there?
7292      BALTHASAR
7293      Full half an hour.
7294      FRIAR LAURENCE
7295      Go with me to the vault.
7296      BALTHASAR
7297      I dare not, sir
7298      My master knows not but I am gone hence;
7299      And fearfully did menace me with death,
7300      If I did stay to look on his intents.
7301      FRIAR LAURENCE
7302      Stay, then; I'll go alone. Fear comes upon me:
7303      O, much I fear some ill unlucky thing.
7304      BALTHASAR
7305      As I did sleep under this yew-tree here,
7306      I dreamt my master and another fought,
7307      And that my master slew him.
7308      FRIAR LAURENCE
7309      Romeo!
7310      Advances

7311      Alack, alack, what blood is this, which stains
7312      The stony entrance of this sepulchre?
7313      What mean these masterless and gory swords
7314      To lie discolour'd by this place of peace?
7315      Enters the tomb

7316      Romeo! O, pale! Who else? what, Paris too?
7317      And steep'd in blood? Ah, what an unkind hour
7318      Is guilty of this lamentable chance!
7319      The lady stirs.
7320      JULIET wakes

7321      JULIET
7322      O comfortable friar! where is my lord?
7323      I do remember well where I should be,
7324      And there I am. Where is my Romeo?
7325      Noise within

7326      FRIAR LAURENCE
7327      I hear some noise. Lady, come from that nest
7328      Of death, contagion, and unnatural sleep:
7329      A greater power than we can contradict
7330      Hath thwarted our intents. Come, come away.
7331      Thy husband in thy bosom there lies dead;
7332      And Paris too. Come, I'll dispose of thee
7333      Among a sisterhood of holy nuns:
7334      Stay not to question, for the watch is coming;
7335      Come, go, good Juliet,
7336      Noise again

7337      I dare no longer stay.
7338      JULIET
7339      Go, get thee hence, for I will not away.
7340      Exit FRIAR LAURENCE

7341      What's here? a cup, closed in my true love's hand?
7342      Poison, I see, hath been his timeless end:
7343      O churl! drunk all, and left no friendly drop
7344      To help me after? I will kiss thy lips;
7345      Haply some poison yet doth hang on them,
7346      To make die with a restorative.
7347      Kisses him

7348      Thy lips are warm.
7349      First Watchman
7350      [Within] Lead, boy: which way?
7351      JULIET
7352      Yea, noise? then I'll be brief. O happy dagger!
7353      Snatching ROMEO's dagger

7354      This is thy sheath;
7355      Stabs herself

7356      there rust, and let me die.
7357      Falls on ROMEO's body, and dies

7358      Enter Watch, with the Page of PARIS

7359      PAGE
7360      This is the place; there, where the torch doth burn.
7361      First Watchman
7362      The ground is bloody; search about the churchyard:
7363      Go, some of you, whoe'er you find attach.
7364      Pitiful sight! here lies the county slain,
7365      And Juliet bleeding, warm, and newly dead,
7366      Who here hath lain these two days buried.
7367      Go, tell the prince: run to the Capulets:
7368      Raise up the Montagues: some others search:
7369      We see the ground whereon these woes do lie;
7370      But the true ground of all these piteous woes
7371      We cannot without circumstance descry.
7372      Re-enter some of the Watch, with BALTHASAR

7373      Second Watchman
7374      Here's Romeo's man; we found him in the churchyard.
7375      First Watchman
7376      Hold him in safety, till the prince come hither.
7377      Re-enter others of the Watch, with FRIAR LAURENCE

7378      Third Watchman
7379      Here is a friar, that trembles, sighs and weeps:
7380      We took this mattock and this spade from him,
7381      As he was coming from this churchyard side.
7382      First Watchman
7383      A great suspicion: stay the friar too.
7384      Enter the PRINCE and Attendants

7385      PRINCE
7386      What misadventure is so early up,
7387      That calls our person from our morning's rest?
7388      Enter CAPULET, LADY CAPULET, and others

7389      CAPULET
7390      What should it be, that they so shriek abroad?
7391      LADY CAPULET
7392      The people in the street cry Romeo,
7393      Some Juliet, and some Paris; and all run,
7394      With open outcry toward our monument.
7395      PRINCE
7396      What fear is this which startles in our ears?
7397      First Watchman
7398      Sovereign, here lies the County Paris slain;
7399      And Romeo dead; and Juliet, dead before,
7400      Warm and new kill'd.
7401      PRINCE
7402      Search, seek, and know how this foul murder comes.
7403      First Watchman
7404      Here is a friar, and slaughter'd Romeo's man;
7405      With instruments upon them, fit to open
7406      These dead men's tombs.
7407      CAPULET
7408      O heavens! O wife, look how our daughter bleeds!
7409      This dagger hath mista'en--for, lo, his house
7410      Is empty on the back of Montague,--
7411      And it mis-sheathed in my daughter's bosom!
7412      LADY CAPULET
7413      O me! this sight of death is as a bell,
7414      That warns my old age to a sepulchre.
7415      Enter MONTAGUE and others

7416      PRINCE
7417      Come, Montague; for thou art early up,
7418      To see thy son and heir more early down.
7419      MONTAGUE
7420      Alas, my liege, my wife is dead to-night;
7421      Grief of my son's exile hath stopp'd her breath:
7422      What further woe conspires against mine age?
7423      PRINCE
7424      Look, and thou shalt see.
7425      MONTAGUE
7426      O thou untaught! what manners is in this?
7427      To press before thy father to a grave?
7428      PRINCE
7429      Seal up the mouth of outrage for a while,
7430      Till we can clear these ambiguities,
7431      And know their spring, their head, their
7432      true descent;
7433      And then will I be general of your woes,
7434      And lead you even to death: meantime forbear,
7435      And let mischance be slave to patience.
7436      Bring forth the parties of suspicion.
7437      FRIAR LAURENCE
7438      I am the greatest, able to do least,
7439      Yet most suspected, as the time and place
7440      Doth make against me of this direful murder;
7441      And here I stand, both to impeach and purge
7442      Myself condemned and myself excused.
7443      PRINCE
7444      Then say at once what thou dost know in this.
7445      FRIAR LAURENCE
7446      I will be brief, for my short date of breath
7447      Is not so long as is a tedious tale.
7448      Romeo, there dead, was husband to that Juliet;
7449      And she, there dead, that Romeo's faithful wife:
7450      I married them; and their stol'n marriage-day
7451      Was Tybalt's dooms-day, whose untimely death
7452      Banish'd the new-made bridegroom from the city,
7453      For whom, and not for Tybalt, Juliet pined.
7454      You, to remove that siege of grief from her,
7455      Betroth'd and would have married her perforce
7456      To County Paris: then comes she to me,
7457      And, with wild looks, bid me devise some mean
7458      To rid her from this second marriage,
7459      Or in my cell there would she kill herself.
7460      Then gave I her, so tutor'd by my art,
7461      A sleeping potion; which so took effect
7462      As I intended, for it wrought on her
7463      The form of death: meantime I writ to Romeo,
7464      That he should hither come as this dire night,
7465      To help to take her from her borrow'd grave,
7466      Being the time the potion's force should cease.
7467      But he which bore my letter, Friar John,
7468      Was stay'd by accident, and yesternight
7469      Return'd my letter back. Then all alone
7470      At the prefixed hour of her waking,
7471      Came I to take her from her kindred's vault;
7472      Meaning to keep her closely at my cell,
7473      Till I conveniently could send to Romeo:
7474      But when I came, some minute ere the time
7475      Of her awaking, here untimely lay
7476      The noble Paris and true Romeo dead.
7477      She wakes; and I entreated her come forth,
7478      And bear this work of heaven with patience:
7479      But then a noise did scare me from the tomb;
7480      And she, too desperate, would not go with me,
7481      But, as it seems, did violence on herself.
7482      All this I know; and to the marriage
7483      Her nurse is privy: and, if aught in this
7484      Miscarried by my fault, let my old life
7485      Be sacrificed, some hour before his time,
7486      Unto the rigour of severest law.
7487      PRINCE
7488      We still have known thee for a holy man.
7489      Where's Romeo's man? what can he say in this?
7490      BALTHASAR
7491      I brought my master news of Juliet's death;
7492      And then in post he came from Mantua
7493      To this same place, to this same monument.
7494      This letter he early bid me give his father,
7495      And threatened me with death, going in the vault,
7496      I departed not and left him there.
7497      PRINCE
7498      Give me the letter; I will look on it.
7499      Where is the county's page, that raised the watch?
7500      Sirrah, what made your master in this place?
7501      PAGE
7502      He came with flowers to strew his lady's grave;
7503      And bid me stand aloof, and so I did:
7504      Anon comes one with light to ope the tomb;
7505      And by and by my master drew on him;
7506      And then I ran away to call the watch.
7507      PRINCE
7508      This letter doth make good the friar's words,
7509      Their course of love, the tidings of her death:
7510      And here he writes that he did buy a poison
7511      Of a poor 'pothecary, and therewithal
7512      Came to this vault to die, and lie with Juliet.
7513      Where be these enemies? Capulet! Montague!
7514      See, what a scourge is laid upon your hate,
7515      That heaven finds means to kill your joys with love.
7516      And I for winking at your discords too
7517      Have lost a brace of kinsmen: all are punish'd.
7518      CAPULET
7519      O brother Montague, give me thy hand:
7520      This is my daughter's jointure, for no more
7521      Can I demand.
7522      MONTAGUE
7523      But I can give thee more:
7524      For I will raise her statue in pure gold;
7525      That while Verona by that name is known,
7526      There shall no figure at such rate be set
7527      As that of true and faithful Juliet.
7528      CAPULET
7529      As rich shall Romeo's by his lady's lie;
7530      Poor sacrifices of our enmity!
7531      PRINCE
7532      A glooming peace this morning with it brings;
7533      The sun, for sorrow, will not show his head:
7534      Go hence, to have more talk of these sad things;
7535      Some shall be pardon'd, and some punished:
7536      For never was a story of more woe
7537      Than this of Juliet and her Romeo.
7538      Exeunt
